State v. Heffner


No. 97-379


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


1998 MT 181; 290 Mont. 114; 964 P.2d 736; 1998 Mont. LEXIS 161; 55 Mont. St. Rep. 732


March 19, 1998, Submitted on Briefs  
July 21, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Twentieth Judicial District, In and for the County of Lake, The Honorable C. B. McNeil, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were found guilty by a jury in the District Court of the Twentieth Judicial District, In and for the County of Lakewere (Montana), of making threats and other improper influence in official and political matters. Judgments of conviction were entered. Defendants jointly appealed the jury verdict and judgments of conviction.


OVERVIEW: Defendants, father and son, attempted to pass a county road grader operator, who was backing up the road grader. Defendants were forced off the road, got out of their truck, and approached the road grader. The operator left his road grader. He pushed the son away because he felt threatened. The father then assaulted the operator. Another county employee arrived and joined in the fight with defendants until defendants left the scene in their truck. The operator was severely injured in the fight. On appeal, the supreme court found that: (1) the State was not required to prove that pushing the son was part of the operator's official duties, because it is not an element of the charged offense; (2) the State produced sufficient evidence from which a rational jury could find that defendants' actions were motivated by either distaste for the operator's work, or a desire to prevent him from completing his work, or both; and (3) there existed sufficient evidence from which a rational jury could find that defendants injured the operator because of the discharge of his duties or to prevent him from discharging his duties and that defendants were not justified in the use of force against him.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

July 7, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied August 13, 1998. Released for Publication August 14, 1998.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis & Clark, The Honorable Jeffrey Sherlock, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed in part, affirmed in part, and remanded for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, inmates and state defender project, sought review of an order of the District Court of the First Judicial District (Montana), which dismissed their action against respondents, state board of pardons and associated individuals, in action initiated by appellants seeking access to appellant inmates' parole files.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, inmates and state defender project, initiated action against respondents, state board of pardons and associated individuals, seeking access to appellant inmates' parole files. The trial court dismissed appellants' complaint for failure to state a claim, and appellants sought review. Pursuant to Mont. Const. art. II, § 9, no person should have been deprived of the right to examine documents or to observe the deliberations of all public bodies or agencies of state government, except in cases in which the demand of individual privacy clearly exceeded the merits of public disclosure. The court held that appellant inmates' pardon files were documents of public bodies to which the right to know applied. The court held that the trial court erred in dismissing appellants' claim that respondents violated the constitutional right to know. The court held that there was nothing in the due process clause which ensured appellant inmates' right to inspect such files and that the trial court properly dismissed that portion of appellants' claim. The court reversed in part and affirmed in part an order of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed in part and affirmed in part and order of the trial court, which dismissed appellants', inmates and state defender project, claim against respondents, state board of pardons and associated individuals. The court held that appellant inmates' pardon files were documents of public bodies to which the constitutional right to know applied. The court held that respondents had not violated the due process clause.

State v. Pipkin


No. 97-581


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


1998 MT 143; 289 Mont. 240; 961 P.2d 733; 1998 Mont. LEXIS 129; 55 Mont. St. Rep. 567


March 26, 1998, Submitted On Briefs  
June 9, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Twentieth Judicial District, In and for the County of Sanders, The Honorable C. B. McNeil, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed the District Court's suppression order and remanded for further proceedings consistent with opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of the decision from the District Court of the Twentieth Judicial District, Sanders County (Montana), which granted defendant's motion to suppress all evidence seized pursuant to a search warrant on the ground that the warrant was not directed to a specific peace officer as required by Mont. Code Ann. § 46-5-223 and that it had not been served by a peace officer named in the warrant as required by § 46-5-226.


OVERVIEW: An officer's name was not inserted following the salutary language of a warrant although the officer was identified in the warrant as having made application for the warrant. Defendant was charged with manufacture of dangerous drugs, criminal possession of dangerous drugs, criminal possession with intent to sell, use or possession of property subject to criminal forfeiture, and criminal possession of drug paraphernalia. Defendant filed a motion to suppress all evidence seized pursuant to the state's search warrant on the ground that the warrant was not directed to a specific peace officer and that it had not been served by a peace officer named in the warrant. The district court granted the motion. On appeal, the court reversed the district court's suppression order. The court held that: (1) defendant's substantial rights were not affected by the fact that the salutation of the warrant was not directed to the officer; and (2) to suppress all evidence obtained because the officer's name was not in the heading, even though he applied for the warrant, served the warrant, and executed the return of service for the warrant, was an irrational result.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the district court's suppression order holding that defendant's substantial rights were not affected by technical deficiencies in the search warrant. The court remanded the case to the district court for further proceedings consistent with its opinion.

Great Falls Tribune Co. v. Day


No. 98-216


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


1998 MT 133; 289 Mont. 155; 959 P.2d 508; 1998 Mont. LEXIS 114; 55 Mont. St. Rep. 524; 26 Media L. Rep. 2377


May 26, 1998, Heard; May 26, 1998, Submitted  
May 29, 1998, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed in part and affirmed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The First Judicial District Court in Lewis and Clark County (Montana), entered an order providing that the public did not have a right to observe the negotiations of a committee of respondent Department of Corrections, but that the public had a right to all information considered by the committee after it made its decisions. Petitioner newspaper and the department appealed.


OVERVIEW: The department created a committee to review proposals for the construction of a new prison. The department refused to allow public access to the proposals or committee meetings. The newspaper filed a petition requesting that the committee be restrained from holding private meetings and compelled to disclose all documents associated with its meetings. The trial court allowed private negotiations but required that the committee's deliberations be made public prior to the award of a contract. The court held that Mont. Const. art. II, § 9 applied to the committee because it was an agency of the state government and a public body. The proposals submitted by private companies to the committee were documents of a public body that had to be disclosed under art. II, § 9. The court found that the private companies did not have an actual expectation that all information in the proposals would be kept confidential; only trade secrets and matters related to security would not ultimately be made public. As the committee meetings were only closed for economic advantage, there was no legitimate reason to preclude public disclosure.


OUTCOME: The portion of the order allowing the newspaper and the public access to information considered by the department's committee after its decisions were made was affirmed; the portion of the order denying access to the committee's negotiations was reversed.

Connery v. Liberty Northwest Ins. Corp.


No. 97-630


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


1998 MT 125; 289 Mont. 94; 960 P.2d 288; 1998 Mont. LEXIS 108; 55 Mont. St. Rep. 496


March 26, 1998, Submitted On Briefs  
May 26, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: The Workers' Compensation Court, Honorable Mike McCarter, Judge Presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a workers' compensation proceeding brought by respondent employee, appellant insurer sought review from a decision of the Workers' Compensation Court (Montana) declaring that the benefit reduction provision of Mont. Code Ann. § 39-71-416(1) was unconstitutional under Mont. Const. art. II, § 16.


OVERVIEW: A coworker collided with the employee injuring her. In addition to filing for workers' compensation benefits, the employee filed a third-party claim with the coworkers homeowner's insurance carrier that settled the claim. The insurer claimed that it was entitled to a the 30 percent offset of the third-party claim pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 39-71-416(1) and refused to pay the benefits that the employee's impairment rating required. The insurer contended that the 30 percent reduction did not affect the employee's third-party recovery and that the statute merely effected the employee's entitlement to benefits. The court affirmed the judgment finding Mont. Code Ann. § 39-71-416(1) unconstitutional. The court held § 39-71-416(1) facially ignored the employee's right to full legal redress under Mont. Const. art. II, § 16 because under the statute, if the employee got anything, however short of full legal redress from her third-party claim, the insurer was entitled to reduce by 30 percent the benefits otherwise payable to the employee.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the workers' compensation court finding the statutory provision reducing benefits by a percent of the employee's third-party claim unconstitutional in the employee's workers' compensation proceeding against the insurer.

Smith v. Driver's Improvement Bureau


No. 97-189


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


1998 MT 94; 288 Mont. 383; 958 P.2d 677; 1998 Mont. LEXIS 67; 55 Mont. St. Rep. 375


February 18, 1998, Submitted On Briefs  
April 30, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighteenth Judicial, In and for the County of Gallatin, Honorable Michael A. Salvagni, Judge Presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant driver challenged an order of the Eighteenth Judicial District Court, Gallatin County (Montana), which upheld the suspension of the driver's license by respondent Driver's Improvement Bureau (Bureau).


OVERVIEW: The driver was stopped on suspicion of driving under the influence of alcohol. When he refused to submit to a preliminary alcohol screening test (PAST), his driver's license was suspended. The driver sought review of his license suspension on grounds that the PAST statute, Mont. Code Ann. § 61-8-409, and the implied consent statute, Mont. Code Ann. § 61-8-403 (1995), were vague because they contained irreconcilable hearing provisions. The trial court held that there was no conflict, and the court affirmed. The court held that inasmuch as the issues which could be considered at a hearing under Mont. Code Ann. § 61-8-403(4) (1995) differed from the provisions of Mont. Code Ann. § 61-8-409(6) (1995), under the rule that the specific prevailed over the general, Mont. Code Ann. § 61-8-409(6) (1995), the specific statute on PASTs, specified the issues which could be discussed at a hearing on suspension of a driver's license when the suspension was based upon refusal to submit to a PAST prior to arrest. Thus the driver did not establish a conflict among Montana's implied consent statutes which rendered them unconstitutionally vague either on their face or as applied to him.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment upholding the Commission's suspension of the driver's license.

Montana Dep't of Revenue v. Archer


No. 97-700


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


1998 MT 101N; 1998 Mont. LEXIS 73


March 12, 1998, Submitted On Briefs  
April 30, 1998, Decided


NOTICE:  [**1] PURSUANT TO THE APPLICABLE MONTANA CODE SECTION, THIS OPINION IS NOT DESIGNATED FOR PUBLICATION.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reported in table case format at: 1998 Mont. LEXIS 406.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, The Honorable Jeffrey M. Sherlock, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Order and judgment of the District Court affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent taxpayer challenged the judgment of the District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark (Montana), which denied the taxpayer's motions to dismiss the action and to remove the matter to federal court, and ordered the taxpayer to file state income tax returns in favor of petitioner state revenue department.


OVERVIEW: The state revenue department petitioned the district court for an order to require the taxpayer to file state income tax returns for two tax years, or to show cause why he should not file returns. The taxpayer brought a motion to dismiss the action and to remove the matter to federal court. After a hearing, the district court denied the motions and ordered the taxpayer to file the returns. The taxpayer appealed. The court affirmed because the taxpayer failed to provide any authority for removal to federal court. In addition, the tax returns sought were required by Montana law, and the taxpayer failed to assert with any support that he was either denied an adequate remedy in the state court, or that his contentions were more appropriately a matter for federal court. Accordingly, there was no basis to remove the matter to federal court, and the district court did not err when it denied the removal motion. The court also held that the motion to dismiss was properly denied because the mere fact that the taxpayer received money as income triggered the obligation to file a state income tax return.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of the taxpayer's motions to dismiss the action and to remove the matter to federal court in a petition brought by the state revenue department for an order requiring the taxpayer to file state income tax returns.

Ranta v. State


No. 97-216


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


1998 MT 95; 288 Mont. 391; 958 P.2d 670; 1998 Mont. LEXIS 97; 55 Mont. St. Rep. 378


October 1, 1997, Heard; October 30, 1997, Submitted  
April 30, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: Original Proceeding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate brought an action for post-conviction relief, challenging the decisions and judgments of the Montana Supreme Court affirming and increasing his sentences. The inmate contended that this was unconstitutional on the grounds that respondent state failed to appoint counsel to represent him in his sentence review. The inmate requested the reinstatement of his original sentences.


OVERVIEW: The inmate pled guilty to and was convicted in three separate criminal prosecutions of criminal mischief, burglary and misdemeanor theft, and theft. The inmate applied for sentence review and subsequently appeared at a hearing before the review division. The inmate, who had previously been determined indigent, did not have the assistance of counsel. Subsequently the inmate's sentences were increased. The court reasoned that the Sixth Amendment to the United States Constitution and Mont. Const. art. II, § 24 guaranteed a criminal defendant the right to counsel in criminal proceedings. Further, the court held that this right to counsel arose at every critical stage of the proceedings against him. The court concluded that sentence review was a critical stage of the proceedings against a defendant. The court reasoned that like the original sentencing itself, the potential substantial prejudice at the sentence review was not only present, but was enormous. Therefore, the court held that the inmate was denied his constitutional right to counsel during the sentence review procedure, and that as a result he suffered substantial prejudice.


OUTCOME: The court granted the inmate's request and reinstated his original sentences. However, the court did so in the context of an extraordinary writ as opposed to granting post-conviction relief.

 

51 of 930 DOCUMENTS


State v. Lancione


No. 97-042


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


1998 MT 84; 288 Mont. 228; 956 P.2d 1358; 1998 Mont. LEXIS 59; 55 Mont. St. Rep. 344


January 30, 1998, Submitted on Briefs  
April 14, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Gallatin, The Honorable Thomas A. Olson, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction and sentence in the District Court of the Eighteenth Judicial District (Montana), for criminal endangerment in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-207.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, real estate agent argued with the victim over the sharing of a commission in his office. Defendant then followed the victim out to the hall and down five flights of stairs. Later, the victim was found lying on the floor in a pool of blood. Defendant was convicted of criminal endangerment, sentenced to six years in prison, and ordered to pay restitution. On appeal, the court affirmed the conviction and sentence. The court found that a witness's testimony that defendant walked real slow before the incident was irrelevant, but that its admission was not prejudicial error. The court also found that (1) § 45-5-207 did not interfere with any constitutional rights of a person or any conduct that was constitutionally protected; (2) § 45-5-207 clearly applied to defendant's conduct; and (3) that defendant did not have standing to challenge § 45-5-207. The court further determined that the evidence was such that a jury could infer that defendant followed the victim down the stairs and that he caused the victim to fall.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's criminal endangerment conviction and sentence.

State v. Lane


No. 96-514


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


1998 MT 76; 288 Mont. 286; 957 P.2d 9; 1998 Mont. LEXIS 54; 55 Mont. St. Rep. 311


October 30, 1997, Submitted on Briefs  
April 7, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Yellowstone, The Honorable Diane G. Barz, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the entry of a nunc pro tunc order entered by the District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, in and for the County of Yellowstone (Montana), which corrected the written judgment and commitment to conform to the sentence as orally pronounced by the district court judge after defendant pled guilty to the offense of sexual intercourse without consent.


OVERVIEW: The district court judge ordered defendant to serve 10 years in state prison and denied him parole until phase I and II of a sexual offender program was completed. The transcript and minute entry stated the same. However, the written judgment filed recommended defendant was not eligible for parole until both phases of the sexual offender program was completed. One year later, the nunc pro tunc order was entered that conformed to the actual judgment intended. Defendant was advised he was ineligible for his parole hearing that was scheduled and was unaware until then of the nun pro tunc order. Defendant filed a late appeal, which was allowed although untimely. Defendant contended that the nunc pro tunc order erroneously modified his sentence and violated his rights. The court, in affirming defendant's sentence, held that the oral pronouncement of sentence was the legally effective sentence and that his Sixth Amendment right was not violated because he was present at sentencing and had the opportunity to respond before sentence was imposed. The court held that the oral pronouncement was the legally effective sentence and that the order did not violate due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the entry of a nunc pro tunc order which corrected the written judgment and commitment to conform to the sentence orally pronounced after defendant pled guilty to sexual intercourse without consent.

 

53 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Town of Whitehall v. Preece


No. 96-648


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


1998 MT 53; 288 Mont. 55; 956 P.2d 743; 1998 Mont. LEXIS 33; 55 Mont. St. Rep. 219


January 13, 1998, Heard; January 15, 1998, Submitted  
March 10, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Fifth Judicial District, In and for the County of Jefferson, Honorable Frank M. Davis, Judge Presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers challenged the decision of the District Court of the Fifth Judicial District, in and for the County of Jefferson (Montana), which declared their petition for a referendum to repeal a city ordinance requiring metering of water to be invalid upon a finding that respondent town's ordinance regarding the metering of water was administrative rather than legislative and was therefore exempt from the referendum proceedings.


OVERVIEW: The town enacted an ordinance requiring the installation of water meters at the direction of the Montana Public Service Commission, in conjunction with the application for grants and loans to improve the water system. The taxpayers filed a petition for a referendum to repeal the ordinance. The town sought a declaratory judgment that the petition was invalid. The trial court ruled in the town's favor and the court affirmed. The court held that, under the constitution, the taxpayers retained the powers of initiative and referendum as to legislative acts only, but that the ordinance was an administrative act. The court, applying newly adopted guidelines for determining whether an action was administrative or legislative, found that the ordinance was administrative because the water meter decision was a small segment of an overall policy, the best method of water use billing entailed decision-making functions involving specialized knowledge and experience, and the existence of previous public hearings and coterminous objections confirmed that the issue had not been addressed for the first time when the ordinance was adopted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision declaring invalid the taxpayer's referendum to overturn the town's meter reading ordinance.

 

54 of 930 DOCUMENTS


State v. Brummer


No. 97-097


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


1998 MT 11; 287 Mont. 168; 953 P.2d 250; 1998 Mont. LEXIS 7; 55 Mont. St. Rep. 30


July 23, 1997, Submitted on Briefs  
January 22, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, In and for the County of Cascade, The Honorable Robert S. Keller, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State challenged the decision of the District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, in and for the County of Cascade (Montana), which ordered a new trial sua sponte after a jury had found defendant guilty of kidnapping.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with sexual intercourse without consent, aggravated kidnapping, and assault. The jury found him guilty of kidnapping, a lesser included offense of aggravated kidnapping. After the jury's verdict the district court, on its own initiative, notified the parties of its intent to order a new trial. Although neither party had made a motion for a new trial, the district court ordered a new trial sua sponte. The State contended that the district court erred in granting a new trial, sua sponte, because under the plain language of Mont. Code Ann. § 46-16-702, the district court had no authority to do so. The court rejected this contention, holding that under § 46-16-702, the district court had authority to order a new trial sua sponte. The court reasoned that the statute did not specifically withhold the power of the trial court to grant a new trial sua sponte. Further, the court held that its attention had not been called to, nor had its research revealed, any authority, constitutional, statutory, or judicial decision, which usurped the inherent power of the trial court to set aside a verdict and order a new trial, sua sponte, in the interest of justice.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order for a new trial of defendant after the jury had found defendant guilty of kidnapping.

 

55 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Langford v. State


No. 97-586


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


287 Mont. 107; 951 P.2d 1357; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 283; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 1522


December 2, 1997, Submitted  
December 30, 1997, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Langford's petition for writ of injunction is DENIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an original proceeding, defendant petitioned for a writ of injunction pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 3-2-205(2) and Mont. R. App. P. 17(a) to enjoin the state from executing him under Mont. Code Ann. § 46-19-103 (1997). Defendant claimed that hanging was cruel and unusual punishment and that the amendment to § 46-19-103, which provided that all death sentences were imposed by lethal injection, constituted an ex post facto law.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, sentenced to death, elected under § 46-19-103 to die by hanging. During his post-conviction actions, § 46-19-103 was amended to eliminate hanging as a means of execution. Defendant brought an original proceeding in the court for a writ of injunction to enjoin the state from executing him. He argued that the amended statute truncated his ability to appeal his death sentence and nullified his claim that hanging was cruel and unusual punishment. He also claimed that § 46-19-103 was an ex post facto law. The court denied his petition for a writ, holding that an ex post facto law deprived a defendant of a defense. Section 46-19-103 came into play after sentence was imposed and addressed only the procedure by which the sentence was carried out. Changes in the procedures did not affect substantial rights in a way that implicated the prohibition against ex post facto laws. The court also ruled that defendant's purely conjectural assertions that he had a due process right to avoid execution under the earlier version of § 46-19-103 did not create a current substantive right which was infringed by an amendment altering only the procedure by which his sentence was carried out.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's petition for a writ of injunction to enjoin the state from carrying out his death sentence by lethal injection.

 

56 of 930 DOCUMENTS


In re S.L.M.


No. 96-576, No. 96-337, No. 96-220, No. 96-546, No. 96-547


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


287 Mont. 23; 951 P.2d 1365; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 278; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 1480


November 6, 1997, Argued; November 14, 1997, Submitted  
December 30, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeals From: District Court of the Eighteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Gallatin, The Honorable Thomas A. Olson, Judge presiding.

Appeal From: District Court of the Twelfth Judicial District, In and for the County of Hill, The Honorable John Warner, Judge presiding.

Appeals From: District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Yellowstone, The Honorable Diane G. Barz, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: District Court's decision that EJPA is unconstitutional, affirmed. The Hill County Matter of J.L.C., the Gallatin County Matter of S.L.M. and the Gallatin County Matter of S.T. remanded to those District Courts for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated appeal from the Eighteenth, Twelfth, and Thirteenth Judicial District Courts (Montana), the court considered the challenges of appellant juveniles to the constitutionality of the Extended Jurisdiction Prosecution Act, Mont. Code Ann. §§ 41-5-1601 to -1607, under the Equal Protection, Due Process and Double Jeopardy Clauses of the federal and state constitutions and under Mont. Const. art. II, § 15.


OVERVIEW: In a consolidated appeal by appellant juvenile offenders, appellants contended that the Extended Jurisdiction Prosecution Act (EJPA), which was applied in each of appellant's cases, violated the Equal Protection, Double Jeopardy, and Due Process clauses of the Montana and United States Constitutions by subjecting them to a longer period of incarceration than that permitted for an adult offender. The court first determined that appellants had standing to challenge the EJPA because of the threat of injury by its imposition. The court then held that the EJPA, on its face, violated appellants' equal protection rights by treating them more harshly than their adult counterparts. The EJPA also violated the Mont. Const. art. II, § 15 by reducing, rather than enhancing, appellants' rights as compared to an adult's rights because if a juvenile violated the terms of the juvenile disposition, it was possible the juvenile would serve a longer term of detention or imprisonment than an adult who had committed the same offense.


OUTCOME: Having found that the Extended Juvenile Prosecution Act (EJPA) violated the equal protection rights of appellant juvenile offenders because it treated them more harshly than their adult counterparts, the court affirmed the district court decisions that also found the EJPA unconstitutional and remanded the remaining cases to their respective district courts for proceedings consistent with the opinion.

Emery v. Department of Pub. Health & Human Servs., Child Support Enforcement Div.


No. 97-242


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


286 Mont. 376; 950 P.2d 764; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 288; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 1454


September 11, 1997, Submitted on Briefs  
December 23, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Fourth Judicial District, In and for the County of Missoula, The Honorable John W. Larson, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with instructions that District Court reinstate its original opinion and order on judicial review.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, state and child support enforcement agency (CSED), sought review of an order of the District Court, County of Missoula (Montana) that dismissed the CSED's income withholding action against respondent father as the result of his failure to comply with his child support obligations, after granting the father's petition to amend its prior opinion and order on judicial review.


OVERVIEW: A father failed to pay child support obligations ordered pursuant to a divorce decree. The mother applied for public assistance and assigned her right to enforce the support order to CSED. The CSED initiated, but did not conclude, collection efforts. The mother thereafter agreed to waive the support arrearages. The CSED then initiated a wage withholding action and an administrative law judge (ALJ) held that the suit could continue because the CSED was not party to the waiver. On judicial review, the trial court reversed the ALJ's decision and dismissed the withholding action. The CSED appealed. The court reversed the trial court judgment. The court held that the CSED's attempts to collect were not one continuous action, therefore the delay between first collection action and the second withholding action was irrelevant. The court found that the father was not denied due process when he was denied a hearing in the first collection action because that action was discontinued. The court concluded that the CSED's second action did not violate Mont. Const. at. II, § 16 because both actions were resolved promptly, one by discontinuance and the other via hearing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court judgment that dismissed the CSED's income withholding action against the father as the result of his failure to comply with his child support obligations. The court remanded the matter with instructions that the trial court reinstate its original ruling that the waiver of child support arrearages was void, and remanded the case to the CSED for a determination of the past-due amount owed by the father.

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the decision of the District Court of the Eighteenth Judicial District (Montana), which convicted him of robbery and criminal possession of dangerous drugs.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of robbery and criminal possession of dangerous drugs and the court affirmed his convictions. The court found that the lower court did not err in concluding that the arresting officer had a particularized suspicion to stop defendant. The court determined that the officer was sufficiently qualified even though he had been on the force for only a short time. The court found that defendant's actions and appearance, along with the totality of the circumstances, justified the investigatory stop. The court concluded that the lower court correctly denied defendant's motion to suppress the physical evidence seized during the stop and frisk because the pat search was proper and the seizure of the ski goggles fell within the plain feel doctrine. The court found that as the officer immediately knew that the item he felt was ski goggles, they were properly seized. The court held that defendant's confession was voluntary and that the lower court did not err in denying defendant's motion to suppress.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for robbery and criminal possession of dangerous drugs.

The Duck Inn v. Montana State University-Northern


No. 97-236


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


285 Mont. 519; 949 P.2d 1179; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 264; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 1295


October 9, 1997, Submitted on Briefs  
December 9, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Twelfth Judicial District, In and for the County of Hill, The Honorable John Warner, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff hotel appealed from a judgment of the District Court, Hill County (Montana), that granted summary judgment for a public university in the hotel's action seeking declaratory judgment and an injunction to enjoin the university's practice of renting its facilities to private persons and organizations on the grounds that the practice violated either Mont. Code Ann. § 20-25-302 (1993) or the Montana Constitution.


OVERVIEW: A private hotel filed a complaint for declaratory judgment and an injunction against a public university, alleging that the university's leasing of its facilities to private persons and organizations for various events placed the university in direct competition with the hotel's business and violated Mont. Code Ann. § 20-25-302 (1993) or the Montana Constitution. The trial court denied the hotel's request for an injunction and granted summary judgment for the hotel. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court judgment. The court held that § 20-25-302 authorized the university's board of regents to rent out the facilities and the regents were authorized to supervise the university system under Mont. Const. art. X, § 9. The court held that Mont. Code Ann. § 20-25-302 (1993) was not an unconstitutional delegation of legislative authority to the regents because their discretion was sufficiently limited by the requirements of § 20-25-302 that the leasing be consistent with the full use of the facilities for academic purposes and produce revenues for capital costs or debt service. The court also found that no tax monies were being spent to satisfy private debts or purposes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment that granted summary judgment for the public university in the hotel's action seeking a declaratory judgment and injunction against the university's practice of renting its facilities for private functions.

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, The Honorable Jeffrey M. Sherlock, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: District Court did not err in granting Department's motion to dismiss on basis that Taxpayers' claims were either barred by applicable statutes of limitations, or otherwise deficient as a matter of law.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The First Judicial District Court, Lewis and Clark County (Montana), granted defendant tax collectors' motion to dismiss the first-amended complaint for declaratory relief filed by plaintiff taxpayers seeking class certification, reduction of certain real property appraisals, a partial refund of certain real property taxes, and related relief. The taxpayers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The Montana legislature modified its real property appraisal system and enacted a new one. Thereafter, the taxpayers paid their real property taxes for four tax years under the new system. The Montana Supreme Court, in the meantime, issued a decision that declared the new tax appraisal system unconstitutional. The legislature attempted to fix the defects, but the state supreme court held the new legislation did not rid the new tax appraisal system of the constitutional defects previously identified. The taxpayers filed their declaratory judgment action approximately three years after the last court decision on the issue and claimed that, because they paid real property taxes based on an unconstitutional method of assessment, they were entitled to relief. After the trial court granted the tax collectors' motion to dismiss, the state supreme court found the taxpayers' were not entitled to relief. It so concluded, because it found either their claims were barred by the 90-day limitations period for bringing a declaratory judgment action found in Mont. Code Ann. § 15-1-406(2) or were insufficient as a matter of law, such as the tax payments not having been paid under protest.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Sullivan


No. 96-584


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


285 Mont. 235; 948 P.2d 215; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 233; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 1128


June 12, 1997, Submitted on Briefs  
October 29, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, In and for the County of Cascade, The Honorable Jeffrey Sherlock, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, In and for the County of Cascade (Montana) of three counts of felony theft. Defendant filed a petition for postconviction relief, asserting that his constitutional rights had been violated and that newly discovered evidence entitled him to a new trial or an evidentiary hearing. The trial court dismissed the petition, and defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended on appeal that the trial court erred in concluding that his rights under Brady v. Maryland were not violated, that the trial court abused its discretion in determining that he was not entitled to a new trial based on newly discovered evidence, and that he was entitled to a new trial as a matter of due process, based on newly discovered evidence. The court held that the trial court correctly concluded that defendant's due process Brady rights were not violated. Defendant's unsupported allegation, made on information and belief, did not establish good cause for permission to conduct discovery as required by Mont. Code Ann. § 46-21-201(3) (1993), nor was it sufficient to entitle him to an evidentiary hearing under § 46-21-201(1). The court further held that the newly discovered evidence was not related to the theft charge at issue, was not probative of defendant's innocence, would not probably have produced a different result upon retrial, and that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying defendant a new trial based on newly discovered evidence. The court also held that defendant was not entitled to a new trial as a matter of due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of defendant's petition for postconviction relief.

Sharp v. Department of Revenue


No. 96-600


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


284 Mont. 424; 945 P.2d 38; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 195; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 949


June 5, 1997, Submitted on Briefs  
September 15, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released For Publication October 22, 1997.
Rehearing Denied October 22, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, the Honorable Jeffrey M. Sherlock, Judge Presiding.



DISPOSITION: Decision of District Court affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant lottery winners sought review of the judgment of the First Judicial District Court, Lewis and Clark County (Montana), which held that their annual lottery payments were subject to Montana state income tax despite their change of residence from Montana to Washington.


OVERVIEW: The lottery winners were Montana residents when they won a multi-state lottery game in 1991. In 1992 they moved to the state of Washington and did not report their 1992 lottery payment as Montana income because they received it after they moved to Washington. The lower court held that the lottery proceeds were income earned in Montana within the meaning of Mont. Code Ann. § 15-30-105 and were subject to Montana's state income tax. The lottery winners contended that a 1991 version of the statutes in question applied and that the winnings were exempt from state income tax. The court concluded that the statutes in effect at the time of each annual lottery payment controlled as to taxation of that payment. The court held that the lottery winners' 1992 and subsequent lottery payments were not excluded from income under Mont. Code Ann. §§ 15-30-105 and 15-30-131, and the lower court did not err when it held that the winnings were subject to an income tax by the state of Montana.


OUTCOME: The court held that the lower court did not err when it ruled that the lottery winners' annual lottery payment was income earned in Montana and subject to an income tax by the State of Montana.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners sought review of an order from the District Court of the Fourth Judicial District, In and for the County of Missoula (Montana), which awarded attorney fees and costs to respondent property owners in a suit quieting title to land.


OVERVIEW: After acquiring their property, respondents constructed a road for ingress and egress, but appellants began to use the road, so respondents filed a suit to quiet title. Ingraham v. State


No. 97-241


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


284 Mont. 481; 945 P.2d 19; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 159; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 782



July 24, 1997, Dated



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Ingraham's application for writ of habeas corpus DENIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoner filed an application for a writ of habeas corpus after the Twentieth Judicial District Court, Lake County (Montana), denied his motion to continue bond pending appeal.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner was convicted of negligent homicide, criminal endangerment, and criminal trespass to property as a result of a fatal traffic accident. He had consumed alcohol prior to the incident. The district court denied the prisoner's motion to continue bond pending appeal. The prisoner then filed an application for a writ of habeas corpus. The court denied his petition. Mont. Code Ann. § 46-9-107 provided that a convicted criminal defendant was not entitled to bail pending appeal unless the district court found that he or she was not likely to either flee or pose a danger. The court found that none of the district court's findings in that regard were unsupported by the record. While the district court could have given additional weight to the findings of the clinicians that had recently treated the prisoner for alcohol dependency and bipolar affective disorder, its failure to do so was not an abuse of its discretion. Moreover, prior to trial, the prisoner had repeatedly violated the bond condition that he not consume alcohol. The court also ruled that the prisoner was not entitled to bond under § 46-9-107 simply because his convictions could be reversed on appeal.


OUTCOME: The court denied the prisoner's application for a writ of habeas corpus.

State v. Nye


No. 96-288


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


283 Mont. 505; 943 P.2d 96; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 154; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 766


January 9, 1997, Submitted on Briefs  
July 23, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Sixth Judicial District, In and for the County of Park, The Honorable William Nels Swandal, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction in the District Court of the Sixth Judicial District, County of Park (Montana) for malicious intimidation or harassment relating to civil or human rights in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-221(1)(c).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with violating the "hate crimes" statute, § 45-5-221(1)(c), after he distributed and affixed bumper stickers stating his objection to a particular church. Defendant pleaded guilty and reserved his right to appeal the denial of his motion to dismiss. Defendant alleged that § 45-5-221(1)(c), was void for vagueness, over broad as applied to him, and in violation of his constitutional rights. On appeal, the court held that Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-221 did not violate defendant's right to freedom of speech under the First Amendment or Mont. Const. art. II, § 7 because defendant coupled his message on the bumper stickers with defacement of property. Further, free speech did not give defendant the right to embarrass, annoy or harass, causing substantial emotional distress. Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-221 was not unconstitutionally vague or over broad on its face because it was reasonably clear that it applied to defendant's conduct and it did not infringe on another's constitutionally protected rights in a real or substantial way. Defendant was precluded from raising on appeal the issue of the penalty in § 45-5-221 because he did not raise it in the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for malicious intimidation or harassment relating to civil or human rights.

 

67 of 930 DOCUMENTS


City of Hamilton v. Mavros


No. 96-623


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


284 Mont. 46; 943 P.2d 963; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 151; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 741


May 29, 1997, Submitted on Briefs  
July 17, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Twenty-First Judicial District, In and for the County of Ravalli; The Honorable Jeffrey H. Langton, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the District court of the Twenty-First Judicial District, in and for the County of Ravalli (Montana), insofar as it convicted him of two counts of assault.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pulled his car to an abrupt halt in front of a car driven by his estranged common-law wife, jumped out of his car, and attempted to open the driver's door of her car while he insulted her and argued with her. Defendant also prevented a child passenger from exiting the car and caused her to cry. After an initial trial in the city court for reckless driving and two counts of assault, defendant received a trial de novo in the district court. The child was unavailable to testify in the district court, and a municipal court judge was allowed to testify to her recollection of the testimony the child gave at the earlier trial. Defendant's common-law wife testified that at no time was she afraid of defendant during the entire incident. The court reversed defendant's conviction on two counts of assault. The testimony of the municipal court judge was inadmissible hearsay because a transcript or some other recording device, which accurately presented a verbatim record of the earlier testimony, did not offer it. There was insufficient evidence to prove an essential element of assault: that the common-law wife had a reasonable apprehension of bodily injury.


OUTCOME: The judgment convicting defendant of two counts of assault was reversed and the case was remanded for proceedings consistent with the court's opinion.

Grooms v. Ponderosa Inn


No. 96-594


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


283 Mont. 459; 942 P.2d 699; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 150; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 725


March 13, 1997, Submitted on Briefs  
July 15, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: Worker's Compensation Court, State of Montana. The Honorable Mike McCarter, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimant challenged an order of the Worker's Compensation Court, State of Montana, which rejected her constitutional challenges to certain Occupational Disease Act statutes and which dismissed her petition for workers' compensation benefits in the suit she filed against respondents, her employer, the State Compensation Mutual Insurance Fund, and the Department of Labor and Industry (Montana).


OVERVIEW: The State Fund denied liability for the claimant's skin allergy under the Workers' Compensation Act (WCA), and it requested that the Department of Labor and Industry schedule the claimant's examination under the Occupational Disease Act of Montana. The workers' compensation court dismissed the claimant's petition for declaratory judgment, and on appeal, the court affirmed. The workers' compensation court did not deny the claimant her right to due process because she failed to take advantage of the due process afforded for resolution of her dispute with the State Fund pursuant to the WCA. The claimant's right to choose her treating physician was not violated by the statute permitting the Department to select an examining physician, nor was her right to equal protection violated by the statute requiring her to pay for a second examination. The claimant was not deprived of her right to legal redress on the basis of her asserted inability to pay for the second examination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the workers' compensation court's order that dismissed the claimant's petition for declaratory judgment.

Hans v. State


No. 93-176


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


283 Mont. 379; 942 P.2d 674; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 141; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 654


March 13, 1997, Submitted on Briefs  
July 2, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by, Motion to strike granted by, Motion to strike denied by In re Petition of Hans, 1998 MT 7, 288 Mont. 168, 958 P.2d 1175, 1998 Mont. LEXIS 5 (1998)
Post-conviction relief denied at Hans v. State, 1998 MT 255, 291 Mont. 227, 967 P.2d 396, 1998 Mont. LEXIS 235 (1998)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Hans v. Mahoney, 2003 U.S. App. LEXIS 21933 (9th Cir. Mont., Oct. 24, 2003)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Hans' remaining claims for relief denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner challenged the judgment from the Tenth Judicial District Court, Fergus County (Montana), which convicted him of deliberate homicide and attempted deliberate homicide after he entered pleas of guilty. Defendant filed an amended petition for post-conviction relief.


OVERVIEW: Defendant student shot and wounded high school staff and students. The court denied defendant's claims for post-conviction relief except for the right to further amend his petition to fully address sentencing issues. Because counsel abandoned the appeal, the court held that all claims foreclosed from appeal because of that abandonment could be raised in a post-conviction petition. Counsel's trial tactics and strategic decisions deserved great deference. In order to constitute ineffective assistance, acts of counsel had to stem from neglect or ignorance rather than from informed, professional deliberation. Counsel's advice as to the offense and the plea were obligatory, not strategic, decisions, and deference was given to those decisions. Withdrawal of guilty pleas was permitted under Mont. Code Ann. § 46-16-105(2). Counsel's acquiescence in the dissemination of the mental health reports pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 46-14-202 was not deficient. Counsel had a duty to inform defendant of the consequences of participation in the mental health evaluation. However, defendant was not harmed by the alleged failure to inform.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's post-conviction relief except to allow further amendment of the petition in order to more fully address sentencing issues. The court also denied claims of ineffective assistance of counsel during the trial and found the guilty pleas to be knowing and voluntary. The court held that defendant was prejudiced by counsel's abandonment of the appeal.

Gryczan v. State


No. 96-202


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


283 Mont. 433; 942 P.2d 112; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 136; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 699


April 11, 1997, Heard; April 17, 1997, Submitted  
July 2, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, The Honorable Jeffrey M. Sherlock, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Decision of District Court affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State challenged a judgment of the District Court for the First Judicial District, Lewis and Clark County (Montana), which declared Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-505 to be unconstitutional when applied to conduct engaged in by respondent citizens.


OVERVIEW: Citizens, without being arrested under Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-505, brought an action challenging the statute's constitutionality. The court granted summary judgment in favor of the citizens, and the State sought review. The court affirmed, holding that § 45-5-505 infringed upon the citizens' right of privacy that was guaranteed by Mont. Const. Art. II, § 10. Because the statute presented the citizens with a fear of prosecution, they had standing to challenge its validity. The State did not present any compelling interest sufficient to support the constitutional validity of Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-505. Section 45-5-505 prohibited same-gender sexual conduct, even between consenting adults. The State's asserted interests in preventing the spread of AIDS and protecting the moral values of the state were not supported by the facts and history surrounding the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of the citizens, declaring the statute to be unconstitutional.

State v. Nelson


No. 96-181


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


283 Mont. 231; 941 P.2d 441; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 132; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 576


May 1, 1997, Submitted  
June 24, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected June 26, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Seventh Judicial District, In and for the County of Dawson, The Honorable Dale Cox, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Order denying motion to quash investigative subpoena, or in alternative to suppress evidence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the District Court (Montana), which convicted him of driving under the influence of alcohol in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 61-8-401.


OVERVIEW: While driving under the influence of alcohol, defendant allowed his car to strike a guardrail. A friend drove him to the hospital, where the treating doctor conducted a test on him that revealed his blood alcohol level to be 0.233. The doctor told police that defendant's blood alcohol level explained his lack of pain from his injuries. The state then obtained the blood alcohol test results with an investigative subpoena under Mont. Code Ann. § 46-4-301, which defendant sought to suppress. The trial court denied the motion and defendant was convicted. On appeal the court held that 1) the exclusionary rule did not apply to the taking of defendant's blood because there doctor did not act at the direction or request of the state, 2) even if the doctor's statements to the police exceeded the scope of disclosure permitted under Mont. Code Ann. § 50-16-530(4), that violation did not provide a basis for suppression of the test results, 3) defendant had a reasonable expectation of privacy in the test results under Mont. Const. art. II, § 10, and 4) there was probable cause to support the issuance of the investigative subpoena for the results of defendant's blood alcohol test.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for driving under the influence of alcohol.

 

72 of 930 DOCUMENTS


State v. Dolan


No. 96-248


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


283 Mont. 245; 940 P.2d 436; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 123; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 583


April 10, 1997, Submitted on Briefs  
June 24, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Twenty-First Judicial District, In and for the County of Ravalli, The Honorable Jeffrey H. Langton, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Order of District Court which denied Dolan's motion to suppress evidence of routine hospital blood test reversed and Dolan's conviction and sentence for offense of driving under the influence of alcohol, reversed. Remanded to District Court for proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged her conviction and sentence for driving under the influence of alcohol, in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 61-8-40, in the District Court of the Twenty-First Judicial District, In and for the County of Ravalli (Montana), and the denial of her motion to suppress evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was in a car accident. An officer at the scene was told by someone that defendant smelled of alcohol. At the hospital, a routine blood test revealed a blood alcohol content of .109. Another officer requested another blood sample. Defendant was charged with driving under the influence of alcohol in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 61-8-401. Her motion to suppress the blood test results was denied and she was convicted. The court reversed. The court noted that neither officer had personal knowledge of defendant's alcohol consumption or received information from a reliable source. Because there was no probable cause, the test ordered by the officer was an unlawful search and seizure. The facts establishing probable cause for an investigative subpoena seeking the results of the routine test were the results of the illegally obtained test and a statement that blood had been taken at the hospital. The court held that the illegally obtained test could not provide probable cause. Because there was no indication that the unnamed source was reliable, that statement also did not establish probable cause. Accordingly, the results of both tests should have been suppressed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment denying defendant's motion to suppress evidence and reversed her conviction and sentence for driving under the influence of alcohol.

74 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Murer v. State Compensation Mut. Ins. Fund


No. 95-542


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


283 Mont. 210; 942 P.2d 69; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 130; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 566


February 18, 1997, Submitted  
June 23, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: Workers' Compensation Court, State of Montana, The Honorable Mike McCarter, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimants sought review of a judgment from the Workers' Compensation Court (Montana), which granted in part and denied in part their claim for benefits and fees on the grounds that the temporary cap on benefits had not applied. The state compensation fund cross-appealed on the grounds that an amount paid as a lump sum was to be increased and that refusal to increase the award had been unreasonable.


OVERVIEW: Claimants were injured at a time in which there were periods of temporary caps on benefits imposed. The claimants held the temporary caps had been temporary and had not applied past a certain date. The lower court's conclusion that the caps were permanent was reversed. A number of absent claimants sought to intervene which was denied on appeal with their petition for fees pursuant to the common fund doctrine. Questions arose as to lump sum settlements as well. On appeal, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court found that the 1987 law provided that temporary caps expired on a date certain and therefore any awards after that time were not subject to the caps and that the law in place at the time of the injury was the law that prevailed. The court held further that any ambiguities as to the settlement agreements were to be construed against the drafters. Finally, as to the issue of fees for the absent claimants, because the active claimants had also established a benefit for the absent claimants, the absent claimants had to contribute to the costs and fees incurred.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the findings of the lower court that the failure to increase the award was unreasonable, and remanded that portion of the findings which found that one claimant was not entitled to an increase based on the fact that the cap had not applied.

State ex rel. Jones v. District Court of the Fourth Judicial Dist.


No. 95-574


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


283 Mont. 1; 938 P.2d 1312; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 96; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 460


March 20, 1997, Submitted on Briefs  
May 28, 1997, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] District Court order reversed and 1989 Updated Decree vacated and set aside.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator property owners sought review of the denial of their motion to amend or set aside respondent District Court of the Fourth Judicial District of Missoula County's (Montana) updated decree of water rights on a certain creek.


OVERVIEW: The district court authorized the update of a decree of water rights from 1902 because of the physical deterioration of the original decree. The owners were not given notice of the action to update the decree. The updated decree included the ownership, priority dates, flow rates, place of use, and ditch used for each water right. The owners were involved in an overclaim dispute in the Montana Water Court. Upon review, the court found that the updated decree adjudicated water rights and exceeded the jurisdiction of the district court. It was solely in the province of the water court to determine priority dates, flow rates, place of use, and means of diversions of the water rights under Mont. Code Ann. § 85-2-234(6). The updated decree reallocated and adjudicated water rights that did not previously exist under the original 1902 decree. The substance of the decree itself was invalid because the district court exceeded its jurisdiction, and thus, Mont. Code Ann. § 85-5-301 afforded no remedy. The jurisdiction of the district court to supervise the distribution of water on the creek pursuant to the 1902 decree was governed by the provisions of Mont. Code Ann. § 85-2-406.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of the owners' motion to set aside the district court's updated decree of water rights on a certain creek. The court vacated and set aside the updated decree.

Zempel v. Uninsured Employers' Fund


No. 96-103


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


282 Mont. 424; 938 P.2d 658; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 89; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 415


July 11, 1996, Submitted on Briefs  
May 15, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: Workers' Compensation Court, State of Montana, The Honorable Mike McCarter, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee appealed from the judgment of the Workers' Compensation Court (Montana) that dismissed his petition for a declaratory judgment against defendant Uninsured Employers' Fund (UEF). The lower court concluded that Mont. Code Ann. § 39-71-501 (1991), as applied to the employee, did not deny the employee equal protection of the laws or access to the courts in violation of the Montana Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The employee injured his eye while employed by a tribe member under a logging contract with a tribally owned business. The employer did not carry workers' compensation insurance. A tribal court determined that the logging contract did not require the employer to carry workers' compensation insurance and that the Workers' Compensation Act (act) did not apply to tribe-owned businesses conducted exclusively with the boundaries of the reservation. The UEF then denied the employee's claim because the employer was not an "uninsured employer" as defined by § 39-71-501 and he could not therefore be required to carry workers' compensation insurance under the (act). The court held that the lower court did not err. The court reasoned that the exclusion of tribe business employees from UEF coverage via § 39-71-501 was rationally related to the legitimate government purpose of providing "substitute" benefits to employees of employers subject to but failing to comply with the act. The court also reasoned that no authority required guaranteed access to state courts for an injury where federal law prohibiting the exercise of state jurisdiction over the claim precluded such access.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the decision affirming the UEF's denial of compensation benefits to the employee because the statute that excluded the employer from the scope of the act did not deny the employee equal protection or access to the courts. No statute prevented the employee from pursuing non-act claims against the employer.

Heisler v. Hines Motor Co.


No. 95-485


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


282 Mont. 270; 937 P.2d 45; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 71; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 345


January 16, 1997, Heard; January 17, 1997, Submitted  
April 24, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: Workers' Compensation Court For the State of Montana The Honorable Michael McCarter, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Decision of Workers' Compensation Court reversed and matter remanded to Workers' Compensation Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee challenged the Workers' Compensation Court For the State of Montana, which denied his motion for summary judgment on the issue of whether he was constitutionally entitled to change his treating physician without the prior approval of the state compensation insurance fund.


OVERVIEW: The employee challenged the lower court's denial of his motion for summary judgment on the issue of whether he was constitutionally entitled to change his treating physician without the prior approval of the state compensation insurance fund. The court concluded that the lower court erred in denying the employee's motion for summary judgment because it violated the employee's equal protection of the laws. The court held that when the state made the payment of treatment costs contingent on its approval of the treating physician, it effectively removed the injured claimant's right to freely choose the physician of his or her choice. The court rejected the state's citation to the cost control achieved by limiting physician choice as the sole rational basis justifying the policy requiring physician preapproval. The court concluded that cost-control alone did not justify disparate treatment which violated an individual's right to equal protection of the law. The court concluded that the precise effect of the rule was to reduce costs by depriving a certain class of persons of benefits they would otherwise receive.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's denial of the employee's motion for summary judgment and remanded to the lower court.

Davis v. Union Pac. R.R.


No. 96-163, No. 96-031, No. 96-115


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


282 Mont. 233; 937 P.2d 27; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 70; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 328


January 14, 1997, Submitted  
April 16, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On Appeal From the Second Judicial District, In and for the County of Silver Bow, The Honorable James E. Purcell, Judge Presiding (No. 96-163).

On Appeal From the Eighth Judicial District, In and for the County of Cascade, The Honorable Robert Goff, Judge Presiding (No. 96-031).

On Appeal From the Second Judicial District, In and For the County of Silver Bow, The Honorable John W. Whelan, Judge Presiding (No. 96-115).



DISPOSITION: Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant railroad companies challenged the constitutionality of Mont. Code Ann. § 25-2-122(2) in actions filed by plaintiff railroad workers in the Eight Judicial District Court (Montana) and the Second Judicial District Court under the Federal Employers Liability Act (FELA), 45 U.S.C.S. §§ 51-60. The actions of the railroad workers were consolidated on appeal.


OVERVIEW: In railroad workers' actions against railroad companies brought in the eighth and second districts under the FELA, the railroad companies filed motions for a change of venue pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 25-2-122(2), a statute that allowed for a change of venue for tort victims injured by individuals or unincorporated non-resident tortfeasors but not for tort victims injured by non-resident corporations. The eighth district granted the motions and the second district denied the motions. In a consolidated appeal, the railroad workers challenged the constitutionality of § 25-2-122(2). The court remanded the cases holding that § 25-2-122(2) was unconstitutional. The court held that the classification used in the statute did not warrant review under strict scrutiny because there was no compelling state interest, and did not warrant review under middle-tier scrutiny because the state's interest in the classification was no greater than that of the interests of the railroad workers, and applied the rational basis test. The court concluded that the objective of § 25-2-122(2) bore no rational relation to the statute's classification and therefore violated the Equal Protection Clause.


OUTCOME: The court ruled in favor of the railroad companies. The court held that the statute allowing a change of venue for tort victims injured by individuals or unincorporated non-resident tortfeasors but not allowing a change of venue for tort victims injured by non-resident corporations violated the Equal Protection Clause.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff child, through his guardian ad litem, sought review of an order from the District Court for the Second Judicial District in Butte-Silver Bow County (Montana), which allowed defendant Public Housing Authority of Butte (authority) to amend its answer and allege a nonparty defense pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 27-1-703(6).


OVERVIEW: The child was struck by a motor home near a housing complex that was owned and operated by the authority, in which he was a tenant. He filed a complaint against the authority, the driver of the motor home, the State of Montana, and the Butte-Silver Bow County and settled with the driver and the state. The county's motion for summary judgment was granted. The child filed an amended complaint against the authority. Its answer did not include a specific nonparty defense and it did not amend its pleadings to allege such a defense by the discovery deadline. The district court, in a pretrial order, allowed the authority to assert a nonparty defense pursuant to Mont. Code. Ann. § 27-1-703(6). The child petitioned the court to reverse that portion of the district court's order allowing the authority to assert a nonparty defense. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded. The court held that § 27-1-703(6) was unconstitutional pursuant to the Montana Constitution as it allowed apportionment of liability to parties, in this case the child's parents, who were not named in the action and who did not have an opportunity to appear and defend themselves.


OUTCOME: The court reversed that portion of the district court's order which allowed the authority to assert a nonparty defense in the child's personal injury action and remanded the case to the district court for further proceedings consistent with the court's opinion and order.

State ex rel. The Missoulian v. Montana Twenty-First Judicial Dist. Court


No. 96-509


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


281 Mont. 285; 933 P.2d 829; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 36; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 175; 25 Media L. Rep. 1577


December 5, 1996, Submitted  
March 6, 1997, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relators newspaper filed an application for a writ of supervisory control to correct orders of respondents, Montana 21st Judicial District Court and the presiding judge, which required parties involved in a homicide case to comply with Rules of Prof'l Conduct R. 3.6 governing pretrial publicity and trial publicity and directed that no evidentiary material could be filed with the trial court unless it was under seal.


OVERVIEW: After the state filed an information against a suspect charged with homicide, the state and suspect agreed to the entry by respondents, Montana 21st Judicial District Court and its presiding judge, of an order (gag order) requiring that restrictions on pretrial publicity be followed in conformity with Rules of Prof'l Conduct R. 3.6. Respondents also directed that no evidentiary material could be filed with the trial court unless it was under seal (filing order). Relator newspaper filed an application for a writ of supervisory control to correct the orders. The court granted the writ. The court held that in issuing the filing order, respondents failed to comply with Mont. Code Ann. § 46-11-701, which required an evidentiary hearing and determination that dissemination of information in court filings would present a clear and present danger to trial fairness. The court held that the consent of the parties was irrelevant given the media's right to be heard concerning the issue. The court also held that the gag order was improper because it violated the Mont. Const. art. II, § 9, guarantee of the public's broad right to information about the entire criminal process.


OUTCOME: The court granted relator newspaper's application for a writ of supervisory control to correct orders of respondents, Montana 21st Judicial District Court and the presiding judge, which limited dissemination of information to the news media about a homicide case. The court held that respondents failed to follow statutory procedures and impermissibly restricted the public's constitutional right to information about the criminal process.

State v. Siegal


No. 95-419


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


281 Mont. 250; 934 P.2d 176; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 39; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 158


November 7, 1996, Submitted; October 24, 1996, Heard  
March 6, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Fifth Judicial District, in and for the County of Madison, The Honorable Frank M. Davis, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of a decision by the District Court of the Fifth Judicial District, in and for the County of Madison (Montana), which denied their motion to suppress the fruits of a search on the grounds that the warrantless use of a thermal imager violated their constitutional rights. In addition, one defendant sought review of the trial court's denial of his motion to dismiss the charges against him on double jeopardy grounds.


OVERVIEW: Through the use of a thermal imager, a police investigator determined that defendants' shed was discharging an amount of heat, which was consistent with the cultivation of marijuana plants. Based on this and other information, police obtained a search warrant. The search yielded evidence of marijuana cultivation and police arrested defendants. The trial court denied defendants' motion to suppress the fruits of the search. In addition, the trial court denied one defendant's motion to dismiss the charges against him on double jeopardy grounds. The court reversed the trial court's ruling on the motion to suppress and held that the warrantless use of a thermal imager was an unconstitutional search. Moreover, the court held that, absent the evidence derived from the use of the thermal imager, the police lacked sufficient probable cause to support the issuance of a search warrant for defendants' premises. The court affirmed the trial court's ruling on the motion to dismiss and held that the criminal proceedings against one defendant, after the state obtained a judgment against him in a civil forfeiture action, were not violative of constitutional double jeopardy provisions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's ruling on the motion to suppress, but affirmed the trial court's ruling on the motion to dismiss.
