City of Billings v. County Water Dist.


No. 96-462


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


281 Mont. 219; 935 P.2d 246; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 35; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 144


December 5, 1996, Submitted on Briefs  
February 28, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication March 20, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Yellowstone, The Honorable G. Todd Baugh, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant water district sought review of an order of the District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, in and for the County of Yellowstone (Montana), which denied its request for a preliminary injunction. Plaintiff city filed suit against the district to recover water charges that the district refused to pay. The district sought to enjoin the city from enforcing a rate increase, pending the outcome of the litigation.


OVERVIEW: When the parties entered into a contract for the sale of water, the city's water rates were regulated by another entity. Later, the legislature adopted Mont. Code Ann. 69-7-101 (1981), which allowed the city to regulate its own rates. When the city raised the district's rates more than the contract allowed, the district refused to pay. On appeal, the court reversed because the district court abused its discretion. The court found that the district had shown a violation of the contract clauses of the state and federal constitutions, Mont. Const. art. II, § 31, U.S. Const. art. I, § 10. The court used a three-part test to analyze the contract clause challenge. It found that the city's use of the statute to raise rates violated the contract clause because the city's reason, to raise revenue for the city, was unrelated to the purpose for which the statute was adopted, which was to simplify the regulatory process. Further, the district showed that it's injury could not be remedied by a monetary damage award after a trial on the merits. Because the district met the requirements of Mont. Code Ann. § 27-19-201(3) it was entitled to a preliminary injunction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the matter and vacated the district court's order denying the water district's application for a preliminary injunction in an action brought by the city to enforce an increase in its water rates.

 

83 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Albright v. State


No. 96-704


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


281 Mont. 196; 933 P.2d 815; 1997 Mont. LEXIS 29; 54 Mont. St. Rep. 132


February 18, 1997, Submitted  
February 27, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication March 20, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, In and for the County of Cascade, The Honorable Marge Johnson, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant revenue department appealed the summary judgments of the District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, Cascade County (Montana) in favor plaintiff taxpayers. The district court found that the use of more than one valuation approach violated Mont. Const. art. VIII, § 3 and Mont. Code Ann. § 15-7-112 and that certain assessments were not enforceable because of a violation of the deadline in Mont. Code Ann. § 15-8-201.


OVERVIEW: The court found that Montana law required the revaluation of all property and that the revenue department set up a valuation system using the cost approach, the market data approach, the income approach, or a combination of the approaches depending on the property. The taxpayers contested the revaluation. The court reversed the summary judgments. In examining the statutory scheme and legislative history, the court concluded that the "same method of appraisal" in Mont. Code Ann. § 15-7-112 did not require the use of a single approach, but referred to a consistent process for determining market value using multiple approaches. The court found that the intent of Mont. Const. art. VIII, § 3 was also not to limit valuation to a single approach and that the revenue department's methodology was a reasonable attempt to equalize the appraisal of real property in the state. The court concluded that the assessment notices sent after the deadline in Mont. Code Ann. § 15-8-201 were not unenforceable because only the assessment had to be completed before the deadline, and there was no substantive effect of the taxpayers' ability to appeal the valuations.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the summary judgments in favor of the taxpayers in their challenges to the revenue department's use of multiple approaches and late assessment notices in the revaluation of taxable property.

 

84 of 930 DOCUMENTS


State v. Lambert


No. 95-335


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


280 Mont. 231; 929 P.2d 846; 1996 Mont. LEXIS 271; 53 Mont. St. Rep. 1379


September 19, 1996, Heard; September 19, 1996, Submitted  
December 16, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Twenty-First Judicial District In and for the County of Ravalli, The Honorable Jeffrey Langton, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with several offenses after he caused a head-on collision. He pleaded guilty to driving with a suspended or revoked license, failing to have insurance, and failing to use a seatbelt. Defendant appealed after the District Court of the Twenty-First Judicial District, in and for the County of Ravalli (Montana), convicted him of criminal endangerment in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-207(1).


OVERVIEW: Defendant pulled out of a parking lot and headed into oncoming traffic. He failed a field sobriety test; his driver's license had previously been revoked. Defendant pleaded guilty on three related counts and was tried on charges of criminal endangerment and driving under the influence. Defendant unsuccessfully moved for an acquittal on the criminal endangerment charge, claiming that the State had failed to prove that he had "knowingly" created a substantial risk of injury to another. Defendant appealed, asserting that the trial court had erred in instructing the jury that, to be convicted, defendant needed only to be aware of his conduct and did not need to be aware of the probable result of his conduct. In reversing, the court held that the trial court had erred in interpreting the scienter requirement for § 45-5-207(1) and, in doing so, had impermissibly altered the State's burden of proof in violation of Mont. Const. art. II, § 17. It was not enough to prove that defendant had intended to engage in conduct, but rather, pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 45-2-101(34) (1995), the State had to prove that defendant was aware of the high probability of the risks posed by his conduct.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded the cause with direction to reconsider defendant's motion for acquittal. It ordered that, if the trial court denied the motion for acquittal, it should grant defendant's motion for a new trial.

State v. Smith


Nos. 95-477 and 95-494


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


280 Mont. 158; 931 P.2d 1272; 1996 Mont. LEXIS 259; 53 Mont. St. Rep. 1345


October 29, 1996, Heard; November 7, 1996, Submitted  
December 13, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied January 30, 1997. Released for Publication January 30, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eleventh Judicial District, In and for the County of Flathead, The Honorable John W. Larson, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of District Court affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the District Court of the Eleventh Judicial District, Flathead County (Montana), which imposed four death penalties. Defendant contended that the trial court's findings of the existence or nonexistence of aggravating and mitigating circumstances was not supported by the evidence, and that the sentence imposed was disproportionate to the penalty imposed in similar cases.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of aggravated kidnapping and deliberate homicides, and was sentenced to death. Defendant contended that the sentence was imposed as the result of vindictive prosecution; the death penalty statutory scheme unconstitutionally allocated the burden of proof of aggravating and mitigating circumstances; the trial court's finding of the existence or nonexistence of aggravating and mitigating circumstances were not supported by the evidence; the trial court erred in excluding mitigating evidence; and the sentence imposed was disproportionate to the penalty imposed in similar cases. The court affirmed the trial court's imposition of the death penalty, finding that the trial court's findings concerning aggravating and mitigating circumstance were supported by the evidence. The court also concluded that it was not its role to reweigh the evidence and that the trial court applied the proper standard in weighing the aggravating and mitigating circumstances. The court further found that the sentence imposed was not the result of vindictive prosecution, and that the penalty imposed was not disproportionate to the penalty imposed in similar cases.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court imposing the death penalty, finding that the trial court properly weighed the aggravating and mitigating circumstances and that the trial court's findings were supported by sufficient evidence. The court also concluded that the penalty imposed was not excessive or disproportionate to the penalty imposed in similar cases.

Reynolds v. United States


No. 95-482


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


280 Mont. 191; 929 P.2d 844; 1996 Mont. LEXIS 269; 53 Mont. St. Rep. 1360


May 30, 1996, Heard; May 31, 1996, Submitted  
December 13, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication January 3, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: CERTIFIED QUESTION FROM: United States District Court, District of Montana, Billings Division. The Honorable Richard W. Anderson, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Decision in Plumb has rendered it unnecessary to reach constitutional issue in first certified question and has rendered second certified question moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States District Court for the District of Montana, Billings Division (Montana) sought review by certified question of the meaning of Mont. Code Ann. § 27-1-703 (1995) in a case instituted by plaintiff employee against defendants, contractor and United States, for work related injuries.


OVERVIEW: The employee worked for a subcontractor at the construction site of the contractor who was hired by the United States to work on a dam. The employee was injured on the job and received workers' compensation benefits through his employer. Thereafter, the employee instituted an action against the United States. The United States filed a third-party complaint against the contractor. The employee then filed an amended complaint against the contractor. Both defendants then were granted leave to file amended answers asserting that the injuries were caused by the employer's negligence. The federal trial court certified two questions for review in light of changes made by Mont. Code Ann. § 27-1-703(4) (1995). On review, the court held that as the immune employer and co-employee could have been neither apportioned liability or negligence as non-parties, nor named, sued, and thereby, apportioned liability or negligence as parties, there was no way in which the injured worker's third-party recovery could have been reduced as a result of any conduct by the employer or co-employee.


OUTCOME: The court held that the injured employee's third-party recovery could not be reduced by the actions of the employer or the co-employee. The court also held that the second certified question was moot and that it was unnecessary to reach the constitutional issue in the first question.

 

87 of 930 DOCUMENTS


State v. Brander


No. 96-389


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


280 Mont. 148; 930 P.2d 31; 1996 Mont. LEXIS 258; 53 Mont. St. Rep. 1340


November 21, 1996, Submitted on Briefs  
December 12, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied January 2, 1997. Released for Publication January 2, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, The Honorable Thomas Honzel, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the District Court of the First Judicial District, in and for the County of Lewis and Clark (Montana), which denied his motion to dismiss an indictment that increased his driving under the influence of alcohol (DUI) offense to a felony based upon his prior DUI convictions.


OVERVIEW: After defendant was arrested for DUI, Mont. Code Ann. § 61-8-714(6) (1995) was enacted. Section 61-8-714(6) allowed the state to consider all previous DUI convictions in imposing a felony sanction upon a repeat DUI offender. The previous statute permitted the consideration of only those DUIs convictions less than five years old. Defendant was charged with DUI. He filed a motion to dismiss the indictment, claiming that the state's application of § 61-8-714(6) to his case violated his constitutional right not to be subjected to ex post facto laws. The trial court denied the motion. Defendant entered a plea of guilty. On appeal, the court reversed the trial court's order and remanded the case to the trial court. The court held that § 61-8-714(6) did not violate the ex post facto clauses of the federal or Montana Constitutions because defendant was on notice that his prior DUI convictions would be considered in a subsequent DUI conviction, so the legal consequences of defendant's offense had not changed. The court ruled, however, that the trial court erred in considering defendant's nine-year-old DUI conviction because the offense had been expunged pursuant to the previous statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order denying defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment charging him with felony DUI based on his prior DUI convictions. The court remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

Sage v. Gamble

SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


279 Mont. 459; 929 P.2d 822; 1996 Mont. LEXIS 243; 53 Mont. St. Rep. 1234

DISPOSITION: Affirmed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parole board sought review of a decision from the Third Judicial District, Powell County (Montana), which concluded that respondent inmate's right to due process had been violated by the denial of the opportunity to personally appear at his parole hearing.


OVERVIEW: The inmate filed a petition for habeas corpus after he was denied parole without an interview. The trial court granted relief and ordered the parole board to conduct another hearing at which the inmate could be present. The court affirmed, holding that Mont. The court held that compliance with the personal appearance rule was necessary to satisfy the minimum due process requirements under U.S. Const. amend. XIV and as independently required by Mont. Const. art. II, § 17.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of habeas corpus relief to the inmate.

Kills on Top v. State


No. 94-183


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


279 Mont. 384; 928 P.2d 182; 1996 Mont. LEXIS 249; 53 Mont. St. Rep. 1197


September 15, 1995, Heard; November 2, 1995, Submitted  
November 25, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied January 2, 1997. Released for Publication January 2, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Sixteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Custer, The Honorable M. James Sorte, Judge presiding.
State v. Kills on Top, 243 Mont. 56, 793 P.2d 1273, 1990 Mont. LEXIS 161 (Mont., 1990)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part and remanded to the District Court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In death penalty case, petitioner challenged a decision by the Sixteenth Judicial District, County of Custer court (Montana), which denied the his amended petition for postconviction relief pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 46-21-101, and his motion for reconsideration. The district court ruled that most of the claims presented by the petitioner on appeal were barred by the doctrine of res judicata.


OVERVIEW: At issue in the petitioner's appeal was whether the petitioner presented issues for postconviction relief review that could have been presented earlier on appeal. The court found that whether one person lived and another died for conduct alleged to have occurred during the same transaction should not have hinged on a district court's discretionary denial of a motion to amend a petition for postconviction relief. The court found that the district court's denial was particularly unreasonable in light of Mont. R. Civ. P. 15(a)'s admonition that leave was to be freely given when justice so required. The motion to amend was filed by the date the district court had established for the amendment and no prejudice to the state was established which would justify its denial. The court further found that pursuant to the individualized treatment required by statute, Mont. Code Ann. § 46-18-310, and rules of proportionality, the propriety of a death sentence had to have been evaluated based on the conduct of the defendant and not by attributing to him the conduct of some other person which he neither participated in nor approved.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision against the petitioner in part, reversed it in part, and remanded to the district court for further proceedings.

Plumb v. Fourth Judicial Dist. Court


No. 96-023


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


279 Mont. 363; 927 P.2d 1011; 1996 Mont. LEXIS 251; 53 Mont. St. Rep. 1187


May 30, 1996, Heard; May 31, 1996, Submitted  
November 22, 1996, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] District Court's order permitting Southgate Mall Associates to amend their answer and set forth a seventh affirmative defense which assigned liability to unnamed persons, reversed, and case remanded to the District Court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs petitioned the court for supervisory control to determine whether the District Court for the Fourth Judicial District in Missoula County erred in permitting defendant mall to file an amended answer where it alleged that plaintiffs' damages were caused by a nonparty and that defendant's liability should be reduced based on Mon. Code Ann. § 27-1-703 (1995).


OVERVIEW: Personal injury plaintiffs brought an action against defendant mall to recover damages for injuries. The district court allowed defendant to amend its answer to include a seventh affirmative defense in which defendant alleged that plaintiffs' damages were caused by plaintiffs' physician who was not a party to the instant action. The court granted plaintiffs' motion for supervisory control and reversed the district court's order. The court found that while apportioning liability for damages was a legitimate governmental objective, the 1995 amendments to Mon. Code Ann. § 27-1-703 (1987) was not rationally related to the legitimate governmental interest. The court concluded that it was unfair to apportion liability to parties who were not named in the lawsuit and who did not have an opportunity to appear and defend themselves. The court found that although some portions of the statute violated the notions of substantive due process, the remainder of the statute could be severed and applied.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the district's court's decision permitting defendant mall to amend its answer to assign liability to a nonparty. The court found that the statute that permitted apportionment of nonparty liability violated notions of substantive due process and thus was unenforceable.

State v. Broken Rope


No. 96-054


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


278 Mont. 427; 925 P.2d 1157; 1996 Mont. LEXIS 198; 53 Mont. St. Rep. 987


August 15, 1996, Submitted On Briefs  
October 18, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Yellowstone, The Honorable Russell C. Fagg, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions in the District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, in and for the County of Yellowstone (Montana), on his guilty pleas to charges of carrying a concealed weapon, criminal possession of dangerous drugs, and criminal possession of drug paraphernalia.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested outside of a convenience store. When the police officer asked whether defendant had any weapons or drugs on him, defendant replied that he had a knife in his pants and some marijuana in his pocket. Defendant filed a motion to suppress the knife and drugs seized by the police. Defendant pled guilty to the charges against him, but reserved his right to appeal. On appeal, the court reversed his weapon and drug convictions. The court found that the objective data that the police officer observed about defendant was that he entered and left a convenience store at late at night, used the telephone, moved around the convenience store, put his hands in his pockets, and stared at the police officer. The court ruled that defendant's actions did not support a particularized suspicion that defendant was, or had been, engaged in criminal activity. Thus, the court held that under the totality of the circumstances, the record was insufficient to support a finding that the police officer had a particularized suspicion that defendant was engaged in criminal activity; therefore, the trial court's denial of defendant's motion to suppress was clearly erroneous.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's convictions for criminal possession of drugs and drug paraphernalia, and for carrying a concealed weapon.

93 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Keating v. Sherlock


Nos. 96-402, 96-452, 96-457


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


278 Mont. 218; 924 P.2d 1297; 1996 Mont. LEXIS 179; 53 Mont. St. Rep. 855



September 10, 1996, Decided



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Keating not entitled to evidentiary hearing on question of manifest necessity and that mistrial declared at his request and for reasons not attributable to judicial or prosecutorial overreaching was not barrier to reprosecution.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant brought three applications to the court for extraordinary writs related to a retrial of his case. Defendant's first trial had ended in a mistrial shortly after opening statements. Defendant claimed that a retrial was barred on double jeopardy grounds.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with felony assault and drug offenses. After a lunch break following opening statements, one of the jurors advised the court that she was ill and would not be returning to jury duty. No alternate juror was available. On review, the court held that defendant was not entitled to an evidentiary hearing on the question of manifest necessity and that a mistrial declared at his request and for reasons not attributable to judicial or prosecutorial overreaching was not a barrier to reprosecution. The court found that jeopardy had attached in the first trial because the jury was empaneled and sworn. The court then overruled precedent, and held that review of a double jeopardy issue must occur before the second trial. The court reasoned that once defendant endured the second trial, regardless of conviction or acquittal, he had incurred the risk; he had been put in "jeopardy," and that fact could not be remedied or expunged after the fact. The court then found that there was no requirement that manifest necessity for the mistrial of the first trial be shown because that standard only applied where the mistrial had been declared without the defendant's request or consent.


OUTCOME: The court held that defendant was not entitled to an evidentiary hearing on the question of manifest necessity and that a mistrial declared at his request and for reasons not attributable to judicial or prosecutorial overreaching was not a barrier to reprosecution.

Petitioner filed a petition for post-conviction relief, arguing that the division's restriction rendered his guilty plea involuntary because the district court did not inform him of the effect of any penalty enhancement provision or special parole restriction. The court found that the division had modified petitioner's sentence by restricting his parole eligibility. State v. Helfrich


No. 95-333


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


277 Mont. 452; 922 P.2d 1159; 1996 Mont. LEXIS 159; 53 Mont. St. Rep. 741


May 2, 1996, Heard; May 16, 1996, Submitted  
August 9, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Second Judicial District, In and for the County of Silver Bow, The Honorable James Purcell, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment entered by the District Court of the Second Judicial District, Silver Bow County (Montana), which accepted his conditional guilty pleas to misdemeanor criminal defamation, Mont. Code Ann. § 45-8-212(2) and stalking, Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-220(1)(b), with a reservation of the right to appeal from the order denying his motion to dismiss the charges pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 46-12-204(3).


OVERVIEW: On review, the court reversed and remanded, holding that the criminal defamation statute, Mont. Code Ann. § 45-8-212(2), was unconstitutionally overbroad and violated the First and Fourteenth Amendments, and Mont. Const. art. II, § 7 because on its face the statute prohibited truthful criticism when not communicated for good motives and justifiable ends. The court held that the stalking statute, Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-220(1)(b), was not facially defective because it specifically did not apply to constitutionally protected activities such as truthful communications, pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-220(2). The burden was on defendant to prove that his activity was constitutionally protected. Truth was not an absolute defense to a charge of stalking, which focused on conduct which might, but did not necessarily, involve speech. If defendant's fliers contained truthful information and were otherwise lawfully posted, then the act of posting the fliers may have been a constitutionally protected activity, which then required a determination of whether the statement was an element of conduct which was harassing, intimidating or threatening within the purview of the stalking statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded to allow defendant to withdraw his plea of guilty to the stalking charge and to raise and prove that his conduct fell within the constitutionally protected activity exception of the stalking statute.

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Fourth Judicial District, In and for the County of Missoula, The Honorable John W. Larson, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction for criminal possession of dangerous drugs a felony in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 45-9-102 entered on defendant's guilty plea by the District Court of the Fourth Judicial District, in and for the County of Missoula (Montana). Defendant also challenged the district court's underlying orders denying his motions to dismiss under Mont. Code Ann. §§ 46-11-504(1) and 45-9-102.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to driving under the influence of alcohol and/or drugs (DUI) in a municipal trial court. Immediately after defendant's arrest for DUI, several pills were found in his possession. As such, he was charged in the district court with criminal possession of dangerous drugs. The court also held that defendant was not denied equal protection of the laws under Mont. Code Ann. § 45-9-102.

Tax Lien Servs. v. Hall


No. 95-395


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


277 Mont. 126; 919 P.2d 396; 1996 Mont. LEXIS 130; 53 Mont. St. Rep. 614


Heard: May 21, 1996, May 31, 1996, Submitted  
July 12, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Twentieth Judicial District, In and for the County of Lake, The Honorable C. B. McNeil, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the District Court granting summary judgment for TLS reversed. Summary judgment entered for Hall, and remanded for further proceedings consistent with this Opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner sought review of an order by the District Court of the Twentieth Judicial District, In and for the County of Lake, (Montana), which ruled that errors in obtaining a tax deed were cured by appellee tax service company's publication of a Notice of Claim of Tax Title pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. §15-18-413. The trial court found that the company was the owner in fee simple of the property owner's real estate.


OVERVIEW: The property owner failed to pay real property taxes on his property. Subsequently, the company obtained an assignment of a Certificate of Tax Sale for the property and mailed and published a notice to all interested parties, including the property owner at his address as it appeared in the county records. The County Treasurer issued a tax deed to the company. The company ran a newspaper ad entitled "Corrected Notice of Claim of a Tax Title" and filed an action to quiet title to the real property. The trial court held that errors in obtaining the tax deed were cured by publication of the corrected notice. The issue was whether the applicant for the tax deed, the company, could avoid having the tax deed declared null and void by publishing notice to cure defects in the notice provided. The court stated that where the identity of the property owner was known or otherwise readily ascertainable, actual rather than constructive notice prior to a proceeding which would adversely affect the liberty or property interests of the party was required. The company could not avoid having the tax deed declared null and void by publishing notice in order to cure defects in the notice.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's grant of summary judgment for the company, entered summary judgment for the property owner, and remanded the matter for further proceedings.

 

98 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Frazier v. Montana State Dep't of Corrections by & Through Montana Adult Probation & Parole


No. 95-501


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


277 Mont. 82; 920 P.2d 93; 1996 Mont. LEXIS 114; 53 Mont. St. Rep. 551


May 9, 1996, Submitted On Briefs  
June 20, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Second Judicial District, In and for the County of Silver Bow, The Honorable John W. Whelan, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant probationer sought pro se review of a ruling from the District Court of the Second Judicial District (Montana), which determined that the supervision fee in Mont. Code Ann. § 46-23-1031(1)(a) was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The probationer claimed that he was sentenced prior to the enactment of § 46-23-1031(1)(a) and that § 46-23-1031(1)(a) could not be retroactively applied to him and other probationers and parolees similarly situated. Further, the probationer claimed that § 46-23-1031(1)(a) violated the prohibition against ex post facto laws contained in U.S. Const. art. I, § 10 and U.S. Const. art. II, § 31. The district court found that the supervision fee in Mont. Code Ann. § 46-23-1031(1)(a) was constitutional. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court found that the probationer was not being punished for his criminal activities, but rather was helping to pay the costs of his supervision. The court found that the legislative history of the supervision statute did not reveal any intent to punish. Even if the supervision statute did have a deterrent purpose, the court found that said purpose did not automatically mark the statute as a form of punishment. Thus, the court found that the district court was correct in concluding that the supervision fee set forth in § 46-23-1031(1)(a) did not violate the prohibition against ex post facto laws.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's holding that the supervision fee did not violate the prohibition against ex post facto laws.

 

99 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Erger v. Askren (In re A.R.A.)


No. 95-243


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


277 Mont. 66; 919 P.2d 388; 1996 Mont. LEXIS 112; 53 Mont. St. Rep. 543


April 30, 1996, Submitted  
June 20, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Yellowstone, The Honorable Robert W. Holmstrom, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed in part and affirmed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant father sought review of a judgment from the District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, County of Yellowstone (Montana), which awarded custody of his child to respondent stepfather.


OVERVIEW: The father and the mother divorced and the mother was awarded custody of the couple's child. The mother later remarried. When she died, the stepfather petitioned for custody of the child. The district court awarded custody to the stepfather based on the best interest of the child test. On appeal, the court reversed and awarded custody to the father. A natural parent had a constitutional right to parent his or her child. A finding of abuse, neglect, or dependency was the jurisdictional prerequisite for any court-ordered transfer of custody from a natural parent to a third party. When a surviving parent did not voluntarily relinquish custody, the best interest of the child test could be used only after a showing of dependency or abuse and neglect by the natural parent. The district court erred in awarding custody of the child to the stepfather based on the best interest of the child test in view of the fact there were no allegations of abuse and neglect or dependency on the part of the father. The district court was informed of both parties' financial resources and it did not abuse its discretion in directing the parties to pay their own attorney fees.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that awarded custody of a child to the stepfather. Custody was awarded to the father.

 

100 of 930 DOCUMENTS


State v. Fuller


No. 95-343


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


276 Mont. 155; 915 P.2d 809; 1996 Mont. LEXIS 58; 53 Mont. St. Rep. 325


January 11, 1996, Heard and Submitted  
April 16, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Yellowstone, The Honorable Robert W. Holmstrom, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, Yellowstone County (Montana) that, pursuant to his guilty plea, convicted defendant of rape and sexual assault.


OVERVIEW: After defendant admitted to past criminal acts at a treatment facility, which he was required to do by court order, he was charged with rape and sexual assault. He moved to dismiss the charges, alleging that the state violated his constitutionally guaranteed privilege against compelled self-incrimination. Defendant's motion was denied, and he pled guilty. Defendant appealed. The state supreme court found that the policies of the treatment program required that defendant fully and honestly disclose his offense history. If he failed to do so, his probation would have been revoked and he would been sent to prison. It was therefore undisputed that the state compelled defendant to divulge past activities that it knew would be criminal. The information divulged by defendant was self-incriminatory. On the basis of the offense history disclosed, defendant was charged and convicted of additional crimes. Accordingly, defendant's motion to dismiss should have been granted because the state improperly compelled him to disclose past criminal acts in violation of his Fifth Amendment privileges against self incrimination and his constitutionally guaranteed right to remain silent.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court reversed defendant's conviction. It found that the state improperly compelled him to disclose past criminal acts which information when divulged by defendant was self-incriminatory. Accordingly, such disclosure was a violation of defendant's Fifth Amendment privileges against self incrimination and his constitutionally guaranteed right to remain silent. Hence, defendant's motion to dismiss was improperly denied.

 

101 of 930 DOCUMENTS


State v. Smaage


No. 95-455


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


276 Mont. 94; 915 P.2d 192; 1996 Mont. LEXIS 57; 53 Mont. St. Rep. 294


March 14, 1996, Submitted On Briefs  
April 15, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, The Honorable Jeffrey M. Sherlock, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the District Court of the First Judicial District, Lewis and Clark County (Montana), which convicted him of felony criminal endangerment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was originally charged with driving under the influence of alcohol (DUI). When it was discovered that defendant had seven prior DUI convictions and a conviction for negligent homicide, the charge was changed to criminal endangerment. Following his conviction, defendant argued on appeal that he was incorrectly charged under the criminal endangerment statute rather than under more specific DUI statutes. The court held that, when there was no clear and manifest legislative intent to the contrary and different proof was required under two alternative statutes, it was not necessary that the most specific statutory violation be charged. The crime to be charged was a matter of prosecutorial discretion. The court concluded that the facts supported a charge of criminal endangerment. Defendant also argued that the statute was vague as applied to him. The court disagreed and affirmed the judgment. The court noted that, with his history of DUI convictions and negligent homicide, defendant should reasonably have understood that his drunk driving created a substantial risk of serious bodily injury to others and was therefore proscribed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment convicting defendant of criminal endangerment.

State v. Graveley


No. 95-295


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


275 Mont. 519; 915 P.2d 184; 1996 Mont. LEXIS 51; 53 Mont. St. Rep. 239


February 1, 1996, Submitted On Briefs  
April 5, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing Denied May 7, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, The Honorable Ed McLean, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the District Court of the First Judicial District of the County of Lewis and Clark (Montana), which convicted him of the offense of solicitation to commit deliberate homicide. He appealed from the district court's sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to attempting to hire another person to murder his former girlfriend. In signing the plea agreement, he acknowledged that he could be sentenced to serve from 10 to 100 years in prison. Defendant contended that the district court erred because the written judgment was inconsistent with the prior oral pronouncement of his sentence. The district court orally sentenced him to a period of 40 years with the first 10 years to be served in prison. The written judgment sentenced him to 40 years and recommended that he serve a portion of his sentence in prison. At the sentencing hearing, he presented evidence that he argued supported a sentence of less than the mandatory minimum of 10 years under Mont. Code Ann. § 46-18-222 (1993). Upon review, the court found that defendant was not prejudiced because while the district court incorrectly varied its written judgment from its oral pronouncement of sentence, the error was harmless because the written judgment was more lenient than the oral pronouncement. The exceptions of § 46-18-222 did not apply because defendant received more than the statutory minimum sentence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's sentence for the offense of solicitation to commit deliberate homicide.

 

103 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Stratemeyer v. Lincoln County


No. 95-326


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


276 Mont. 67; 915 P.2d 175; 1996 Mont. LEXIS 48; 53 Mont. St. Rep. 245


January 11, 1996, Submitted  
April 5, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Nineteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Lincoln, The Honorable C. B. McNeil, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee appealed the order of the District Court of the Nineteenth Judicial District, Lincoln County (Montana), which granted a motion to dismiss the action that was filed by defendants, county, county commissioners, and county sheriff. The trial court held that the action was time-barred, that the Workers' Compensation Act (WCA) provided an exclusive remedy, and that the Montana Safety Act did not apply to the police officer's claim.


OVERVIEW: The employee was a deputy who suffered severe emotional trauma after he unsuccessfully tried to save a teenage suicide victim. The employee's workers' compensation claim was denied. The employee filed the instant action without first filing a notice of claim with the county clerk within the limitations period. On appeal, the court reversed the trial court's decision. The court held that although filing a notice of state tort claims pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 2-9-301(2) was a prerequisite to filing an action, no prior filing was necessary under Mont. Code Ann. § 2-9-301(3) for an action against a political subdivision. Thus, the complaint was not time-barred. The court held that because an employee with "mental-mental" injuries did not suffer an injury as defined in the WCA, the WCA did not afford him any "remedy." Thus, in keeping with the quid pro quo nature of the WCA, the employee was allowed to proceed in tort against defendants. The court also found that the Montana Safety Act applied to mental as well as physical injuries and, thus, there was a factual issue as to whether defendants breached their duty in failing to train, supervise, treat, or debrief the employee.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court, which had dismissed the employee's tort action for mental injuries suffered on the job. The court remanded the action.

Wadsworth v. State


No. 94-602


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


275 Mont. 287; 911 P.2d 1165; 1996 Mont. LEXIS 30; 53 Mont. St. Rep. 146; 11 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 861


October 17, 1995, Heard; October 17, 1995, Submitted  
February 26, 1996, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, In and for the County of Cascade, The Honorable Thomas McKittrick, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant State challenged the judgment of the District Court of the Eighth Judicial District (Montana), which denied the State's motion for summary judgment and entered judgment upon a jury verdict in favor of plaintiff employee in his action for wrongful termination under the Wrongful Discharge from Employment Act (WDFEA).


OVERVIEW: The State argued, inter alia, that the employee's action was barred by res judicata and that he had no constitutional right to employment. The employee argued that the conflict-of-interest rule, Mont. Dept. of Rev. R. 81-2, infringed upon his right to pursue additional employment activities. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that the employee's wrongful termination was not barred by res judicata because his petition for judicial review of an agency decision that validated Rule 81-2 was a distinct and separate proceeding. The court also held that the right to the opportunity to pursue employment was a fundamental right under Mont. Const. art. II, § 3 because it was a right without which other constitutionally guaranteed rights would have little meaning. The court applied strict scrutiny analysis and found that Rule 81-2 infringed upon the employee's right to the opportunity to pursue employment outside of his government employment. The court concluded that the employee's termination was wrongful because the State failed to demonstrate a compelling interest for the infringement by Rule 81-2 and retaliated against him for refusing to violate policy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in finding that the State wrongfully discharged an employee for refusing to comply with a State agency's conflict-of-interest rule.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant gasoline retailer sought review of an order from the District Court In and for the County of Lewis and Clark (Montana), which granted respondent Montana Department of Transportation's motion for summary judgment in the retailer's suit for declaratory and injunctive relief, and for a refund of taxes paid under Mont. Code Ann. § 15-70-204.

State v. Martel


No. 94-295


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


273 Mont. 143; 902 P.2d 14; 1995 Mont. LEXIS 188; 52 Mont. St. Rep. 873


May 4, 1995, Submitted  
August 24, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released For Publication September 11, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Twenty-First Judicial District, In and for the County of Ravalli, The Honorable Jeffrey H. Langton, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the District Court of the Twenty-First Judicial District, in and for the County of Ravalli (Montana), which found him guilty following a bench trial of misdemeanor stalking pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-220.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that Montana's anti-stalking law in § 45-5-220, was unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. He argued that § 45-5-220 was unconstitutionally vague on its face because it did not define "repeatedly," "harassing," "intimidating," "reasonable apprehension," or "substantial emotional distress." Defendant argued that without the definitions, a person of ordinary intelligence could not understand the statute. The court disagreed and affirmed his conviction. The court held that § 45-5-220 was not unconstitutionally vague because the legislature was not required to define every term it employed when constructing a statute. The court held that if a term was one of common usage and readily understood, it would be presumed that a reasonable person of average intelligence comprehended it, and that words such as "repeatedly," "harassing," and "intimidating" had commonly understood meanings. The court held that defendant's overbreadth argument failed because he made no argument of how § 45-5-220 infringed on any constitutionally protected First Amendment activities.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for misdemeanor stalking.

 

109 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Gulbrandson v. Carey


No. 94-526


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


272 Mont. 494; 901 P.2d 573; 1995 Mont. LEXIS 194; 52 Mont. St. Rep. 843


April 11, 1995, Submitted  
August 24, 1995, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff judge challenged a decision of defendant Montana Public Employees' Retirement Board (board), which held that an amendment increasing pension benefits for judges pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 19-5-502 did not apply to judges who retired prior to the effective date of the amendment.


OVERVIEW: The statute, 1989 Mont. Laws 664, § 3, amended by Mont. Code Ann. § 19-5-502, increased pension benefits for judges who served more than 15 years. The amendment provided that it took effect two years after its enactment. The judge, who had served for 29 years, retired after the amendment was enacted but before it took effect. The board denied the judge's petition for a redetermination of his pension benefits. Upon the judge's certified question to the court, the court determined that the amendment did not apply to judges who retired after the enactment date but prior to the effective date. Therefore, the court held that the judge was not entitled to increased pension benefits pursuant to 1989 Mont. Laws 664, § 3. The court held that § 3 did not violate the Contracts Clause of the Constitution because pension benefits were to be determined by the latest enactments in effect prior to a recipient's actual retirement. The court ruled that § 3 was not unreasonably or arbitrary in violation of the equal protection clause because it was rationally related to the legitimate governmental interest of providing an incentive for judges to remain in service for a longer period of time.


OUTCOME: The court held that the amendment increasing pension benefits for judges did not apply to judges who retired after the enactment date of the amendment but prior to its effective date.

 

110 of 930 DOCUMENTS


State v. Rothacher


No. 94-369


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


272 Mont. 303; 901 P.2d 82; 1995 Mont. LEXIS 176; 52 Mont. St. Rep. 772


April 27, 1995, Submitted On Briefs  
August 11, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied September 7, 1995.
Released For Publication September 11, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eleventh Judicial District, In and for the County of Flathead, The Honorable Michael H. Keedy, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the District Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged in the District Court for the Eleventh Judicial District in Flathead County (Montana) with deliberate homicide, in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-102. Following a trial by jury, defendant was convicted of mitigated deliberate homicide in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-103. Defendant appealed from his conviction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred when it instructed the jury that it was not necessary for the State to prove that defendant intended to cause the death of the victim. Finding that the instruction was error, though harmless, the court affirmed the conviction. The instruction provided that it would be sufficient to convict defendant if the act, which caused the harm, was done purposely without regard to whether any harm was intended. The court held that deliberate homicide was committed when a person purposely or knowingly caused the death of another human being. A person acted knowingly when there was proof beyond a reasonable doubt that he was aware of the high probability of the result of his conduct. Finally, there was a causal relationship though the result was not within the contemplation of or purpose of defendant, where the same kind of harm or injury was contemplated by him though the precise harm or injury was different or occurred in a different way. To the extent that prior decisions were inconsistent with the opinion, the court overruled them.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction.

 

111 of 930 DOCUMENTS


State v. Bullock


No. 92-536


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


272 Mont. 361; 901 P.2d 61; 1995 Mont. LEXIS 163; 52 Mont. St. Rep. 717


January 17, 1995, Submitted  
August 4, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released For Publication August 22, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Fifth Judicial District, In and for the County of Jefferson, The Honorable Frank M. Davis, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants pleaded guilty to unlawfully killing a game animal in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 87-3-103 and possession of an unlawfully killed game animal in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 87-3-112, after the District Court of the Fifth Judicial District, in and for the County of Jefferson (Montana) denied their motions to dismiss and to suppress evidence. Pursuant to the preservation of their right to do so, defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: A game warden, without a warrant, entered the property of one of the defendants and observed an elk hanging near the cabin. On appeal, defendants alleged that search and subsequent seizure violated article II, § 11 of the Montana Constitution. The state claimed that the game warden's presence on the property and observation of the contraband was constitutionally permissible under the open fields doctrine. The court reversed the district court's ruling on the suppression issue. The court noted that Montana's constitutional right of privacy was broader than that of the federal constitution. The rule that an individual could never have an expectation of privacy in an open field was repugnant to the state's recognition of the right of privacy absent a compelling state interest. The court noted that: (1) defendant's cabin was set back from and not observable from the road; (2) the property was fenced, with "no trespassing" signs posted; and (3) in the past, law officials had requested permission before entering the property. The court found that defendants had a reasonable expectation of privacy in the property. Therefore, their motion to suppress the evidence should have been granted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's denial of defendants' motion to dismiss but held that the district court erred in failing to grant defendants' motion to suppress evidence obtained after officials entered one defendant's property without permission or a warrant and observed the elk carcass.

Christenot v. State


No. 94-539


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


272 Mont. 396; 901 P.2d 545; 1995 Mont. LEXIS 172; 52 Mont. St. Rep. 749


May 25, 1995, Submitted On Briefs  
August 4, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication September 7, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Nineteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Lincoln, The Honorable Robert S. Keller, Judge presiding.
Board of Dentistry v. Kandarian, 248 Mont. 444, 813 P.2d 409, 1991 Mont. LEXIS 135 (Mont., 1991)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant dental board sought review of a grant by the District Court of the Nineteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Lincoln (Montana) of a request by appellees, care providers and patients, for an injunction against the enforcement of Mont. Admin. R. 8.17.808.


OVERVIEW: The board enacted Mont. Admin. R. 8.17.808. The care providers and patients sought to enjoin the enforcement of that Rule and two others. The trial court granted the injunction. The board appealed as to the aforementioned Rule. The court found that it had jurisdiction over the matter, even though a final order had not issued, because it was an order that granted an injunction and was therefore an exception to the final order rule set forth in Mont. The court found that the board's interpretation of the Rule was presumed controlling and that the care providers and patients failed to show compelling indications that the construction was incorrect. The court found that Rule 8.17.808 was not shown to add provisions not envisioned by the legislature. The court reversed the prior grant of injunction as to the Rule.

113 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Mont. Power Co. v. Burlington N. R.R.


No. 94-441


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


272 Mont. 224; 900 P.2d 888; 1995 Mont. LEXIS 143; 52 Mont. St. Rep. 625; 49 A.L.R.5th 951


May 18, 1995, Heard and Submitted  
July 25, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released For Publication August 14, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, In and for the County of Cascade, The Honorable Joel G. Roth, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee power company filed a condemnation action to secure an easement over the land of appellant railroad. The Eighth District Court of Cascade County (Montana) granted a preliminary condemnation order and order of possession of the easement. The railroad challenged the order granting the preliminary condemnation order and order of possession of the easement.


OVERVIEW: On appeal the railroad argued that the district court erred when it found that an easement through its property was necessary for the intended use and subject to condemnation. The court rejected this argument finding that there was substantial need for the new transmission lines in the area in question and that condemnation was proper under Mont. Code Ann. § 70-30-103. The railroad also argued that the district court erred when it found that the power company's transmission lines were a more necessary use under Mont. Code Ann. § 70-30-111(3). Essentially, the railroad claimed that the property in question was already appropriated to public use. The court rejected this argument finding that § 70-30-111(3) was only applicable when there were two public uses of the same property and that said uses were not compatible. The court found that the railroad's use was compatible with the power company's use of the property. Finally, both parties proposed certain conditions for inclusion in the final condemnation order. The court found that further hearings on the subject was warranted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order granting the preliminary condemnation order and order of possession of the easement. The court remanded the action for a further hearing to determine which provisions should be placed in the final order.

Synek v. State Comp. Mut. Ins. Fund


No. 94-471


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


272 Mont. 246; 900 P.2d 884; 1995 Mont. LEXIS 148; 52 Mont. St. Rep. 651


March 9, 1995, Submitted On Briefs  
July 25, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released For Publication September 8, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: The Workers' Compensation Court, State of Montana The Honorable Mike McCarter, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee challenged the decision of the Workers' Compensation Court (Montana), which affirmed the decision of the Montana Department of Labor and Industry, denying her claim for payment for past and continuing chiropractic treatments. The employee filed a petition with the Department, seeking the cost of unpaid chiropractic treatments and a determination with regard to the compensability of future treatments.


OVERVIEW: The employee argued that it was error to find that her chiropractic treatments were not compensable. The court affirmed the judgment, finding no error. The court explained that Mont. Admin. R. 24.29.2003, which contained the chiropractic treatments that were compensable, and Mont. Admin. R. 24.29.2004, which contained the chiropractic treatments that were not compensable, were promulgated pursuant to the broad statutory authority contained in Mont. Code Ann. § 39-41-704(1) (1979) and were not facially or inherently arbitrary, capricious, or unreasonable. The court found that the characterization by the employee's doctor of the employee's condition was that it was worsening, was not expected to become preclinical, and might never reach a stable condition, and her testimony that the treatments were provided "as needed" and for "temporary relief from pain" corresponded to the definition of "maintenance," a noncompensable treatment, which was contained in Mont. Admin. R. § 24.29.2004.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

State v. Brogan


No. 94-116


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


272 Mont. 156; 900 P.2d 284; 1995 Mont. LEXIS 146; 52 Mont. St. Rep. 636


April 27, 1995, Submitted On Briefs  
July 25, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released For Publication August 15, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Sixth Judicial District, In and for the County of Park, The Honorable Richard G. Phillips, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction by the District Court of the Sixth Judicial District, In and for the County of Park (Montana) for the felony offense of possession of unlawfully taken wildlife in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 87-3-118(2) (1991).


OVERVIEW: Defendant owned a wild game farm where he bred and raised elk. An inspection of defendant's farm discovered wild and untagged elk. Defendant was charged with possession of unlawfully taken wildlife, in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 87-3-118(2) (1991). His first trial resulted in a hung jury and the second in conviction. Defendant filed a motion for a new trial, which was denied. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that: (1) a juror's expression of his opinion of the law, which may have been wrong, did not indicate any outside influence on the jury as required to impeach a verdict under Mont. R. Evid. 606(b); (2) the trial court's refusal to instruct the jury on the legal obligations of a game farmer was proper when the jury had been fully and correctly instructed; (3) defendant's prior convictions for failing to maintain his fence were admissible because they were similar to the new charge, not remote in time, and demonstrated an absence of mistake or accident by defendant; and (4) Mont. Code Ann. § 87-3-118(2) was not unconstitutionally vague by virtue of the "purposefully or knowingly" mental state element of the offense or its lack of definition of "constructive possession."


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of possession for unlawfully taken wildlife.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the District Court of the Sixteenth Judicial District, Custer County (Montana), which denied his petition for postconviction relief and writ of habeas corpus. Defendant was sentenced to death for robbery, aggravated kidnapping, and deliberate homicide convictions.

State v. Egelhoff


No. 93-405


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


272 Mont. 114; 900 P.2d 260; 1995 Mont. LEXIS 128; 52 Mont. St. Rep. 548


October 27, 1994, Heard; February 23, 1995, Submitted  
July 6, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Nineteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Lincoln, The Honorable Robert S. Keller, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for a new trial.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted on two counts of deliberate homicide after a jury trial before the District Court of the Nineteenth Judicial District, Lincoln County (Montana). He appealed his conviction and 84-year sentence on the ground that due process was violated by a jury instruction stating that voluntary intoxication was not to be taken into consideration in determining the existence of a mental state which was an element of the crime.


OVERVIEW: Defendant owned a gun and was drinking for most of the day with two companions; that evening they were shot to death in their car, he was found in the back seat, and then behaved erratically when the police took him to the hospital. His blood alcohol level was .36 percent and he claimed to have an alcohol-induced blackout. He was convicted of two deliberate homicides. The trial court's jury instruction used language from Mont. Code Ann. § 45-2-203 and stated that the jury could not consider intoxication a defense unless he proved that he did not know he consumed an intoxicating substance. On appeal, the court reversed and found that the statute impermissibly shifted the burden of proof for an element of a crime from the state to defendant, in violation of Fourteenth Amendment due process. The court held that part of § 45-2-203 was unconstitutional and applied its "new" rule of constitutional law to all cases before it still subject to direct review as of the date of the opinion, but declined to apply it retroactively to cases on collateral review after the date of its opinion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction on two counts of deliberate homicide and remanded for a new trial.

City of Bozeman v. Vaniman


No. 94-625


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


271 Mont. 514; 898 P.2d 1208; 1995 Mont. LEXIS 126; 52 Mont. St. Rep. 543


April 6, 1995, Submitted On Briefs  
June 29, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Gallatin, The Honorable Larry W. Moran, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant condemnees challenged a judgment of the District Court of the Eighteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Gallatin (Montana), which determined that the State had lawfully exercised its power of eminent domain, but that respondent city's chamber of commerce could not be part of a planned highway interchange building complex.


OVERVIEW: The city, on behalf of the state department of transportation, sought to have a portion of the condemnees' property condemned to create an off-ramp and rest area/visitor's center at an interchange. The condemnation complaint did not mention that part of the visitor center was a planned area for the city chamber of commerce. A preliminary condemnation order was issued but, after an appeal, the case was remanded for the trial court's consideration of the chamber's presence and a determination of whether that presence was "de minimus. The court affirmed the trial court's determination that the presence was not de minimus and that the chamber could have no part in locating its offices on any section of the condemned property. The trial court, upon determining that the chamber's presence was not de minimus, correctly then severed the chamber's offices from future plans for the rest area. Moreover, the condemnation was not required to have been negated after severing the chamber's offices. The loss of the chamber's participation did not limit the taking. The same amount of land was required to be taken regardless of the chamber's presence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Carbon County v. Union Reserve Coal Co.


No. 93-426


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


271 Mont. 459; 898 P.2d 680; 1995 Mont. LEXIS 124; 52 Mont. St. Rep. 529


May 16, 1995, Heard and Submitted  
June 27, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing Denied July 27, 1995. Released for Publication August 1, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY: APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Carbon, The Honorable Robert W. Holmstrom, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant coal lessee sought review of a decision from the District Court for the Thirteenth Judicial District, Carbon County (Montana), which entered judgment in favor of the lessee and awarded it nominal damages, but did not award punitive damages in plaintiff lessor's quiet title action. The lessor and intervenor gas lessee also challenged the judgment.


OVERVIEW: The lessor entered into a lease with the coal lessee that gave the coal lessee the rights to all coal on the subject property. Subsequently, the lessor entered into a lease with the gas lessee that gave the gas lessee the rights to oil and gas on the property, including coal seam methane gas. The coal lessee refused to provide the gas lessee with a protective lease. The lessor filed the quiet title action, asserting claims to all rights to minerals excluding coal on the property. The gas lessee intervened in the action, claiming to have rights to the oil and gas in the subject property. The trial court held that the coal seam methane gas in question belonged to the coal lessee and that the gas lessee had committed a trespass by drilling a well to collect the gas. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded, holding that the coal seam gas did not belong to the coal lessee because the gas was not part of the coal and thus could be severed, it was not part of the coal estate, and the coal lessee's lease did not contain any language granting the right to the coal seam gas. The court also held that recent legislative changes did not affect the case.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court that entered judgment in favor of the lessee in the lessor's quite title action.

State Pub. Employee's Ass'n v. Office of the Governor


No. 94-576


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


271 Mont. 450; 898 P.2d 675; 1995 Mont. LEXIS 115; 52 Mont. St. Rep. 523


March 23, 1995, Submitted On Briefs  
June 20, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, The Honorable Jeffrey Sherlock, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant public employees sought review of the decision of the District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark (Montana), which granted the summary judgment motion of respondents, the governor, the department of transportation, and the department of social services, on the employees' complaint seeking a declaratory judgment ordering that a plan for reinstatement of compensation steps be activated.


OVERVIEW: The employees' compensation system incorporated basic pay levels and a series of steps within each level. The employees contended that it had been the practice of state agencies to take away steps when promoting employees more than one grade. The promoted employee did not actually suffer a reduction in compensation, but he would have received less of a pay increase than he would have received without the reduction in steps. The departments issued a written memorandum of a plan to reinstate the employees' steps. Before the lost steps were reinstated, the governor sent a memorandum to the departments prohibiting them from reinstating the steps lost to multi-grade promotions. The employees filed an action seeking declaratory relief. The trial court entered summary judgment in favor of respondents. The court noted that in order for there to have been an unconstitutional impairment of contract, there had to have been a contract in existence. The court held that the employees failed to demonstrate the existence of any contract under which they had a vested right to reinstatement of the lost steps, and therefore, there were no contractual obligation impaired by the governor's actions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

Tefft v. State


No. 94-229


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


271 Mont. 82; 894 P.2d 317; 1995 Mont. LEXIS 83; 52 Mont. St. Rep. 357; 2 Wage & Hour Cas. 2d (BNA) 1329


January 19, 1995, Submitted On Briefs  
May 4, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, In and for the County of Cascade, The Honorable Thomas McKittrick, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the District Court is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant State of Montana (state) appealed a summary judgment from the District Court, Cascade County (Montana), entered in favor of plaintiff firefighters in the firefighters' action to recover wages due from the state and liquidated damages for violation of the federal Fair Labor Standards Act (FLSA).


OVERVIEW: The state paid the firefighters biweekly for 80 hours of work notwithstanding the fact that they actually worked between 96 and 120 hours during a two-week period. When the United States Supreme Court reversed previous case law that excluded state jobs from coverage under the FLSA, the state responded by reducing the firefighters' hourly wages. The court affirmed summary judgment in favor of the firefighters, concluding that the uncontroverted evidence established a violation of the FLSA. The court rejected the state's assertion of an economic necessity defense. The court also rejected the state's contention that the firefighters ratified the wage cuts when they signed agreements that reduced their hourly wage. The court held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion when it awarded liquidated damages under 29 U.S.C.S. § 216(b), finding that the state did not objectively demonstrate good faith or reasonable grounds under 29 U.S.C.S. § 260. Finally, the court concluded that the trial court's damage calculations were not clearly erroneous.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment in favor of the firefighters in their action to recover wages due from the state and liquidated damages for violation of the FLSA.

Siroky v. Richland County


No. 94-508


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


271 Mont. 67; 894 P.2d 309; 1995 Mont. LEXIS 76; 52 Mont. St. Rep. 343


March 23, 1995, Submitted On Briefs  
April 25, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Seventh Judicial District, In and for the County of Richland, The Honorable Richard G. Phillips, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought review of a decision of the District Court of the Seventh Judicial District, Richland County (Montana), which granted summary judgment in favor of appellee trustee, in a case brought by the trustee to recover damages from the county for the conversion of interest earned on a cash bond deposited to satisfy a condition of bail in a criminal action.


OVERVIEW: A cash bond was deposited into the county's trust account to satisfy a condition of bail in a criminal action. A guilty plea was entered, and the individual was sentenced. The bond was exonerated, with half applied toward restitution and half returned to the individual. The individual unsuccessfully submitted a claim to the county for the accrued interest. The trustee then instituted an action for the individual, successfully filing a motion for full summary judgment. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision. The court examined relevant statutes, stating that they did not indicate whether a defendant was entitled to the interest generated by a cash bond during the time of deposit. After an examination of relevant case law, the court found that the state could not transform private property into public property without compensation, even for the limited duration of the deposit of the bond with the county. The court therefore stated that the retention of earned interest on that money would amount to a taking of the individual's property in violation of the Fifth and Fourteenth Amendments of the U.S. Constitution and Mont. Const. art. II., § 29.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's summary judgment decision in favor of the trustee.

State v. Cooney


No. 94-372


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


271 Mont. 42; 894 P.2d 303; 1995 Mont. LEXIS 71; 52 Mont. St. Rep. 320


January 26, 1995, Submitted On Briefs  
April 25, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Third Judicial District, In and for the County of Powell, The Honorable Ted L. Mizner, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the District Court of the Third Judicial District, in and for the County of Powell (Montana), which convicted defendant of misdemeanor stalking in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-220.


OVERVIEW: Defendant repeatedly sent the stalking victim flowers and letters and repeatedly phoned her. A justice court found defendant guilty of misdemeanor stalking. He appealed his conviction to a district court which also found defendant guilty as charged. On appeal the court affirmed, holding that: (1) venue was proper under Mont. Code Ann. § 46-3-112 because the result of the letters and telephone calls which formed the bases of the charge against defendant occurred in the county where prosecution occurred; (2) defendant was not denied his constitutional right to due process based on an impermissible presumption because defendant failed to object at trial and the statute complained of, § 45-5-220(6), did not form any basis for the district court's decision; (3) defendant's conviction was supported by sufficient evidence because defendant's persistence, despite rejection, caused the victim substantial emotional distress; and (4) the stalking statute, Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-220, as applied to defendant did not violate his constitutional right to free speech because free speech does not include the right to cause substantial emotional distress by harassment or intimidation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's judgment which convicted defendant of misdemeanor stalking.

Johnson v. Killingsworth


No. 94-499


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


271 Mont. 1; 894 P.2d 272; 1995 Mont. LEXIS 58; 52 Mont. St. Rep. 274


January 19, 1995, Submitted On Briefs  
April 11, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Fourth Judicial District, In and for the County of Missoula, The Honorable John W. Larson, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant contestee sought review of a decision by the District Court of the Fourth Judicial District, County of Missoula (Montana), which, in an action brought by appellee contestant, voided his election as an irrigation district commissioner.


OVERVIEW: The contestant brought a petition to set aside the election of the contestee as an irrigation district commissioner. The trial court granted the petition on the basis that the contestee did not own land within the district and thus did not satisfy the statutory freeholder requirement of Mont. Code Ann. § 85-7-1501. The contestee sought review and the court affirmed. The court noted that irrigation districts were created, funded, and operated pursuant to statute. Every district was divided into divisions, which were represented by commissioners possessing the qualifications set forth in § 85-7-1501. Pursuant to that section, the court said, a person was not entitled to be a commissioner unless he owned irrigable land within the division. The court found that the contestee did not own land within the division for which he was elected commissioner. Further, the court rejected the contestee's claim that the ownership requirement violated the equal protection clause of the federal constitution. The court said that the statute was subject to review under the standard of whether it was reasonably related to a legitimate state objective. The court said that the statute met the standard.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment that voided the contestee's election as an irrigation district commissioner. The action was initiated by the contestant.
