State v. Stubbs


No. 94-258


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


270 Mont. 364; 892 P.2d 547; 1995 Mont. LEXIS 50; 52 Mont. St. Rep. 232


January 26, 1995, Submitted On Briefs  
March 30, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Nineteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Lincoln, The Honorable Robert S. Keller, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed an order of the District Court of the Nineteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Lincoln (Montana), which suppressed all evidence seized prior to, or as a result of, defendant's arrest. Defendant had been convicted at a bench trial of possession of .05 grams of marijuana, possession of drug paraphernalia, and driving under the influence. On appeal to the district court, defendant filed a motion to suppress.


OVERVIEW: A police officer pulled defendant over after he was observed weaving and speeding. Defendant refused to stop for four miles. When the officer approached the vehicle, he noticed defendant fumbling around on the passenger side of the car. He also saw numerous firearms within defendant's immediate reach, ammunition, and an empty holster. The officer frisked defendant. The officer felt a lump in defendant's pocket and he retrieved a brass pipe. The district court granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence before his trial. The state appealed the order, and the court reversed and remanded for trial. The court held that the officer's actions were within the bounds of a proper stop and frisk. The court noted that it one purpose of a stop and frisk was to protect the officer from deadly weapons, and it was unrealistic to limit the officer's search to a weapon of a specified size. The court held that once the brass pipe was removed, it was in plain view, and the officer had probable cause to conclude that the brass pipe was drug paraphernalia. The court noted that, in the alternative, the search was proper as a search incident to a lawful arrest.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the district court suppressing evidence and remanded for trial.

Moran v. Shotgun Willies


No. 94-361


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


270 Mont. 47; 889 P.2d 1185; 1995 Mont. LEXIS 16


January 12, 1995, Submitted On Briefs  
February 15, 1995, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied March 16, 1995. Released for Publication March 17, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Yellowstone, The Honorable Maurice R. Colberg, Jr., Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employer sought review of a decision by the District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District in and for the County of Yellowstone (Montana), which ordered the state human rights commission to adopt the findings of its hearing commissioner. The hearing commissioner ruled in favor of appellee employee on her employment discrimination claim.


OVERVIEW: The employee brought an action against the employer for employment discrimination. A human rights commission hearing examiner found that the employee had proven her claim by substantial evidence. The human rights commission reversed. The employee sought judicial review and the district court reinstated the hearing examiner's decision. The employer appealed and the court affirmed. The court concluded that the district court applied the proper standard of review in this case. The court noted that the standard of review under the Montana Administrative Procedure Act required the court to determine whether the agency's decision was arbitrary or capricious or characterized by an abuse of discretion. The court further found that it was an abuse of discretion for an agency to modify the findings of a hearing examiner unless the agency first determined from a review of the entire record that the findings of fact were not based on competent substantial evidence. The court found that the hearing examiner's findings were based on competent substantial evidence and, therefore, that the agency abused its discretion when it rejected those findings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a decision that ordered the state human rights commission to reinstate its hearing examiner's ruling in favor of the employee in her action for employment discrimination by the employer.

129 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Wetch v. Unique Concrete Co.


No. 94-176


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


269 Mont. 315; 888 P.2d 425; 1995 Mont. LEXIS 4; 52 Mont. St. Rep. 5


October 28, 1994, Submitted On Briefs  
January 18, 1995, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Sixteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Custer, The Honorable Kenneth R. Wilson, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant contractor sought review from a judgment of the District Court of the Sixteenth Judicial District, in and for the County of Custer (Montana), which held in favor of plaintiff receptionist. The receptionist had filed a complaint against the contractor alleging negligence and seeking special and general damages for injuries caused when she fell into a five to six foot hole where steps had been before their removal by the contractor.


OVERVIEW: The contractor contended that the district court erred in granting the receptionist's motion in limine restricting evidence of negligence attributable to her employer. Finding that the district court's holding was correct, the court affirmed the judgment. After the contractor removed the stairs outside the rear door of the receptionist's place of employment, the employer stated that he would make sure that the door was bolted during business hours and that he would put up a warning sign to prevent use of the rear door. Mont. Code Ann. § 27-1-703 specifically removed from consideration and determination by the fact finder any amount of negligence on the part of an injured person's employer to the extent that such employer had tort immunity under Montana's Workers' Compensation Act. It was undisputed that the employer was immune from tort liability for the receptionist's injuries because she was covered by and received benefits under his workers' compensation insurance. Because the employer was absolutely immune from tort liability injuries, any amount of his negligence that caused or contributed to the receptionist's injuries could not be considered or determined by the jury.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Casarotto v. Lombardi


No. 93-488


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


268 Mont. 369; 886 P.2d 931; 1994 Mont. LEXIS 321; 51 Mont. St. Rep. 1350


April 19, 1994, Submitted:  
December 15, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, In and for the County of Cascade, The Honorable John M. McCarvel, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff franchisees appealed the judgment of the District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, in and for the County of Cascade (Montana), which granted defendant franchisor's motion under § 3 of the Federal Arbitration Act, 9 U.S.C.S. § 1 et seq., to stay further judicial proceedings on the franchisees' breach of contract and tortious conduct claims against the franchisor until arbitration proceedings were completed.


OVERVIEW: The franchisees alleged that the franchisor's award of the franchisees' preferred location to another franchisee breached a verbal agreement giving the franchisee the exclusive right to open a store at that location when it became available, and that the award defrauded the franchisees and breached the covenant of good faith and fair dealing. The franchisor alleged that the franchisees were bound by a choice-of-law provision applying Connecticut law, rather than § 27-5-114(4) of Montana's Uniform Arbitration Act, codified at Mont. Code Ann. § 27-5-114(4), which required that the contract contain notice that it was subject to arbitration on the first page in order to be valid. The appellate court held that (1) Montana law applied where it would have been applicable absent an effective choice of law by the parties and application of Connecticut law would have been contrary to the fundamental policy of Montana by eliminating the statutory notice requirement, and (2) Montana's notice requirement was not preempted by § 2 of the Federal Arbitration Act (act), 9 U.S.C.S. § 1 et seq., where the notice requirement did not undermine the act's goals and policies.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the trial court's order staying further judicial proceedings until arbitration was completed and remanded for further proceedings.

 

131 of 930 DOCUMENTS


BROCKIE v. OMO CONSTR., INC.


No. 93-631


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


268 Mont. 519; 887 P.2d 167; 1994 Mont. LEXIS 291


June 23, 1994, Submitted On Briefs  
December 13, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied January 18, 1995. Released for Publication January 24, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, The Honorable Jeffrey Sherlock, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: We reverse the jury verdict finding zero survivorship damages and remand for a new trial limited to the issue of survivorship damages.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, decedent's personal representative, sought review of a order of the District Court of the First Judicial District, Lewis and Clark County (Montana), which denied the personal representative's motion for a partial new trial limited to the question of survivorship damages in an action against respondent tortfeasor.


OVERVIEW: The decedent was killed in an automobile accident as a passenger in a vehicle that collided with a construction sign owned by the tortfeasor. The jury returned a special verdict finding the tortfeasor 35 percent negligent and that the non-party driver was 65 percent negligent for proximately causing the decedent's injuries and death. The jury awarded wrongful death damages to the decedent's estate but adjusted that amount to reflect the 35 percent negligence attributable to the tortfeasor. The jury also awarded the decedent's estate zero dollars in survivorship damages. The personal representative filed a motion for a partial new trial on the issue of survivorship damages, which the district court denied. On appeal, the court reversed the jury's survivorship damages verdict and, on remand, directed reinstatement of the full wrongful death award. The evidence was either stipulated to or offered without contradiction. Thus, the jury was compelled to award survivorship damages for the decedent's personal injuries. The court also found that, because there was insufficient evidence to justify the jury's survivor damages verdict, a partial new trial should have been granted.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the jury verdict finding zero survivorship damages in the personal representative's wrongful death action against the tortfeasor. The court remanded the case for a new trial limited to the issue of survivorship damages. On remand, the court directed the district court to reinstate the full amount of the wrongful death award and to offset that amount dollar-for-dollar by the pretrial settlement with the non-party driver.

Buhr ex rel. Lloyd v. Flathead County


No. 92-061


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


268 Mont. 223; 886 P.2d 381; 1994 Mont. LEXIS 284; 51 Mont. St. Rep. 1258

December 8, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eleventh Judicial District, In and for the County of Flathead, The Honorable Leif B. Erickson, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff personal representative brought an action against defendants, county, sheriff, hospital, and mental health center, claiming negligence for decedent's personal injuries and death and for deprivation of his civil rights. The personal representative appealed from a judgment of the District Court of the Eleventh Judicial District (Montana) for all defendants and from an order that denied the representative's motion for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: The personal representative claimed that the district court erred in instructing the jury on negligence per se, on statutory duties, and on the full theory of his claims. The representative also claimed that the district court erred in directing a verdict on the civil rights claims and that the jury's verdicts were not supported by substantial evidence. The court affirmed the district court's judgment for defendants and order denying a new trial, as the jury instructions were proper, the representative failed to prove deliberate indifference and substantial evidence supported the jury's verdicts. The court found that the direct statutory quotes in the instructions were not erroneous and that under the facts the legal duties under Mont. Code Ann. § 53-21-146 were not applicable. The court concluded that the representative could not establish deliberate indifference to the decedent's need for medical attention or to an alleged use of excessive force against the decedent while detained. As conflicting testimony was presented and the jury was free to accept or reject the testimony, substantial credible evidence existed for the verdicts.


OUTCOME: Th court affirmed the district court's judgment for defendants in the personal representative's action for the decedent's personal injuries, wrongful death, and deprivation of civil rights and its order denying the representative a new trial.

McKamey v. State


No. 94-180


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


268 Mont. 137; 885 P.2d 515; 1994 Mont. LEXIS 273; 51 Mont. St. Rep. 1218; 2 Wage & Hour Cas. 2d (BNA) 879


October 13, 1994, Submitted On Briefs  
November 29, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighth Judicial District, In and for the County of Cascade, The Honorable Joel G. Roth, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state sought review of a judgment of the District Court for the Eighth Judicial District, Cascade County (Montana), which declared unconstitutional a requirement that firefighters employed by the state at the air national guard base to be members of the air national guard. Appellee firefighter cross-appealed for denial of attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: The sate military service initiated a policy requiring its firefighters to be members of the air national guard to benefit from a wage requirement exemption. The firefighter sought a declaratory, injunctive, and equitable relief declaring the military service requirement to be discriminatory and violative of his employment rights and others similarly situated. The trial court granted the firefighter's motion for summary judgment, enjoined the state from using military service requirement in any manner relative to the firefighter or employees similarly situated, and ordered each party to its own attorney fees. The state argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred in finding that the military service requirement was unconstitutional, while the firefighter argued that it erred for denying him attorney fees. The court affirmed the trial court's decision, holding that the state failed to offer any compelling evidence that the military service was rationally related to a legitimate government interest and that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying the firefighter attorney fees request because the state's defense was not frivolous or pursued in bad faith.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision that granted a summary judgment in favor of the firefighter and denied the firefighter's request for attorney fees.

 

134 of 930 DOCUMENTS



State v. Moore


No. 93-369


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


268 Mont. 20; 885 P.2d 457; 1994 Mont. LEXIS 260; 51 Mont. St. Rep. 1151


September 1, 1994, Heard; September 13, 1994, Submitted  
November 22, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Gallatin, The Honorable Larry W. Moran, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction for murder issued by the District Court of the Eighteenth Judicial District in and for the County of Gallatin (Montana). Defendant challenged the introduction of DNA evidence after statistical evidence related to DNA testing was excluded. Defendant also contended, among others, that the trial court erred in denying defendant's motion to suppress a statement made to police while being transported in a patrol car.


OVERVIEW: Defendant called the victim to give him a ride. The victim was not seen again. The police obtained a search warrant for defendant's pickup and camper. When confronted with the evidence, defendant changed his story. Police found tissue in the camper, which was determined to be brain and muscle tissue. DNA testing showed that the tissue was most likely from the father of the victim's children. Police interviewed defendant, and the defendant waived his Miranda rights. Defendant admitted he had a gun and offered to get it. A police officer that was a friend of defendant drove defendant to his house. The officer stated that he did not want defendant to do anything to himself, to which defendant responded that if he did something to himself they would never find the victim. The trial court denied defendant's motion to suppress the statement. The trial court convicted defendant, and the court affirmed. The court held that the DNA evidence was not dependent upon the statistics that the trial court suppressed. The court found that the officer did not interrogate defendant and that the officer's statement of worry was not likely to elicit an incriminating response.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's murder conviction. The court held that DNA evidence was properly admitted and was not dependent on the DNA statistics that the trial court suppressed. The court found that a statement defendant made to an officer was not a result of custodial interrogation because the officer's expression regarding defendant's well being was not likely to elicit an incriminating response.


 

135 of 930 DOCUMENTS


State v. Folda


No. 94-144


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


267 Mont. 523; 885 P.2d 426; 1994 Mont. LEXIS 265; 51 Mont. St. Rep. 1149


October 28, 1994, Submitted On Briefs  
November 21, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Yellowstone, The Honorable Robert W. Holmstrom, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Thirteenth Judicial District Court of Yellowstone County (Montana), which, in an opinion stemming from consolidated cases, affirmed defendants' convictions in city court and justice court for operating a motor vehicle without liability insurance, operating an unregistered motor vehicle, and not wearing a seatbelt.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted in justice court and in city court of operating a motor vehicle without liability insurance, of operating an unregistered motor vehicle, and of not wearing a seat belt. A district court upheld these convictions. On further appeal, the court affirmed, rejecting defendant's contention that because he was a "free" man who was not a "Fourteenth" Amendment citizen he did not have to obey state or federal statutes concerning vehicle registration, wearing a seatbelt, or maintaining liability insurance. The court explained that state governments had the inherent power to enact reasonable regulations concerning the health, safety, welfare, and morals of the public. Such reasonable regulations include requirements for registration, insurance, and seatbelt usage, the court said. Recognizing that the statutes defendant violated were regulatory in nature, the court said that no person in the state was exempt from regulatory statutes, even if he claimed he was a "free" man who did not have to obey state or federal law. Given defendant's failure to show that the statutes were unreasonable, his constitutional rights were not violated by requiring his adherence to them.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's multiple count convictions for operating a motor vehicle without liability insurance, for operating an unregistered motor vehicle, and for not wearing a seat belt.

State v. Hildreth


No. 94-025


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


267 Mont. 423; 884 P.2d 771; 1994 Mont. LEXIS 244; 51 Mont. St. Rep. 1086


July 6, 1994, Submitted On Briefs  
November 1, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied December 8, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Fifth Judicial District, In and for the County of Beaverhead, The Honorable Frank M. Davis, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the District Court of the Fifth Judicial District, In and for the County of Beaverhead (Montana), which convicted him of sexual assault, a felony, in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-502, sentenced him to six years in prison, and denied his motion for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant began tutoring his 15-year old female cousin. The sessions were held in the evenings at the college that defendant attended. After one of the sessions, defendant grabbed the victim's arm, pushed her to the floor face down, he removed her bra while sitting on her back, pulled of her pants, and ejaculated on her buttocks. The parents of the victim learned of the incident when they found letters in the victim's room describing what had happened. At trial, the victim also described similar sexual assaults. Defendant argued that the prosecution failed to give him notice of its intent to use other incidents and that he was not allowed to examine the victim about her sexual conduct. The court affirmed the conviction. The court held that: (1) defendant had notice of the State's intention to use other bad acts as evidence; (2) defendant could only cross-examine the victim where there was evidence of prior accusations that had been adjudicated false, and those facts were not present in the case; (3) defendant did not prove that his trial counsel was ineffective; and (4) the trial errors were not prejudicial to defendant's case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

In re Marriage of K.E.V.


No. 93-427


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


267 Mont. 323; 883 P.2d 1246; 1994 Mont. LEXIS 237; 51 Mont. St. Rep. 1055


June 28, 1994, Submitted; May 19, 1994, Heard  
October 25, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Second Judicial District, In and for the County of Silver Bow, The Honorable Thomas C. Honzel, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner husband and respondent wife sought review of a judgment of the District Court of the Second Judicial District, In and for the County of Silver Bow (Montana), that held that the wife was prevented by the doctrine of equitable estoppel from denying the existence of the father and child relationship between the husband and their child, but the wife was not time-barred from asserting that the husband was not the father.


OVERVIEW: The wife and the husband were involved in a common law marriage. The wife informed the husband that he was the father of the child. Due to a mix-up at the county registrar's office, the husband was not listed as the father on the child's birth certificate. The court held that the wife's claim seeking to declare the nonexistence of the father-child relationship was not time-barred after five years under Mont. Code Ann. § 40-6-108(1)(b) (1983), which was the statute of limitations in effect at the time of the child's birth, because the statute had been held unconstitutional. The court also found that it was appropriate to apply the doctrine of equitable estoppel to the case because the wife told the husband that he was the father of the child, the husband relied on that statement, and it would be both unconscionable and inequitable to allow the wife to claim that the husband was not the father in order to advance her own self-serving interests.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

State v. Ray


No. 93-256


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


267 Mont. 128; 882 P.2d 1013; 1994 Mont. LEXIS 217; 51 Mont. St. Rep. 968


April 14, 1994, Submitted On Briefs  
October 6, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied with Amendment to Opinion October 20, 1994. Released for Publication October 20, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Fourth Judicial District, In and for the County of Missoula, The Honorable Edward McLean, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the District Court, in the Fourth Judicial District, Missoula County (Montana), which, upon a second trial, convicted him of two counts of solicitation of sexual assault.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that the district court abused its discretion when it admitted testimony of prior acts, that the evidence presented was insufficient to convict him of solicitation of sexual assault, that a jury instruction on solicitation given was improper, that the district court erred in admitting the testimony of two expert witnesses who were not identified as experts prior to trial, and that the cumulative error committed required the conviction to be reversed. The court reversed the conviction on the grounds that the admission of prior acts that occurred approximately 16 to 18 years before the charged conduct was prejudicial error as the prior acts were too remote in time. The only error found was the admission of the prior acts evidence. The evidence used to prove solicitation of sexual assault was legally sufficient but whether such evidence was sufficient to sustain a conviction upon retrial was solely within the province of the finder of fact upon a new trial. While the State erred in not disclosing the status of the experts and in failing to provide their statements, there was no prejudice given defendant knew their identity for over eight months before trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for two counts of solicitation of sexual assault and remanded the case for a new trial.

Newville v. Department of Family Servs.


No. 92-310


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


267 Mont. 237; 883 P.2d 793; 1994 Mont. LEXIS 174; 51 Mont. St. Rep. 758


October 14, 1993, Heard; March 16, 1994, Submitted  
August 29, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied November 17, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Gallatin, The Honorable John Warner, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff co-guardians of a foster child appealed an order from the District Court of the Eighteenth Judicial District, Gallatinin County (Montana) that granted judgment in their favor in their negligence suit against defendant social services department. The department also appealed. Plaintiff claimed that they were entitled to a new trial because a negligence allocation statute, Mont. Code Ann. § 27-1-703, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Co-guardians ad litem brought suit on behalf of a foster child against the child's former foster parents, the foster parents' counselor, a state employee, and a state social services department for injuries the child received while in foster care. Due to various settlements, the trial proceeded against the department alone. The jury found for the guardians and allocated negligence between the department and the nonparties under Mont. Code Ann. § 27-1-703. The guardians sought a new trial solely against the department claiming that the statute was unconstitutional because it arbitrarily prejudiced plaintiffs by requiring them to exonerate nonparties. The department also appealed. Because the guardians would suffer an economic loss if a percentage of negligence were attributed to unrepresented parties, they had standing to assert the rights of the unrepresented parties. The appellate court ruled that the statute, in part, violated substantive due process because it unreasonably mandated an allocation of percentages of negligence to nonparties without any kind of procedural safeguard. Because the statute was severable, the entire statute was not unconstitutional. Retrial was ordered.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the trial court's judgment for the guardians and remanded for a new trial. On retrial, the trier of fact could consider the negligence of the following parties to the action: claimant, injured person, defendant and third party defendants.

Nicholson v. Cooney


No. 93-657


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


265 Mont. 406; 877 P.2d 486; 1994 Mont. LEXIS 139; 51 Mont. St. Rep. 579


May 24, 1994, Submitted  
June 30, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Counsel Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark The Honorable Thomas C. Honzel, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state teachers and employees sought review of the decision from the District Court of the First Judicial District, in and for the County of Lewis and Clark (Montana), which granted summary judgment in favor of defendant citizens groups. The state teachers and the employees sought to have declared unconstitutional a referendum and suspension of an enactment that raised taxes pending the outcome of the referendum.


OVERVIEW: The legislature enacted 1993 Mont. Laws 634 (chapter 634), revising state income and corporate tax laws. The citizen groups gathered petitions with sufficient signatures to put chapter 634 on the ballot and to suspend it until the vote on the referendum occurred. The state teachers and employees brought an action to declare the referendum and suspension unconstitutional. The district court granted summary judgment in favor of the citizens groups. On appeal, the court affirmed the district court's decision. The court held that: (1) state teachers and employees' claims were not time-barred under Mont. Code Ann. § 3-302(6)(a) where there was an equitable tolling of the statute of limitations; (2) suspension of chapter 634 did not deny the state teachers and employees equal protection in light of the fact that a majority of voters would decide the fate of chapter 634 at the ballot box; (3) the suspension did not unconstitutionally place the state's budget out of balance since Mont. Const. art. VIII, § 9, requiring a balanced budget, was a restriction on the legislature not the people; and (4) a referendum vote was not prohibited where chapter 634 was not an appropriations measure.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's decision, which granted summary judgment in favor of the citizens groups, finding that a referendum submitting a tax enactment to vote and a suspension of the enactment pending the election was not unconstitutional.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of a decision by the District Court of the Eighteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Gallatin (Montana), which found Mont. Code Ann. § 87-3-142 to be unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: A defendant was convicted under Mont. Code Ann. § 87-3-142 for interfering with an individual engaged in the legal act of hunting bison. The court also found that the statute was not vague because it was reasonably clear in its application to the conduct of the defendant who stood in front of the hunter as the hunter attempted to fire his weapon. The court reversed the finding of the trial court that the statute was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court found the statute at issue was not unconstitutionally overbroad or vague.

Carbon County v. Dain Bosworth, Inc.


No. 93-145


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


265 Mont. 75; 874 P.2d 718; 1994 Mont. LEXIS 104; 51 Mont. St. Rep. 436


April 18, 1994, Submitted  
May 16, 1994, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, The Honorable Thomas C. Honzel, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: We reverse the District Court's order for summary judgment and remand for further proceedings in accordance with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, underwriters and bondholders, sought review from an order of the First Judicial District Court, Lewis and Clark County (Montana), which granted summary judgment to respondents, county commissioners, finding that the county had no further obligation to make loans from the revolving fund to the rural special improvement district (RSID) fund when the RSID assessments were insufficient to pay the RSID bonds.


OVERVIEW: The county created RSID funds to assist in the development of a subdivision and agreed to issue bonds to the public to finance the improvements and to levy and collect assessments in the principal amount of the bonds against property within the RSIDs. The county created a revolving fund and agreed to authorize loans from the revolving fund to the RSID fund when assessments were deficient to pay the bond payments. The RSID property did not sell as expected, and assessments became delinquent, resulting in inadequate revenue to pay the bonds. The county filed an action for a declaratory judgment to define the county's obligations, and a bankruptcy court stated that the revolving fund was voluntary. A district court denied the underwriters' and bondholders' motion that challenged the evidence and granted the county summary judgment. On appeal, the court reversed and held that the county's obligation to make loans from the revolving fund and the obligation to levy taxes to fund the revolving fund was not an unconstitutional pledge of credit. The court held that the county's agreement to make loans from the revolving fund was mandatory and not discretionary.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the district court's order for summary judgment in favor of the county, in an action against the county by the underwriters and bondholders to determine the county's obligations regarding a revolving fund for RSIDs, and remanded the case for further proceedings.

Mead v. M.S.B., Inc.


No. 93-322


Supreme Court of Montana


264 Mont. 465; 872 P.2d 782; 1994 Mont. LEXIS 86; 51 Mont. St. Rep. 348


February 1, 1994, Submitted  
April 12, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication May 2, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the District Court of Missoula County. Fourth Judicial District. Honorable Ed McLean, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant injured skier sought review of the judgment of the District Court of Missoula County (Montana), which granted the motion for summary judgment filed by appellee ski area operator. The skier argued that the trial court erred in concluding as a matter of law that the skier's injury resulted from the inherent risks of skiing as set forth in Mont. Code Ann. § 23-2-736 (1989) of the Montana Skier Responsibility Act (Act).


OVERVIEW: The skier was injured by a rocky outcropping on the ski trail bank striking him on the knee. The operator argued that the skier was injured by a condition that presented an inherent risk of skiing, for which the operator was relieved of liability by the Act. The trial court dismissed the skier's action, finding that it was the type of claim which the Act was meant to prevent. The trial court, however, failed to specify which provision of the Act barred the skier's claim. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded for trial. The court rejected the operator's contention that its duties were limited to those listed in the Act, holding that the operator also had a duty of care. The court found that whether the rocky outcropping was a condition enumerated in the Act had not been established as a matter of law, and therefore whether the skier was injured by a condition listed in the Act was a question of fact that could not be resolved on summary judgment. The court rejected the operator's argument that the skier had assumed the risk of injury. The court held that assumption of the risk, except as specified in the Act, was not a separate defense.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's summary dismissal of the skier's action against the ski area operator. The court remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with its opinion that whether the skier was injured by a condition that presented an inherent risk of skiing enumerated in the Act was a question of fact that could not be resolved on summary judgment.

Monroe v. State


No. 93-403


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


265 Mont. 1; 873 P.2d 230; 1994 Mont. LEXIS 76; 51 Mont. St. Rep. 327


February 16, 1994, Submitted  
March 30, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied May 26, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, The Honorable Thomas C. Honzel, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff applicants appealed from a judgment of the District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark (Montana), which upheld the constitutionality of Mont. Code Ann. § 87-2-102(2)(1989), concerning its definition of "resident" for obtaining fishing, hunting, and trapping licenses in the applicants' declaratory judgment action against defendant State of Montana.


OVERVIEW: The state filed misdemeanor charges against the applicants, alleging that they made false statements in obtaining resident hunting licenses in violation of § 87-2-205. The applicants filed a motion to dismiss the charges, which the justice court denied. The applicants then filed a declaratory judgment action in the district court, arguing that § 87-2-102(2) was unconstitutionally vague. The district court entered judgment in the state's favor, finding that § 87-2-205 was constitutional. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the applicants' facial challenge to the constitutionality of § 87-2-102(2) failed because at least some license applicants could have determined whether they qualified as "residents." Section 87-2-102(2) required that, for a period of six months immediately preceding the application for a resident license to fish, hunt, or trap in Montana, the applicant was required to have been a resident as defined in Mont. Code Ann. § 1-1-215. Thus, a person who came into Montana on the day before hunting season on his or her first trip to Montana, intending to stay for only a week or two of hunting, would have known that he or she did not qualify as a "resident."


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision upholding the constitutionality of the statute defining "resident" for the purpose of obtaining a Montana fishing, hunting, or trapping license in the applicants' declaratory judgment action against the state.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant high school district sought review of an order of the district Court of Big Horn County, Thirteenth Judicial District (Montana), which distributed coal tax revenues from mines within an elementary school district to respondent high school district.

148 of 930 DOCUMENTS


State v. Brandon


No. 93-293


Supreme Court of Montana


264 Mont. 231; 870 P.2d 734; 1994 Mont. LEXIS 58; 51 Mont. St. Rep. 244


January 7, 1994, Submitted on Briefs  
March 22, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied April 14, 1994. Released for Publication April 18, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the District Court of Park County. Sixth Judicial District. Honorable Dale Cox, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his jury conviction for conspiracy to commit deliberate homicide against his wife entered in the District Court of Park County (Montana). Defendant alleged that the trial court erred in refusing his jury instructions on solicitation with entrapment and in denying his motion to dismiss and acquit on the grounds of entrapment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and his wife decided to divorce. Upset about the pending divorce and potential financial difficulties, defendant asked a friend to kill his wife. After speaking with the county sheriff, the friend decided to wear a wire. During defendant's next few meetings with the friend, defendant provided him with a gun, gave him detailed instructions on how the killing should occur, and promised to pay. Defendant was convicted of conspiracy to commit deliberate homicide against his wife. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the trial court did not err by refusing defendant's proposed jury instructions on solicitation and entrapment, because the jury was properly instructed as to the definition of "purposely," the mental state element of solicitation, and because inclusion of a phrase in the jury's entrapment instruction that wrongly allowed it to reject the entrapment defense was harmless error in view of additional language in the instruction. Denial of defendant's motion to dismiss and acquit on the grounds of entrapment was proper because based on the evidence defendant was not lured or induced into committing the crime, but rather developed the scheme himself.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for conspiracy to commit deliberate homicide against his wife.

State v. Mummey


No. 93-165


Supreme Court of Montana


264 Mont. 272; 871 P.2d 868; 1994 Mont. LEXIS 54; 51 Mont. St. Rep. 198


December 2, 1993, Submitted on Briefs  
March 17, 1994, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication May 4, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the District Court of Blaine County. Seventeenth Judicial District. Honorable Leonard H. Langen, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the orders of the Blaine County District Court (Montana), which denied his motions for directed verdict and for judgment not withstanding the verdict after his conviction for felony assault.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was tried for the crimes of aggravated assault and felony assault with a weapon. At the close of trial, defendant moved for a directed verdict, which was denied. Defendant was convicted of felony assault and defendant moved for judgment not withstanding the verdict, which was denied Defendant appealed on the issues of whether a tennis shoe was a weapon under the assault statute and whether the language in the information was properly included in the jury instructions. The court held that whether a tennis shoe was a weapon was a fact for the jury and that the trial court did not err in finding defendant guilty on the felony assault charge. The court also held that an information was merely to inform defendant of the charges against him or her and that incorporating the language in the information in the jury instructions was not error where the language contained the statutory wording. The court affirmed the conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction of defendant by the trial court for felony assault.

 

150 of 930 DOCUMENTS


SJL Assocs. Ltd. Partnership v. City of Billings


No. 92-449


Supreme Court of Montana


263 Mont. 142; 867 P.2d 1084; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 408; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 1726; 22 Media L. Rep. 1758


February 23, 1993, Submitted on  
December 28, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied March 1, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the District Court of Yellowstone County. Thirteenth Judicial District. Honorable Russell K. Fillner, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a judgment of the District Court of Yellowstone County, Thirteenth Judicial District (Montana), which declared that appellee station had a constitutional right to attend meetings between certain city employees and a contractor. The station cross-appealed the denial of attorney fees and questioned whether the station's request for an injunction was moot.


OVERVIEW: When the city denied the reporter access to a meeting of city agency employees and the contractor, the station petitioned for declaratory judgment and injunctive relief. The district court determined that the station was entitled to a judgment that it had a constitutional right to attend meetings between the employees and the contractor. On appeal, the court noted that the meeting was not one of a public or governmental body, board, bureau or commission. Thus, the only manner in which the meeting could have fit within the parameters of Montana's open meeting statute, Mont. Code Ann. § 2-3-203, was if it could be properly construed as a meeting of a public agency. However, the city agency employees were not authorized to make rules, determine contested cases, or enter into contracts, and the open meeting statutes thus did not require the meeting to be open to the public The statutory definition of "agency" was not restrictive of the public's right to observe deliberations of public bodies and agencies contained in Mont. Const. Art. II, § 9. The legislative history established that there was no intent to make "public agencies" synonymous with individual public employees.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the declaratory judgment. In view of that resolution, it did not address the issues raised in the station's cross-appeal.

 

151 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Arneson v. State by & Through its Dep't of Admin., Teachers' Retirement Div.


No. 92-301


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


262 Mont. 269; 864 P.2d 1245; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 368; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 1546


August 16, 1993, Submitted  
December 7, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication December 30, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, The Honorable Jeffrey M. Sherlock, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, Teachers' Retirement Division of the State of Montana Department of Administration and Teachers' Retirement Board, challenged the judgment of the District Court of the First Judicial District, Lewis and Clark County (Montana), concluding that Mont. Code Ann. § 19-4-711 was unconstitutional to the extent that it employed an unreasonable classification in violation of the Equal Protection Clause of Mont. Const. art. II, § 4.


OVERVIEW: Respondent beneficiary received retirement benefits through her deceased mother. She challenged § 19-4-711 because it provided for an increase in retirement benefits for retirees or their beneficiaries over age 55 or, irrespective of age, all those receiving disability or survivorship benefits. The beneficiary did not fall under the classification entitling her to the increase because she was classified as a beneficiary, due to the fact that her mother had retired and was a beneficiary at the time of her death, and because she was under age 55. The court affirmed the trial court's finding of unconstitutionality. The trial court correctly applied the rational basis test of review. Strict scrutiny was not required because the beneficiary was not a member of a suspect class nor was a fundamental right involved. Middle tier scrutiny was not applicable to an "age plus" classification absent a constitutionally based benefit. The classification did not rationally relate to a legitimate state purpose because the purpose of the law, to grant beneficiaries, retirees' survivors, and disabled an amount to alleviate the effect of inflation, was applicable to others who were excluded.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment finding the classification unconstitutional.

Buckley v. Wordal


No. 93-183


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


262 Mont. 306; 865 P.2d 240; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 376; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 1570


October 28, 1993, Submitted on Briefs  
December 7, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication December 28, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, The Honorable James E. Purcell Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county and commissioners challenged a decision from the District Court of Lewis and Clark County (Montana), which ordered them to delete or refund rural improvement district assessments and re-notice the hearings which initiated the rural improvement district process.


OVERVIEW: Landowners in an area of the county requested that the county create a rural improvement district to improve the road system in the affected area. The county and commissioners initiated the program for the creation of the rural improvement district set forth in Mont. Code Ann. § 7-12-2101 et seq. After the creation of the rural improvement district the landowners initiated an action that sought to enjoin the county and commissioners from continuing with the rural improvement district. The trial court denied injunctive relief but directed the county and commissioners to re-notice the hearings and allow the landowners to participate further in the process because it determined that the action of the county and commissioners in adjourning the hearings and rescheduling hearings without the opportunity to protest denied the landowners their right to participate. The court reversed the decision from the trial court because it determined that the county and commissioners had followed the requirements for the formation of the rural improvement district and there was no requirement that the landowners be afforded opportunities other than the initial hearing to participate in the process.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision from the trial court that ordered the county and commissioners to delete or refund rural improvement district assessments and re-notices the hearings that initiated the rural improvement district process.

State v. Bradley


No. 93-227


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


262 Mont. 194; 864 P.2d 787; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 364; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 1511


October 21, 1993, Submitted on Briefs  
November 30, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied December 22, 1993. Released for Publication December 27, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Seventeenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Blaine, The Honorable Leonard Langen, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction in the District Court of the Seventeenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Blaine (Montana), for negligent homicide, negligent vehicular assault, and failure to wear a seat belt.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of negligent homicide, negligent vehicular assault, and failure to wear a seat belt. On appeal, the court affirmed. Defendant was not subject to ineffective assistance of counsel. Lay witnesses were competent to give opinion testimony as to defendant's intoxication, and there was thus no basis for counsel to object to the testimony. Counsel's performance was not deficient for failure to object to introduction of a lab report. The blood test report from the state crime laboratory was not excluded by the hearsay rule because the state complied with the notification requirements of Mont. R. Evid. 803(8). Defendant failed to make any showing that the pool of prospective jurors contained an inadequate representation of Native Americans. Thus, he failed to show that his right to a jury drawn from a cross-section of his community was violated or that discrimination based on race occurred.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for negligent homicide, negligent vehicular assault, and failure to wear a seat belt.

Cosgriffe v. Cosgriffe


No. 93-075


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


262 Mont. 175; 864 P.2d 776; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 359; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 1501


September 16, 1993, Submitted  
November 24, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication December 15, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Yellowstone, The Hon. Maurice R. Colberg, Jr., Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant daughter sought review of the order of the District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Yellowstone (Montana), which granted summary judgment to respondent father in the daughter's action against the father for damages incurred as the result of sexual abuse.


OVERVIEW: The daughter filed a claim against the father when she was 41 years old based on alleged instances of sexual abuse by the father when she was between the ages of 16 and 19. The trial court granted the father's motion for summary judgment. The trial court ruled that the statute of limitations for torts based on sexual abuse, Mont. Code Ann. § 27-2-216, which extended the statute of limitations to three years from when a plaintiff knew or should have known of the injury, was unconstitutional. The daughter appealed. The court reversed the decision of the trial court. The court held that the statute did not deprive the father of due process under the Fourteenth Amendment because its retroactive application did not divest him of any vested interest. The court further held that the statute did not violate Mont. Const. art. II, § 17 because the statute was reasonably related to a legitimate purpose of the state and because the legislature had the power to extend statutes of limitation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's award of summary judgment to the father in the daughter's action for damages based on sexual abuse and remanded the cause for trial.

Sheehy v. Public Employees Retirement Div.


No. 92-499


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


262 Mont. 129; 864 P.2d 762; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 358; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 1477


April 28, 1993, Heard on; July 27, 1993, Submitted on  
November 23, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied December 23, 1993. Released for Publication December 27, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, The Honorable Thomas C. Honzel, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers and intervenor retiree association challenged the judgment of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark (Montana), which granted summary judgment to respondent state in the taxpayers' declaratory judgment action challenging the validity of 1991 Mont. Laws ch. 823, which brought all income, including state and federal pensions, within the Montana income tax.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers contended that ch. 823 impaired private contractual rights that exempted their pensions from state taxation in violation of Mont. Const. art. II, § 35; that a provision phasing out an exemption violated 4 U.S.C.S. § 111; and that the retirement adjustment payment of 1991 Mont. Laws ch. 823 violated 4 U.S.C.S. § 111. The trial court granted the state summary judgment. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in part and reversed it in part. The court held that state employees who retired prior to the effective date of 1991 Mont. Laws ch. 823 did not have a contractual right to continued exemption from state taxation because the statute the retirees relied upon contained no manifestation of legislative intent to create private and enforceable contract rights. The provision of ch. 823 phasing out the exemption did not violate 4 U.S.C.S. § 111 because it did not discriminate as to source of income; however, an adjustment payment to state retirees did violate § 111 because it constituted discriminatory taxation based solely on the source of their retirement income. The court concluded that the invalid provisions of 1991 Mont. Laws ch. 823 could be severed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment to the state in the taxpayers' declaratory judgment action as to the trial court's findings that the challenged legislation did not impair private contractual rights and that a provision phasing out a tax exemption did not violate federal law. The court reversed the finding that a retirement adjustment payment did not violate federal law, but the court found that the provision was severable.

State ex rel. Nelson v. Montana Ninth Judicial Dist. Court


No. 92-512


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


262 Mont. 70; 863 P.2d 1027; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 354; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 1447


September 3, 1993, Submitted on  
November 18, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication January 4, 1994.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought relief from an order of the trial court (Montana), which held that defendant had a right to waive a jury trial, contending that the trial court erred in determining that the state did not have the right to demand a trial court jury.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with felony assault in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-202(2)(c), obstructing a peace officer in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 45-7-302(1), and resisting arrest in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 45-7-301(1)(a). Prior to trial, defendant formally waived his right to a jury trial, but the state demanded a jury trial of all factual issues. The trial court concluded that Mont. Code Ann. § 46-16-110(3) permitted defendant to waive his right to trial by jury and did not violate Mont. Const. art. II, § 26. The trial court denied the state's demand for a jury trial. The State petitioned the court to review the trial court's decision. The court held that the Mont. Const. art. II, § 26 provided that a jury trial was guaranteed in all criminal cases unless waived in the manner provided by law, and the legislature was free to provide the procedure for waiver of trial by jury in criminal cases, and had done so in Mont. Code Ann. § 46-16-110(3). The court concluded that § 46-16-110(3) did not require approval by the state, nor the trial court, and did not violate the Montana Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court which allowed defendant to waive his right to a jury trial.

Montana Dep't of Revenue v. Sheehy


No. 93-193


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


262 Mont. 104; 862 P.2d 1181; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 353; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 1464


October 5, 1993, Submitted on  
November 18, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected November 19, 1993. As Corrected November 23, 1993. Released for Publication December 10, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, The Honorable Jeffrey Sherlock, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Department of Revenue (DOR), sought review of an order of the First Judicial District Court of Lewis and Clark County (Montana), which affirmed a decision of the State Tax Appeal Board (STAB) upholding respondent homeowners' challenge of the assessed value of their home.


OVERVIEW: A district court affirmed a STAB decision that DOR's use of a stratified sales assessment ratio study to determine a value multiplier, as applied to respondents' home, caused a disparity of treatment of taxpayers with a specified area and amounted to a denial of equal protection. On appeal, the court affirmed, noting that 1991 Mont. Laws 680, S.B. 412, authorized DOR to use a specific stratified sales ratio study to adjust property values in a given district during an appraisal cycle. Because the law in effect prior to S.B. 412 was held to have violated the appraisal provisions of state statutes requiring general and uniform appraisal, assessment, and equalization of all taxable property, S.B. 412 was enacted, requiring reappraisal of areas with widely disparate appraisals. However, the court said, the method now employed by DOR to achieve equalization might do so between areas but not between individual properties where inequities existed. The DOR did not include in its study a representative sample relating to age, condition, and other factors that determine market value. Thus, S.B. 412 was unconstitutional as relating to the appraisal process as a denial of equal protection.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court decision, ruling that the appraisal process was a denial of equal protection.

State v. Smith

Supreme Court of Montana


261 Mont. 419; 863 P.2d 1000; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 336; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 1388

November 9, 1993, Decided

DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the District Court of Flathead County (Montana), which, in his third sentencing hearing, sentenced him to death for his participation in the kidnapping and deliberate homicide of two victims.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to two counts each of deliberate homicide and aggravated kidnapping. He presented no evidence of mitigating circumstances and requested the death penalty, which was imposed. A month later, defendant submitted motions for reconsideration of the sentence and for a psychiatric examination to establish mitigating circumstances. At a hearing on the motions, defendant testified that he had committed the offenses under the influence of alcohol and drugs. The district court appointed a psychiatrist, who concluded that none of the specified mitigating circumstances existed. The district court reimposed the death sentence. The court affirmed. The Ninth Circuit held that defendant's right to due process was violated. The district court, after another hearing, reimposed the death penalty. On appeal, the court vacated the sentence and remanded for resentencing. The court held, inter alia, that the district court did not violate defendant's privilege against self-incrimination, his right to counsel, or his due process rights. However, the district court did err by failing to order a current presentence investigation and report upon resentencing.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the district court's order imposing the death penalty and remanded for resentencing. The court also directed the district court to appoint a different judge to preside over the resentencing hearing.

Associated Students of the Univ. of Mont. v. City of Missoula


No. 93-277


Supreme Court of Montana


261 Mont. 231; 862 P.2d 380; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 304; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 1301


September 17, 1993, Submitted on Briefs  
October 21, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication November 15, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the District Court of Missoula County. Fourth Judicial District. Honorable John S. Henson, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, university students and faculty, sought review of an order of the District Court of Missoula County, Fourth Judicial District (Montana), which granted judgment to defendant city on plaintiff's action seeking injunctive and declaratory relief from Missoula, Mont., Ordinances 2480, 2505, which restricted parking in the residential areas adjacent to a university to residents.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, university students and faculty, filed suit against defendant city for injunctive and declaratory relief, arguing that Missoula, Mont., Ordinances 2480, 2505, which restricted parking in a residential area adjacent to the university to residents, violated the Equal Protection Clauses of the federal and state constitutions in that the ordinances created an unlawful distinction between residents and nonresidents. Plaintiffs also alleged that defendant exceeded its authority in enacting the ordinances. The trial court found for defendant, and plaintiffs sought review. The court affirmed, holding that defendant did not exceed its authority in enacting the parking ordinances. Mont. Const. art. XI, § 4(2) supported a liberal construction of powers granted to local municipalities by the legislature. The court also held that the ordinances' distinction between residents and nonresidents did not violate the state and federal equal protection clauses. The ordinances were rationally related to the legitimate government objective of the residential interest of comparative tranquility, and thus the resident-nonresident distinction did not constitute invidious discrimination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of judgment to defendant city on the suit by plaintiffs, university students and faculty, seeking injunctive and declaratory relief from two ordinances restricting parking in a residential area adjacent to the university to residents. Defendant had the authority to regulate the parking in the district. The distinction between residents and nonresidents was also rationally related to a legitimate government objective.

Saint Vincent Hosp. & Health Ctr. v. Blue Cross & Blue Shield


No. 92-450


Supreme Court of Montana


261 Mont. 56; 862 P.2d 6; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 295; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 1190


April 28, 1993, Submitted  
October 12, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication October 26, 1993.

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff hospital sought review of an order of the District Court of Lewis and Clark County (Montana), which granted defendant insurer summary judgment in the hospital's action to compel a health care provider to contract with the insurer.


OVERVIEW: The insurer entered into a healthlink agreement with a medical center, which established a preferred provider organization. The hospital requested to participate in healthlink, but the insurer denied the request. The hospital filed an action against the insurer and requested production of the healthlink contract. The district court granted the insurer summary judgment, and the court affirmed. The court held that the district court did not err in finding that application of the Willing Provider Amendment of 1991 (amendment), Mont. Code Ann. § 33-22-1704(2) (1991), to compel the insurer to enter into an agreement with the hospital would result in retroactive application of law. The amendment would affect the insurer's substantive rights; therefore, it could not be applied to healthlink. The amendment was approved on April 29, 1991 and could not be applied to a contract entered prior to that date. If applied, the amendment would impair the insurer's vested rights, create new obligations for it, and attach a new disability with respect to its transactions with the medical center.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that granted summary judgment to the insurer in the hospital's action to compel the insurer to enter into a health care provider contract.

 

163 of 930 DOCUMENTS


State v. Cowan


No. 91-289


Supreme Court of Montana


260 Mont. 510; 861 P.2d 884; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 287; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 1153


April 4, 1993, Submitted  
October 6, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied November 4, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the District Court of Missoula County. Fourth Judicial District. Honorable Ed McLean, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the District Court for the Fourth Judicial District, Missoula County (Montana), which convicted defendant of aggravated burglary and attempted deliberate homicide. Defendant contended that he did not have the requisite intent to commit the crimes due to his mental illness.


OVERVIEW: Defendant broke into a government-owned cabin and attacked the cabin's occupent when she returned, severely injuring her. Defendant was caught and charged with burglary and attempted deliberate homicide. Defendant suffered from paranoid schizophrenia, but was found competent to stand trial. The trial court convicted defendant in a bench trial and ordered him committed to the state department of institutions for placement in an appropriate facility. Defendant challenged the finding that he acted knowingly or purposely. The court affirmed the conviction because a trier of fact could have found that defendant possessed the required mental state to have committed the crimes. The presence of a mental illness did not necessarily preclude defendant from acting knowingly or purposely. Testimony from witnesses and experts indicated that defendant was coherent the day before the attack, that he mocked the occupant as she called for help, that he had a tendency to exaggerate his symptoms, and that he often lied to get out of trouble. The court also found that the homicide laws did not establish an unrebuttable presumption of criminal intent but merely established a permissive inference.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's conviction of defendant for burglary and attempted deliberate homicide.

Ritland v. Rowe


No. 93-136


Supreme Court of Montana


260 Mont. 453; 861 P.2d 175; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 292; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 1183


September 2, 1993, Submitted on Briefs  
October 6, 1993, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the District Court of Chouteau County. Twelfth Judicial District. Honorable John Warner, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff ranchers appealed an order from the District Court of Chouteau County (Montana), which granted summary judgment in favor of defendant cattle owners, finding that the ranchers' tort claim against the cattle owners was barred by the two-year statute of limitations contained in Mont. Code Ann. § 27-2-207.


OVERVIEW: The ranchers gave the cattle owners permission to graze their cattle on the ranchers' land, intermingled with the cattle belonging to the ranchers. The ranchers alleged that the cattle owners knew, or should have known, that their cattle were infected with trichomoniasis. The ranchers' cattle became infected with trichomoniasis and, as a result, the ranchers sustained damage to their cattle and their ranching operation. The district court granted the cattle owners' motion for summary judgment, holding that the ranchers' claim was barred by the two-year statute of limitations applicable to injury to property. Mont. Code Ann. § 27-2-207. The ranchers appealed and the appellate court reversed. The appellate court held that the ranchers' action was timely under the applicable three-year statute of limitations for tort actions. Mont. Code Ann. § 27-2-204(1). The court found that Mont. Code Ann. § 27-2-207 was not a more specific statute of limitations than Mont. Code Ann. § 27-2-204(1), but rather both were applicable and were in conflict. The court resolved the conflict in favor of the statute containing the longer limitation period.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the district court's grant of summary judgment in favor of the cattle owners on the basis that the ranchers' complaint was timely under the applicable three-year statute of limitations and remanded to the district court for further proceedings.

State v. Byers


No. 91-406


Supreme Court of Montana


261 Mont. 17; 861 P.2d 860; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 294; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 1162


February 11, 1993, Submitted  
October 6, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Rehearing Denied November 4, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the District Court of Gallatin County. Eighteenth Judicial District. Honorable Frank M. Davis and Thomas Olson, Judges. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions from the District Court of the Eighteenth Judicial District, Gallatin County (Montana) for two counts of deliberate homicide.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of deliberate homicide. On appeal, the court affirmed the convictions and found that (1) the mental disease and defect statute, Mont. Code Ann. § 46-14-102, was constitutional; it did not relieve the state of its burden to prove all elements of the crimes, it provided defendant the opportunity to present evidence of a mental disease or defect in order to cast a reasonable doubt in the minds of the jurors, and it made it the province of the jury to determine whether any mental disease or defect had an impact on defendant's required state of mind to commit an offense, (2) the consideration of mental disease or defect by the judge at sentencing did not nullify the jury verdict or violate defendant's right to a jury trial, but was a precaution for the protection of defendant and society, (3) evidence of alteration to the shotgun used in the crime was part of the corpus delicti of the crime and did not require a Just or Modified Just notice, and (4) the weapon enhancement statute, Mont. Code Ann. § 46-18-221, was a sentencing factor; it neither constituted a separate offense nor restricted sentencing considerations to a single period of time.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for two counts of deliberate homicide.

OVERVIEW: The landowner instituted an action alleging that respondents annexed his real property in violation of his constitutional rights, giving rise to a claim under § 1983. OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of summary judgment to respondents.

City of Helena v. Lewis


Nos. 92-407, 92-443


Supreme Court of Montana


260 Mont. 421; 860 P.2d 698; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 273; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 1103


May 20, 1993, Submitted on Briefs  
September 20, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied October 15, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the District Court of Lewis and Clark County. First Judicial District. Honorable Thomas C. Honzel (92-407) and Jeffrey Sherlock (92-443), Judges. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed orders from the District Court of Lewis and Clark County, First Judicial District (Montana) that found one defendant guilty of criminal trespass and disorderly conduct and one defendant guilty of criminal trespass. Defendants claimed that they were improperly barred from raising certain defenses, that the prosecution did not establish the elements of the crimes, and that exculpatory material was withheld in discovery.


OVERVIEW: Defendants' convictions arose from their participation in an anti-abortion protest in which they chained themselves together and blocked the entrance to a clinic. In affirming their convictions, the court ruled that: (1) the district court properly granted the city's motion in limine to prohibit defendants from introducing evidence regarding the defense of necessity, under Mont. Code Ann. § 45-2-212, because the defense did not include imminent threats of harm to a third party and defendants did not allege that they reasonably believed that death or serious bodily injury would be inflicted upon them if they did not trespass; (2) defendants were properly barred from asserting a justifiable use of force defense because they did not prove or allege that any of the clinic's activities were "unlawful" under Mont. Code Ann. § 45-3-102; (3) the district court properly refused to instruct the jury on those defenses; (4) the city adequately proved the mental state required by the statutes at issue; and (5) the district court's refusal to admit defendants' proffered evidence regarding their asserted defenses did not in way amount to an unconstitutional withholding of exculpatory evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' convictions for criminal trespass and disorderly conduct.

Bozeman Daily Chronicle v. City of Bozeman Police Dep't


No. 93-127


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


260 Mont. 218; 859 P.2d 435; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 254; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 1014; 21 Media L. Rep. 2303


July 29, 1993, Submitted On Briefs  
August 27, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication September 13, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the Eighteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Gallatin, The Honorable Thomas C. Honzel, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant police department challenged the judgment from the District Court of the Eighteenth Judicial District, Gallatin County (Montana), which held that the city had to pay attorney's fees to respondent newspaper. The newspaper cross-appealed from that portion of the trial court's judgment that denied the newspaper's request for the release of investigative documents.


OVERVIEW: The newspaper wanted the police to release the name of an officer involved in an incident and to release the documents pertaining to the investigation. The newspaper also named the sheriff's department as a defendant, which did not appeal the trial court's ruling. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment that awarded attorney's fees, but remanded that portion of the trial court's judgment that denied the request for the release of the documents. The court held that the newspaper had a right to know the information under the Montana Constitution, which was only qualified by the constitutional limitation of the right to privacy of the individuals involved in the incident, pursuant to Mont. Const. art. II, § 10. Therefore, the court ordered that the trial court conduct an in camera inspection of the documents to determine what could be released to the newspaper after balancing the privacy rights of the alleged victim and of the witnesses with the public's right to know. Lastly, the court held that, as the prevailing party on the constitutional claim, the newspaper was properly entitled to an award of attorney's fees.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court that denied the newspaper's request for the release of investigative documents after it held that the newspaper was entitled to the information limited only by the constitutional limitation of the right to privacy of the individuals involved in the incident. The court affirmed that portion of the trial court's judgment that awarded attorney's fees to the newspaper as the prevailing party.
