Story v. City of Bozeman


No. 91-632


Supreme Court of Montana


259 Mont. 207; 856 P.2d 202; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 197; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 761


March 25, 1993, Submitted  
June 24, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication July 12, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the District Court of Gallatin County. Eighteenth Judicial District. Honorable Jeffrey Sherlock, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part; reversed in part and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a city and an employee, sought review of the decision of the District Court of Gallatin County (Montana), which awarded damages to appellee construction company on its breach of contract and intentional interference with contract claims.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the city had wholly failed to object to the amount of damages presented by the construction company at trial. The court held that the city could not now claim that the proof of damages was speculative and excessive. The city argued that no action could be taken against its employee under Mont. Code Ann. § 2-9-305(5). The court found that the statute did not bar an action against an employee when commenced at the same time action was initiated against the city. The court stated that although § 2-9-305(5) was not a model of clarity, one clear purpose behind the statute was to preclude a claimant from recovering from both the governmental entity and the governmental employee for the same conduct. The construction company recovered damages from the city. The court held that the double recovery awarded by the jury was barred by the plain language of § 2-9-305(5), and on that basis, the separate award against the employee for intentional interference with contractual relations was stricken from the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the award of damages against the city, concluding that the district court did not err in permitting retrial of the issues of constructive fraud, defamation and reformation. The court reversed the award of damages against the employee, holding that it was an improper double recovery against the city and employee for the same conduct.

 

170 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Stratemeyer v. Lincoln County


No. 92-376


Supreme Court of Montana


259 Mont. 147; 855 P.2d 506; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 188; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 731


December 16, 1992, Submitted  
June 23, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied July 16, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Montana Workers' Compensation Court. Honorable Timothy Reardon, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant workers' compensation trust appealed an order of the Montana Worker's Compensation Court entered in favor of claimant employee in a workers' compensation action. The order stated that Mont. Code Ann. § 39-71-119(3)(a)-(b) (1987) violated the equal protection clause of Mont. Const. art. II, § 4.


OVERVIEW: The employee suffered a work-related psychological injury. Mont. Code Ann. § 39-71-119 excluded from the definition of injury work-related injuries that did not have a physical component. The workers' compensation court found that § 39-71-119 was unconstitutional because it violated the equal protection clause of Mont. Const. art. II, § 4. On appeal, the court reversed. The court held that the rational basis test was applicable because the statute did not affect any fundamental right or suspect class. It further held that the purpose of the legislation did not have to appear on the face of the legislation or in the legislative history. Upon consideration of the facts, the court determined that the legislation was presumed to be constitutional, and that the party challenging the constitutionality of a statute had the burden of proof. Under a rational basis test, denying compensation for work-related injuries that did not have a physical component was rationally related to a legitimate governmental objective of controlling the costs of the workers' compensation program and providing benefits. Thus, the statute was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court entered in favor of the claimant in his workers' compensation action against the workers' compensation trust.

Lence v. Hagadone Inv. Co.


No. 92-152


Supreme Court of Montana


258 Mont. 433; 853 P.2d 1230; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 161; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 601; 21 Media L. Rep. 1641


December 10, 1992, Submitted  
May 27, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication June 22, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the District Court of Flathead County. Eleventh Judicial District. Honorable Michael H. Keedy, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The District Court of the Eleventh Judicial District, Flathead County (Montana) granted summary judgment in favor of respondents, a newspaper and related individuals, against appellants, an attorney and his corporation. The attorney sought damages arising from libel, false light invasion of privacy, intentional and negligent infliction of emotional distress, and negligence. The attorney and corporation appealed.


OVERVIEW: The newspaper published an article, which reported that the Montana Supreme Court's Commission on Practice was looking into allegations of fraud and professional conduct on the part of the attorney. The article also stated that a local building contractor had filed a complaint against the attorney with the "high court," alleging fraud, professional misconduct, and failure to pay for services. The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment because there were no material issues of fact. The attorney failed to meet his threshold burden of establishing the falsity of the article, which was addressed in subsequent articles by the newspaper. The articles were protected by the First Amendment as truthful information about a matter of public significance. There was no false light invasion of privacy claim when falsity was not established. The deposition testimony demonstrated the absence of any genuine issue of material fact concerning the severity of the attorney's alleged emotional distress. The attorney's negligence claim failed because the newspaper owed no duty to investigate or to maintain confidentiality. The newspaper only reported that there was an investigation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of the newspaper and related individuals.

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the District Court of Lewis and Clark County (Montana), which affirmed a judgment from the Helena Municipal Court (Montana), which convicted defendant of criminal trespass, a misdemeanor.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who was part of an anti-abortion demonstration, was arrested at a family planning clinic for violating Mont. Code Ann. § 45-6-203. The statute made it a crime to enter or remain unlawfully on the premises of another. The trial court convicted defendant of criminal trespass, a misdemeanor. The court of appeals affirmed the judgment of the trial court. On appeal, defendant admitted that she had trespassed but contended that a fine assessed against her was improper because she entered and remained on the clinic property to exercise the right of free speech guaranteed by the First Amendment and by Mont. Const. art. II, § 7. The court affirmed the judgments of the trial court and the court of appeals. Const. art. II, § 7 entitled defendant to have access to the family planning clinic's property in order to exercise her right of free speech.

Vainio v. Brookshire


No. 92-332


Supreme Court of Montana


258 Mont. 273; 852 P.2d 596; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 138


January 14, 1993, Submitted on Briefs  
May 13, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication June 3, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the District Court of Silver Bow County. Second Judicial District. Honorable Mark P. Sullivan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant personal representative challenged the decision of the Second Judicial District Court, Silver Bow County (Montana), which affirmed the order of the Human Rights Commission (commission) that found the employer/supervisor at decedent's nightclub committed sexual harassment against respondent employee and also affirmed the commission's damage award.


OVERVIEW: The employee filed a complaint with the commission against the nightclub, her employer, and her manager for sexual harassment prior to her termination. During the time of the sexual harassment, the decedent owned the nightclub. The personal representative argued that Mont. Code Ann. § 49-2-506(1)(b), unconstitutionally denied the decedent's right to a jury trial. The court found that the personal representative failed to prove beyond a reasonable doubt that the administrative contested case procedures under the Human Rights Act unconstitutionally denied the decedent's right to a jury trial. The decedent was aware of the employee's complaint three months after she had been fired. All that was needed to make the decedent subject to the administrative proceeding was for the employee to allege that the manager, an agent of the decedent, committed unlawful sexual discrimination. Therefore, the decedent was liable on the basis of respondeat superior.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was affirmed.

Weber v. State


No. 92-417


Supreme Court of Montana


258 Mont. 62; 852 P.2d 117; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 114; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 425; 8 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 835; 125 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P57,441


January 21, 1993, Submitted on Briefs  
April 19, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Changed May 25, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the District Court of Lewis and Clark County. First Judicial District. Honorable Jeffrey Sherlock, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant a former employee sought review of a judgment of the District Court of Lewis and Clark County (Montana) eliminating interest on the judgment award, pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 2-9-317, in the employee's action brought against cross-appellant State in the employee's wrongful discharge action. The State appealed the assessment of half the cost of the trial transcript on appeal to the State.


OVERVIEW: In the employee's action, the trial court rendered judgment for the employee and awarded damages, which did not provide for costs or for post-judgment interest. The employee's motion brought, pursuant to Mont. R. Civ. P. 59(g), was granted and the trial court included costs in the judgment, and interest thereon. The State filed its own Rule 59 motion, which was granted. The trial court entered a nunc pro tunc order striking the post-judgment interest from the order because of the State's immunity to post-judgment interest pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 2-9-317. The court affirmed, holding that the employee was not entitled to attorney fees and costs. Because the State paid the judgment amount plus costs as prescribed by the nunc pro tunc amended judgment within the two year period described in § 2-9-317, interest was not appropriate. The court found that § 2-9-317 was constitutional as a limitation on interest assessed against the State and did not violate either the spirit or substance of Mont. Const. art. II, § 18. Because both parties appealed, the trial court correctly divided the costs of the transcript, in compliance with Mont. Code Ann. § 25-10-104.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

State v. Scott

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the District Court for the First Judicial District, Broadwater County (Montana), which convicted defendant after a jury found him guilty of sexual assault.


OVERVIEW: The victim told her grandmother that defendant, the child's stepfather, had sexually assaulted her. The grandmother filed a report of child abuse, and defendant was charged and convicted. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment. Defendant failed to show that the district court abused its discretion in the admission of the videotape, the denial of a continuance requested by defendant, the control of cross-examination, and the choice of jury instructions. The properly qualified expert witness properly testified directly about the credibility of the child victim when defendant attacked the credibility of the child.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

State ex rel. Dreher v. Fuller

Supreme Court of Montana


257 Mont. 445; 849 P.2d 1045; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 89; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 349

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant land owner appealed from an order of the First Judicial District Court, Lewis and Clark County (Montana), which dismissed her action against defendants, the Lewis and Clark County Commissioners, seeking a writ of mandamus and declaratory judgment that the Montana Subdivision and Platting Act (Act) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The land owner purchased approximately 132 acres of land and applied for approval of the creation of a six-acre tract based on an occasional sale exemption. Defendants denied the request on the basis that the proposed division did not comply with the newly enacted county subdivision regulations. Based upon the county's denial of the proposed division, the land owner filed an action in district court seeking a writ of mandamus and declaratory judgment alleging the unconstitutionality of the Act. The district court ruled against her and she appealed. The court affirmed, finding that the regulations were not inconsistent and contradictory to the Act. The court found that the state legislature did not impermissibly delegate its authority to the Lewis and Clark County Commissioners for the regulation of subdivisions and the Montana Subdivision and Platting Act and that the subdivision regulations adopted by Lewis and Clark County pursuant to the Act were constitutional. Further, the subdivision regulations did not violate due process nor did they constitute a taking without just compensation.


OUTCOME: The order that dismissed the land owner's action seeking a writ of mandamus and declaratory judgment that the subdivision regulations were unconstitutional, was affirmed.

Stuart v. Department of Social & Rehabilitation Services


No. 91-316


Supreme Court of Montana


256 Mont. 231; 846 P.2d 965; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 16; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 48


March 26, 1992, Submitted on Briefs  
January 29, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication February 17, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from District Court of Lewis and Clark County. First Judicial District. Honorable Dorothy McCarter, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former employees challenged a judgment from the District Court of Lewis and Clark County, First Judicial District (Montana), which granted summary judgment for respondent former public employer in the employees' action for accrued vacation benefits. The trial court found that the failure to pay the benefits did not violate double jeopardy and equal protection and that Mont. Code Ann. § 1-18-617(2) was not impermissibly vague.


OVERVIEW: The employees were terminated as a result of a criminal investigation and alleged sexual misconduct. The employer refused to pay the employees any accrued vacation benefits. Although the employees did not challenge their termination in light of their conviction for criminal mischief, they sought a declaratory judgment that the employer was obligated to pay them accred vacation benefits. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of the employer and the employees appealed, contending that the employer's refusal to pay the benefits violated double jeopardy protection and denied equal protection and that Mont. Code Ann. § 2-18-617(2) was unconstitutionally vague. The court held that the refusal to pay accrued vacation benefits was not a civil sanction and therefore did not violate double jeopardy because the employees were not being subjected to different penalties for the same conduct. The court also found that the Equal Protection Clause was not violated because the employees were not discriminated against on an impermissible ground. Lastly, the court concluded that the employees did not have standing to challenge the facial constitutionality of § 2-18-617(2).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

178 of 930 DOCUMENTS


In re Marriage of Bross


No. 92-172


Supreme Court of Montana


256 Mont. 174; 845 P.2d 728; 1993 Mont. LEXIS 2; 50 Mont. St. Rep. 13


August 13, 1992, Submitted on Briefs  
January 12, 1993, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication January 26, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from District Court of Yellowstone County. Thirteenth Judicial District. Honorable G. Todd Baugh, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife challenged a judgment from the District Court of Yellowstone County of the Thirteenth Judicial District (Montana) granting her request for spousal support but finding that it terminated upon her cohabitation in a marital-like relationship.


OVERVIEW: The wife and appellee husband agreed as to all the terms of their dissolution except for spousal maintenance. The wife argued that the award was improper because it failed to consider her standard of living and because it failed to account for her tax liability. The wife further challenged the termination of support upon her cohabitation. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court held that the lower court's finding was not clearly erroneous. The record showed that the lower court did consider the couple's standard of living, noting that the wife's requested maintenance would comport with her established standard of living. While a district court must consider each of the factors in Mont. Code Ann. § 40-4-203(2), it was not necessary for the court to set forth a specific finding for each element. To determine the final maintenance award, all relevant facts should be considered by the court as a whole. The court reversed, holding that the lower court was assuming that if the wife was to cohabit, then there would be a substantial change in her financial circumstances. That presumption was not supported by substantial evidence and was, therefore, clearly erroneous.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the award of spousal support but reversed finding that there was no authority to terminate an award upon cohabitation in a marital-like relationship. The court remanded to modify the decree.

 

179 of 930 DOCUMENTS


State v. Leistiko


No. 92-088


Supreme Court of Montana


256 Mont. 32; 844 P.2d 97; 1992 Mont. LEXIS 338; 49 Mont. St. Rep. 1104


October 14, 1992, Submitted on Briefs  
December 17, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication January 6, 1993.
As Amended.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from District Court of Flathead County. Eleventh Judicial District. Honorable Michael Keedy, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Vacated in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of revocation from the District Court of Flathead County, Eleventh Judicial District (Montana), which continued his suspended sentence and required defendant to pay his victim $ 10,000 additional restitution after defendant violated the terms and conditions of his suspended sentence while on parole.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the district court had no authority to increase his original sentence by an additional $ 10,000 in restitution. He relied on the version of Mont. Code Ann. § 46-18-203 (1983), which was in effect at the time of his crime and at the time of the initial sentencing. The district court relied on the 1991 revision § 46-18-203 in its sentencing order. Defendant contended that he was entitled to the benefits of the former statute. The court vacated defendant's sentence in part, holding that under U.S. Const. art. I, § 10 and Mont. Const. art. II, § 31, the district court could not order defendant to pay his victim $ 10,000 additional restitution after defendant violated the terms and conditions of his suspended sentence while on parole. The court determined that the revised statute clearly changed the legal consequences of defendant's crime by allowing the district court to change his sentence. The court concluded that the additional restitution constituted an unconstitutional increase in punishment, which defendant could not have foreseen at the time he committed his crime or when he entered into a plea agreement with the prosecutor before sentencing.


OUTCOME: The court vacated that part of the order, which required defendant to pay his victim $ 10,000 in additional restitution after defendant violated the terms and conditions of his suspended sentence while on parole.

 

180 of 930 DOCUMENTS


State v. Larson


No. 92-197


Supreme Court of Montana


255 Mont. 451; 843 P.2d 777; 1992 Mont. LEXIS 335; 49 Mont. St. Rep. 1077


October 8, 1992, Submitted on Briefs  
December 15, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication December 30, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from District Court of Lewis and Clark County. First Judicial District. Honorable Thomas Honzel, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the District Court of Lewis and Clark County (Montana) that convicted him of negligent endangerment in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-208, arguing that the evidence was insufficient to sustain his conviction.


OVERVIEW: After consuming at least four cans of beer and two shots of whiskey and with a blood-alcohol level of at least .17, defendant mounted a horse described to him by the owner as "hot-blooded" and "inexperienced." The owner of the horse warned defendant not to tug on the reins and not to give rides to children. Despite these warnings, defendant allowed a five-year-old child on the horse with him. When the horse began to jump, defendant reined the horse back. The horse fell over backward, and the child was fatally injured. Defendant was charged with and convicted of negligent endangerment in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-208. He appealed his conviction. In affirming, the court concluded that, under the facts, a rational jury could find that defendant's conduct amounted to a gross deviation from the ordinary care that a reasonable person would observe in a similar situation. As such, the jury was justified in finding beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant acted negligently.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction was affirmed.

 

181 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Remington v. Mont. Dep't of Corr. & Human Servs.


Nos. 92-284, 92-451


Supreme Court of Montana


255 Mont. 480; 844 P.2d 50; 1992 Mont. LEXIS 334; 49 Mont. St. Rep. 1084


October 8, 1992, Submitted on Briefs  
December 15, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication January 6, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from District Court of Powell County. Third Judicial District. Honorable Ted L. Mizner, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed, and writ denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate filed a petition for mandamus, habeas corpus, and declaratory relief, seeking approval for "good time" credit to which he claimed entitlement based on correspondence courses he had completed. The District Court of Powell County (Montana) dismissed the inmate's petition, and the inmate appealed. The inmate also filed a second petition for habeas corpus in the appellate court.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the district court did not abuse its discretion in dismissing the petition for declaratory judgment because declaratory judgment was not the proper procedure for resolution of the case. The court held that a declaratory judgment was primarily intended to determine the meaning of a law or a contract and to adjudicate the rights of the parties therein, but not to determine controverted issues of fact such as facts which give rise to a claim of denial of procedural due process. The resolution of the questions in this case involved factual determinations prior to any consideration of due process or equal protection. The court held further that the inmate failed to present facts that warranted habeas corpus. The court held that because a liberty interest was created only when the enabling statute specifically constrained or limited the discretion of the decision maker, and such constraint was absent here, the inmate did not have a liberty interest in "good time" credits.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's order dismissing the inmate's petition for declaratory judgment and denied the inmate's application for a writ of habeas corpus.

 

182 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Wolfe v. Dep't of Labor & Indus.


No. 92-194


Supreme Court of Montana


255 Mont. 336; 843 P.2d 338; 1992 Mont. LEXIS 318; 49 Mont. St. Rep. 1042; 61 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 351; 142 L.R.R.M. 2758


September 17, 1992, Submitted on Briefs  
December 8, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication December 30, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from District Court of Lewis and Clark County. First Judicial District. Honorable Dorothy McCarter, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Vacated and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee appealed a decision from the District Court of Lewis and Clark County in the First Judicial District (Montana), which declared Mont. Code Ann. § 39-31-204 unconstitutional. The employee alleged that respondents, union and State of Montana Department of Labor and Industry Board of Personnel Appeals (Board of Personnel Appeals), violated her rights of non-association under § 39-31-204 and to exercise her religious beliefs.


OVERVIEW: The employee's employment was subject to a collective bargaining agreement. The union allowed the payment of an agency fee in lieu of joining the union. The employee objected to payment of the agency fee on religious grounds because the union publicly supported legalized abortion and birth control. The employee sent a letter to the union and to the Board of Personnel Appeals. The employee was informed that she did not qualify for non-association under Mont. Code Ann. § 39-31-204. The employee sought judicial review, and the district court declared § 39-31-204 unconstitutional. The court chose to consider the freedom of religion claim even though the district court did not address it because it was a fundamental right. The court indicated that the case could be decided without ruling on the constitutionality of § 39-31-204. The court focused on the issue of religious discrimination by employers and unions under Title VII of the Civil Rights Act of 1964, 42 U.S.C.S. § 2000e et seq.; Mont. Code Ann. § 49-2-101 et seq. The court concluded that the issue was whether the unions reasonably accommodated the employee's religious beliefs and remanded for a determination of that issue.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the decision from the district court, which had declared unconstitutional a statute regarding public employees' right of non-association with and non-financial support of unions based upon certain religious grounds. The court remanded the case to the Board of Personnel Appeals to determine whether the union offered a reasonable accommodation of the employee's religious beliefs.

 

183 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Great Falls Tribune Co. v. Great Falls Pub. Sch.


No. 91-474


Supreme Court of Montana


255 Mont. 125; 841 P.2d 502; 1992 Mont. LEXIS 295; 49 Mont. St. Rep. 944; 141 L.R.R.M. 2963; 20 Media L. Rep. 2120


May 21, 1992, Submitted  
November 12, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication December 2, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from District Court of Cascade County. Eighth Judicial District. Honorable Joel G. Roth, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant newspaper sought review of the declaratory judgment of the District Court of Cascade County (Montana) in the newspaper's action against the Great Falls Public Schools, Board of Trustees (school board) that the collective bargaining strategy exception to the open meeting law, Mont. Code Ann. § 2-3-203(4), did not violate Mont. Const. art. II, § 9.


OVERVIEW: Before an open meeting to discuss a report regarding labor negotiations, the school board met privately to discuss the report. At the open meeting, the report was rejected without discussion. The newspaper, which attended the open meeting, filed an action against the school board, alleging violation of the open meeting laws, Mont. Code Ann. § 2-3-203 and Mont. Const. art. II, § 9. The school board counterclaimed, seeking a declaratory judgment that meetings that it held were lawful pursuant to the collective bargaining strategy exception to the open meeting law, Mont. Code Ann. § 2-3-203(4), and that § 2-3-203(4) was constitutional. The trial court granted the school board's motion for summary judgment on its counterclaim. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment of the trial court. The court ruled that art. II, § 9 was unambiguous and capable of interpretation without extrinsic aids. Article II, § 9 mandated that meetings be closed only when the need for individual privacy exceeded the merits of public disclosure. Thus, the court ruled that the collective bargaining strategy exception to the open meeting law, Mont. Code Ann. § 2-3-203(4), was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the declaratory judgment of the trial court in the newspaper's action against the school board that the collective bargaining strategy exception to the open meeting law did not violate the Montana Constitution.

 

184 of 930 DOCUMENTS


State v. Fertterer


No. 91-443


Supreme Court of Montana


255 Mont. 73; 841 P.2d 467; 1992 Mont. LEXIS 269; 49 Mont. St. Rep. 846


July 9, 1992, Submitted  
September 28, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied November 12, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from District Court of Cascade County. Eighth Judicial District. Honorable Joel G. Roth, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part; remanded for recalculation.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants sought review of a decision of the District Court of Cascade County, Eighth Judicial District (Montana), which, after a jury trial, convicted each defendant of two counts of felony criminal mischief, as proscribed by Mont. Code Ann. § 45-6-101(1)(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendants, who ran a large-scale poaching operation, were arrested after an undercover investigation. The jury convicted each defendant of two counts of felony criminal mischief. As a condition of defendants' partially suspended sentences, the district court charged both defendants with restitution for the value of the wild game they illegally killed, the cost of the jury trial, and investigation expenses incurred by the state. On appeal, the court affirmed defendants' convictions. The court held that: (1) under Montana's police powers, the state could regulate and/or prohibit the killing of wild game; (2) wild animals were "public property" within the meaning of Montana's criminal mischief statute, § 45-6-101(1)(a); and (3) the prosecutor had the discretion to charge defendants with felony criminal mischief, because the misdemeanor provisions of Mont. Code Ann. tit. 87 did not provide an exclusive remedy for the illegal taking of game. The court did, however, hold that defendants' restitution for investigation expenses should have been limited to the amount paid directly to defendants for guided hunts and for meat and animal parts purchased in the undercover investigation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the district court's decision, which convicted defendants of felony criminal mischief and, as a condition of defendants' partially suspended sentences, charged defendants with restitution, except that the court remanded for recalculation of restitution for investigative expenses.

 

185 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Mt. Water Company v. Mont. Dep't of Public Serv. Regulation


No. 91-446


Supreme Court of Montana


254 Mont. 76; 835 P.2d 4; 1992 Mont. LEXIS 196; 49 Mont. St. Rep. 632


May 7, 1992, Submitted on briefs  
July 21, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified on Denial of Rehearing August 13, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from District Court of Lewis and Clark County. First Judicial District. Honorable Thomas C. Honzel, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state agencies sought review of the order of the District Court of Lewis and Clark County, First Judicial District (Montana), which ruled that respondent water company was entitled to reimbursement through a rate increase for prior expenses it had incurred pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 69-4-511. The state agencies claimed the water company could not receive a rate increase based on past expenditures.


OVERVIEW: A federal court held that Mont. Code Ann. § 69-4-511, which made the water company responsible for the cost of maintaining water service pipelines from the main to the owner's property line, was a taking of private property for a public use and that the water company could seek just compensation through rate setting for its property taken. Appellant public service commission denied the water company's request for a rate increase to reimburse it for prior expenses under § 69-4-511 but granted the application for an increase to compensate the water company for future expenses. The trial court held that the public service commission's refusal to allow recovery of prior expenses failed to justly compensate the water company for the taking. The court held that public utility rates had to be set to match utility costs during the period the rates were in effect. The court ruled that rate-making was a legislative activity and was therefore prospective only in its effect and that the water company could not set its rates so as to amortize past deficits. The court reversed the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court that found that the water company was entitled to reimbursement through a rate increase for prior expenses.

 

186 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Sorensen v. Dep't of Revenue


Nos. 91-379, 91-569


Supreme Court of Montana


254 Mont. 61; 836 P.2d 29; 1992 Mont. LEXIS 195; 49 Mont. St. Rep. 624


May 28, 1992, Submitted  
July 21, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied August 13, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from District Court of Missoula County. Fourth Judicial District. (91-379) Honorable John S. Henson, Judge.

Appeal from District Court of Lewis and Clark County. First Judicial District (91-569). Honorable Jeffrey M. Sherlock, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Department of Revenue (DOR) sought review of orders by the District Court of Missoula County, Fourth Judicial District (Montana) and the District Court of Lewis and Clark County, First Judicial District (Montana), which ruled that a tax assessed by the DOR on defendants for drugs in their possession violated double jeopardy.


OVERVIEW: The DOR contended that double jeopardy did not attach to the Drug Tax because the tax was an excise tax for raising revenue, not a criminal penalty or civil sanction. Defendants contended that the Drug Tax was a criminal penalty, and thus, violated double jeopardy. The court concluded that the Legislature clearly intended to create a tax not a criminal sanction. The tax did not impose any affirmative disability or restraint upon a taxpayer. A taxpayer was required to pay an assessment based on the quantity of drugs in his possession and was not subject to incarceration or any other restraint of his liberty or privileges. The tax had a remedial purpose other than promoting retribution and deterrence. The tax collected was used for youth evaluations, chemical aftercare, chemical abuse assessments, and juvenile detention facilities. The tax was based on possession and storage of dangerous drugs. Although the conduct of possessing dangerous drugs subjected taxpayers to both a criminal penalty and a tax, it was not so punitive in purpose or effect that it negated the legislative intent to create a civil sanction. The tax was not a multiple punishment and did not violate double jeopardy.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial courts.

 

187 of 930 DOCUMENTS


In re D.S.


No. 91-264


Supreme Court of Montana


253 Mont. 484; 833 P.2d 1090; 1992 Mont. LEXIS 167; 49 Mont. St. Rep. 552


February 6, 1992, Submitted on briefs  
June 25, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication July 9, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from District Court of Beaverhead County. Fifth Judicial District. Honorable Frank M. Davis, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parents sought review of an order of the Montana Fifth Judicial District Court, Beaverhead County, which terminated their parental rights in their seven children. Respondent Department of Family Services (DFS) filed the petition to have the rights terminated.


OVERVIEW: After the children had excessive absences and sexual abuse was suspected, the DFS investigated. Several hearings were held at which the parents were not represented. The result of the hearings was that the oldest child was removed to foster care and a treatment plan was proposed, which the parents refused to sign or comply with. After 10 months of the parents refusing to abide by the plan or to take any action to improve the lives of the children, the DFS petition the court to terminate the parental rights. At such hearing, counsel was appointed for the parents. On appeal, the court held that while the trial court was incorrect in taking judicial notice of the prior proceedings and incorporating those findings into its final order, the due process rights of the parents were not violated and were satisfied by the appointment of counsel prior to the termination hearing. The criteria of Mont. Code Ann. § 41-3-609 were satisfied because the children were adjudicated to be youths in need of care, the parents failed to comply with the treatment plan, and the parents' conduct causing the problem could not be rectified within a reasonable time.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order.

Brisendine v. Dep't of Commerce


No. 91-218


SUPREME COURT OF MONTANA


253 Mont. 361; 833 P.2d 1019; 1992 Mont. LEXIS 132


November 14, 1992, Submitted on Briefs  
May 21, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication June 5, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] APPEAL FROM: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis & Clark, The Honorable Thomas C. Honzel, Judge presiding.
Board of Dentistry v. Kandarian, 248 Mont. 444, 813 P.2d 409, 1991 Mont. LEXIS 135 (Mont., 1991)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a licensed denturist, challenged an order of the First judicial District Court, Lewis and Clark County (Montana), which dismissed his complaint for declaratory and injunctive relief. Respondent state board of dentistry had filed a Mont. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(6) motion to dismiss for failure to state a claim. The trial court held that the complaint did not present a justiciable controversy.


OVERVIEW: The denturist sought approval to enter into a business association with a dentist regarding fees and compensation. The board issued a letter informing the denturist that if he went ahead with his proposal he would be illegal practicing dentistry, and would be risking his denturist license. In his action for declaratory relief, the denturist requested the court to decide whether he could enter into such a business association. The court found that the letter from the board was advisory in nature and was not a final opinion from which an appeal could be taken. The court held that by using the Declaratory Judgment Act, the denturist was attempting to seek an advisory opinion from the court regarding his rights under Mont. Code Ann. §§ 37-4-103 and 37-29-103, which would be an unwarranted intrusion into the board's regulatory authority. Additionally, the court held that the denturist had not exhausted his administrative remedies, nor raised a constitutional issue such that the requirement to exhaust his administrative remedies could be excepted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

 

189 of 930 DOCUMENTS


In re the Existing Rights ex rel. All the Water


No. 91-140


Supreme Court of Montana


253 Mont. 167; 832 P.2d 1210; 1992 Mont. LEXIS 192


January 23, 1992, Submitted  
May 7, 1992, Decided  
May 6, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Revised.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Montana State Water Courts. Honorable C. Bruce Loble, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners sought review of an order of the Montana State Water Court that held the owners' water rights forfeit for their failure to timely file a claim pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 85-2-226. The water court upheld the constitutionality of § 85-2-226, finding that its application worked as a forfeiture and not an abandonment. The owners asserted that § 85-2-226 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The action was initiated sua sponte through an order of the Chief Water Judge. The law governing Montana's water rights adjudication required pre-1973 water claims to be filed with the Department of Natural Resources by a certain date or be conclusively presumed abandoned. The water court consolidated 109 late claims filed in the Yellowstone River Basin. The water court entered a judgment that the owners' late claims were conclusively abandoned under § 85-2-226. The owners sought review, and the court affirmed the judgment. The court held that Mont. Const. art. IX, § 3 did not establish that pre-1973 water rights were immune from sovereign powers. The court ruled that these rights, like other property rights, were protected against unreasonable state action; however, they were not granted indefeasible status. The court concluded that consistent with Mont. Const. art. IX, § 3(1), the Montana legislature could enact constitutionally sound regulations, including the requirement for property owners to take affirmative actions to maintain their water rights. Section 85-2-226 satisfied the guarantees of substantive due process and was not a taking without just compensation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the water court against the property owners and upheld the constitutionality of a state statute that conclusively presumed water rights were forfeited if the water claims were not filed by a date certain.

 

190 of 930 DOCUMENTS


State of Monatana ex rel. Gould v. Mike Cooney


No. 92-174


Supreme Court of Montana


253 Mont. 90; 831 P.2d 593; 1992 Mont. LEXIS 116; 49 Mont. St. Rep. 410



May 6, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication May 20, 1992


PRIOR HISTORY: Original Jurisdiction. 



DISPOSITION: Injunction denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, members of the Montana legislature, residents, registered voters, and taxpayers, sought a writ of injunction to prevent a referendum of the Treasure State Endowment. Petitioners also sought a declaratory judgment that the bill was unconstitutional under Mont. Art. VI, § 10. Respondents, Secretary of State, election administrators, and the State of Montana, filed a motion to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: A referendum measure identified as House Bill 19, otherwise known as the "Treasure State Endowment," was set to appear on an election ballot. The bill was not subjected to the veto power of the governor because it was not submitted for his signature. Petitioners applied for a writ of injunction to prevent the referendum. Petitioners also sought to declare the bill unconstitutional. The court denied the injunction, holding that Mont. Const. art. VI, § 10, art. III, § 5 clearly provided that legislation calling for a referendum measure did not require the governor's signature. Also, the referendum measure, which was a measure redirecting coal severance tax, was not an appropriations measure. Thus, Mont. Const. art. III, § 5 did not prevent its submittal to the people as a ballot issue. Further, Mont. Code Ann. § 13-27-310(2) required a copy to be transmitted to the attorney general at least six months before the election. However, the court held that the particular intent in the referendum that it was to be on a special election ballot on a specified day controlled over the statement of general intent set forth at §13-27-310(2).


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioners' application for a writ of injunction.

Burris v. Employment Relations Div./Dep't of Labor & Indus.


No. 91-243


Supreme Court of Montana


252 Mont. 376; 829 P.2d 639; 1992 Mont. LEXIS 101; 49 Mont. St. Rep. 326


October 31, 1991, Submitted on Briefs  
April 15, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication May 1, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Workers' Compensation Court. Honorable Timothy W. Reardon, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Claimant attorney sought review of a decision of the Workers' Compensation Court (Montana), which affirmed a finding of the hearing division of respondent Department of Labor and Industry (department), which held that the department had the authority to regulate attorney fees and that the attorney had not shown any injury under his constitutional claims.


OVERVIEW: The attorney submitted the attorney retainer forms provided by the department to the Employment Relations Division (ERD) as required by the statute and included a copy of his client contracts, which provided for fees that were higher than those allowed by law. The workers' compensation court affirmed the denial of the approval of the attorney retainer forms and the court affirmed. The court stated that the workers' compensation court or the ERD were the proper authorities to determine the reasonableness of attorney fees and exercise discretion in their regulation. The court stated that the more difficult question was whether Mont. Code Ann. § 39-71-613 denied due process of law and equal protection of the law. The court held that the attorney had not set forth a factual or legal analysis to support his constitutional claims, nor had he shown any injury that had resulted from the legislation regulating attorney fees. The court held that § 39-71-613 and the department's application of the statute to a claimant's attorney fees was rationally related to the government's legitimate interest in protecting the claimant's net benefits in workers' compensation cases.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision that the department had the authority to regulate attorney fees and that the attorney had not shown any injury under his constitutional claims.

 

192 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Trout v. Bennett


No. 90-497


Supreme Court of Montana


252 Mont. 416; 830 P.2d 81; 1992 Mont. LEXIS 97; 49 Mont. St. Rep. 303


March 22, 1991, Submitted on Briefs  
April 9, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication May 8, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the District Court of Lewis and Clark County. First Judicial District. Honorable Jeffrey Sherlock, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff licensed non-resident life insurance agent appealed the judgments of the District Court for the First Judicial District, Lewis and Clark County (Montana), which fined the agent for violations of Mont. Code Ann. §§ 33-17-201(4), 33-18-203 and which granted summary judgment to defendant Commissioner of Insurance in the agent's § 1983 actions against the commissioner in her individual and official capacities.


OVERVIEW: Upon inquiries as to the agent's ability to advertise and conduct business in Montana, the commissioner suspended the agent's license and imposed fines for numerous statutory violations. In the agent's actions against the commissioner, the district court bifurcated the actions, found the agent guilty of advertising and soliciting business without an appointment, and granted the commissioner summary judgment on both § 1983 claims. On appeal, the court affirmed in part, reversed in part, and remanded. The court affirmed the two statutory violations and the denial of the agent's motion to amend. The court affirmed the summary judgment granted the commissioner in her official capacity because an official was not a person for § 1983 purposes. In holding that the commissioner was not absolutely immune from personal liability under § 1983 for all of her official actions, the court reversed the summary judgment granted on the agent's allegations of the sign removal, the business closing, and the notifications of his suspended license. The court remanded for further proceedings to determine if these actions were discretionary functions protected by quasi-judicial immunity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the agent's violations, the denial of the agent's motion to amend his complaint, and the grant of summary judgment to the commissioner in her official capacity in the agent's § 1983 action. The court reversed the summary judgment on the commissioner's right to claim quasi-judicial immunity for her actions in removing the agent's sign, closing his business, and announcing the suspension of the agent's license.

 

193 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Francetich v. State Compensation Mut. Ins. Fund


No. 91-093


Supreme Court of Montana


252 Mont. 215; 827 P.2d 1279; 1992 Mont. LEXIS 69; 49 Mont. St. Rep. 222


November 5, 1991, Submitted  
March 10, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication March 30, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Workers' Compensation Court. Honorable Timothy W. Reardon, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimant for workers' compensation benefits sought review of a decision of the Workers' Compensation Court (Montana), which held that appellee state fund could exercise subrogation rights pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 39-71-414(6)(a). The claimant contended that the statute was in direct violation of Mont. Const. art. II, § 16, in that it deprived him of the full legal redress guaranteed by the Constitution.


OVERVIEW: Following an injury at his work, a claimant for workers' compensation benefits received an award from the state fund. He also obtained a policy limits insurance settlement from the third-party tortfeasor. The state fund attempted to exercise subrogation rights under Mont. Code Ann. § 39-71-414(6)(a). The claimant contended that the statute was in direct violation of Mont. Const. art. II, § 16, which guaranteed the claimant full legal redress. The Workers' Compensation Court held that the state fund could exercise subrogation rights, and the claimant sought review. On review, the court confirmed that the statute restricted the claimant's constitutional right to obtain full legal redress against third-party tortfeasors. The court held that where a claimant was forced to settle for the limits of an insurance policy which, together with claimant's workers' compensation award, did not grant full legal redress under general tort law to the claimant, under workers' compensation laws the insurer was not entitled to subrogation rights under Mont. Code Ann § 39-71-414.


OUTCOME: The court held that the statute providing for subrogation was unconstitutional. The court reversed the judgment of the lower court allowing the state fund to exercise subrogation rights and remanded the matter.

 

194 of 930 DOCUMENTS


State v. Huebner


No. 90-472


Supreme Court of Montana


252 Mont. 184; 827 P.2d 1260; 1992 Mont. LEXIS 68; 49 Mont. St. Rep. 210


February 11, 1992, Submitted  
March 9, 1992, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication March 25, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from: District Court of Madison County. Fifth Judicial District. Honorable Frank M. Davis, Judge. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment upon a jury verdict by the District Court of Madison County, Fifth Judicial District (Montana) which convicted defendant of the misdemeanor charge of wasting game in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 87-3-102 (1989).


OVERVIEW: Subsequent to his conviction of wasting game in violation of § 87-3-102 after defendant shot a mountain goat and took only the head, horns, and cape of the animal, defendant sought review contending that the statute was unconstitutional do to it being vague or overbroad. Defendant contended that the statute was unconstitutionally vague because it did not clearly set forth the conduct, which was prohibited. The court found that the statute established the elements of how a person wasted game meat other than the grizzly, brown, or black bear or mountain lion. Accordingly, the court held that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague. The court also held that the statute was not unconstitutionally over broad as it specified that the act of removing specified body parts without utilizing the rest of the animal was illegal. The court also found that the district court did not err in failing to instruct the jury as to criminal intent. The court held that Mont. Code Ann. § 45-2-104 included two disjunctive requirements for an absolute liability offense and concluded that the second requirement had been met. Accordingly, the court affirmed the district court's conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the district court, which convicted defendant of wasting game.

 

195 of 930 DOCUMENTS


State v. Knight


No. 91-023


Supreme Court of Montana


251 Mont. 85; 822 P.2d 99; 1991 Mont. LEXIS 302; 48 Mont. St. Rep. 1060


October 24, 1991, Submitted on briefs  
December 3, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Released for Publication December 24, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the District Court of Yellowstone County. Thirteenth Judicial District. Hon. Robert W. Holmstrom, Judge presiding. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged an order of the District Court for the Thirteenth Judicial District, Yellowstone County (Montana), which convicted him on a jury verdict of deliberate homicide, in violation of Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-102, and attempted deliberate homicide. The trial court sentenced defendant to 100 years in prison on each count, plus 10 additional years on each count for the use of a dangerous weapon.


OVERVIEW: Defendant lent money to the victim of the homicide to complete a drug deal. When the victim failed to share the profits, defendant tracked him down and shot him. He also shot another person on the scene before fleeing. The sole issue was whether the jury was properly instructed on the lesser-included offense of mitigated deliberate homicide pursuant to Mont. Code Ann. § 45-5-103. The court held that mitigated deliberate homicide was a lesser-included offense of deliberate homicide only if defendant presented evidence that he acted under extreme mental or emotional stress for which there was a reasonable explanation or excuse. The court noted that defendant did not object to the trial court's instructions. With regard to defendant's claim of constitutional error, the court opined that the requirement that defendant prove by a preponderance of the evidence the presence of mitigating facts in support of the affirmative defense did not unconstitutionally shift the burden of proof to defendant. The court concluded that nothing prevented the jury's consideration of mitigating evidence and that § 45-5-103(2) did not unconstitutionally shift the burden of proof to defendant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

 

196 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Sheehy v. Dep't of Revenue


No. 90-450


Supreme Court of Montana


250 Mont. 437; 820 P.2d 1257; 1991 Mont. LEXIS 290; 14 Employee Benefits Cas. (BNA) 2604


August 15, 1991, Submitted  
November 14, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication December 19, 1991. Rehearing Denied November 22, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal From: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis and Clark, The Honorable Jeffrey M. Sherlock, Judge presiding.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff retired federal employees (employees) challenged a decision of the District Court of the First Judicial District, in and for the County of Lewis and Clark (Montana) that denied summary judgment to the employees and held that they were not entitled to refunds for state taxes they had paid on their retirement benefits in their action seeking a declaratory judgment that Mont. Code Ann. § 15-30-111(2)(c)(i) (1989) was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: A Montana statute exempted state employees from state and local taxation of retirement benefits, but § 15-30-111(2)(c)(i) made federal employees' benefits taxable. After a 1988 holding by the United States Supreme Court that a similar taxation scheme in another state was invalid, the parties stipulated that § 15-30-111(2)(c)(i) was invalid after 1988. The employees sought retroactive application of the holding and a refund for taxes paid between 1983 and 1988. The court affirmed the trial court decision refusing to apply the holding retroactively. The holding was not foreshadowed by the longstanding principle of intergovernmental tax immunity because it required three extensions of former law and directly contradicted prior law by replacing the equal protection standard with a new, "significant difference" standard to be applied in state tax discrimination cases. The court determined that refunds as a result of retroactive application of the holding would not promote the concept of intergovernmental tax immunity because the tax was not illegal when it was imposed. The court held that it would have been inequitable to impose the burden of paying refunds on Montana taxpayers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision denying summary judgment to the employees and holding that they were not entitled to refunds for state taxes they had paid on their retirement benefits.

 

197 of 930 DOCUMENTS


Johnson v. Capital Ford Garage


No. 91-291


Supreme Court of Montana


250 Mont. 430; 820 P.2d 1275; 1991 Mont. LEXIS 286; 48 Mont. St. Rep. 992


October 17, 1991, Submitted on Briefs  
November 14, 1991, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication November 29, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from: District Court of the First Judicial District, In and for the County of Lewis & Clark, The Honorable Dorothy McCarter, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant car repairer appealed a judgment of the First Judicial District, in and for the County of Lewis and Clark, Montana, which affirmed the Small Claims Division's monetary award to plaintiff owner for damage to her car that occurred while the car was in the repairer's control. The issues included whether the prohibition in Mont. Code Ann. § 25-35-803 of a trial de novo in the district court violated the repairer's due process guarantees.


OVERVIEW: A mechanic admitted that at least some of the damage occurred while the car was in his possession. On appeal, the court noted that, in an earlier case, it held that the right to counsel in civil cases was implicit in the due process guarantees of the Montana Constitution. Nevertheless, the court rejected the repairer's argument that the combined prohibitions of the appearance of attorneys and trial de novo in the small claims court, Mont. Code Ann. § 25-35-505(2), violated that right. The court pointed out that, under Mont. Code Ann. § 25-35-602, defendant in a small claims action had to be given notice of his right to remove the case to justice's court and failure to do so waived the rights to counsel and to a jury trial. The repairer did not deny receiving such a warning. The court held that it was no longer unconstitutional to prohibit de novo appeals. Further, under Mont. Code Ann. §§ 25-35-501, 25-35-702, the goal of the small claims court was to provide a speedy remedy, for which a small claims judge was given broad discretion in controlling the admissibility of testimony and exhibits. The court held the record contained sufficient evidence to support the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

 

198 of 930 DOCUMENTS


AP v. State


No. 91-475


Supreme Court of Montana


250 Mont. 299; 820 P.2d 421; 1991 Mont. LEXIS 275; 48 Mont. St. Rep. 958; 19 Media L. Rep. 1509



October 29, 1991, Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff news organizations filed a petition for writ of supervisory control, injunction pendente lite, and declaratory relief. The organizations alleged that Mont. Code Ann. § 46-11-701(6) was an unconstitutional deprivation of the public's right to know guaranteed by Mont. Const. art. II, § 9.The State conceded that the statute was unconstitutional. The State Bar's criminal defense section filed an amicus curiae brief opposing the petition.


OVERVIEW: Mont. Code Ann. § 46-11-701(6) required that an affidavit filed in support of a motion for leave to file a charge or warrant had to be sealed unless a judge determined that disclosure of the information in the affidavit was required to protect the health, safety, or welfare of the public. The organizations claimed that the statute irreparably harmed their ability to gather and disseminate information about pending criminal matters, and they sought a declaratory judgment that the statute was unconstitutional. The court granted the organizations' petition and held that the statute violated the right to know guaranteed by Mont. Const. art. II, § 9. There was nothing in the legislative record to indicate that the legislature determined that, or even considered whether, individual privacy requires that the affidavits described in the statute had to be sealed. Nor did the statute require such an evaluation by district judges in individual cases. The standard that affidavits had to be sealed was the antithesis of the standard required under Mont. Const. art. II, § 9. Thus, the court ordered that § 46-11-701(6) be stricken from the laws of Montana.


OUTCOME: The court granted the organizations' petition, held that § 46-11-701(6) violated the right to know guaranteed by Mont. Const. art. II, § 9, and ordered that § 46-11-701(6) be stricken from Montana's laws.

In re Application for Change of Appropriation Water Rights Nos. 101960-41S & 101967-41S


No. 91-009


Supreme Court of Montana


249 Mont. 425; 816 P.2d 1054; 1991 Mont. LEXIS 210; 48 Mont. St. Rep. 747


June 12, 1991, Submitted  
August 12, 1991, Decided


NOTICE:  [***1] Released for Publication August 29, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the District Court of Lewis and Clark County. First Judicial District. Honorable Thomas C. Honzel, Judge presiding. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant senior appropriators challenged the decision of the First Judicial District Court, Lewis and Clark County (Montana), which upheld the denial of their application for a change of place of use of water rights and a change of use from flood irrigation to sprinkler irrigation.


OVERVIEW: Respondent junior appropriators complained that the proposed expansion of irrigated acreage and change to sprinkler irrigation would result in greater depletion of the creek than had historically occurred, to the detriment of the junior appropriators. On review, the court affirmed. The court found that the applicant for a change of appropriation right had the burden as to the nonexistence of adverse impact. The court also found that, to allow the senior appropriators to present evidence of the junior appropriators' water rights could essentially lead to the readjudication of water rights claimed in the creek. The court determined that the district court had not erred in upholding the hearing examiner's decision to use the temporary preliminary decree as prima facie evidence of the junior appropriators' water rights rather than to allow the senior appropriators to present evidence of those rights. The court concluded that the senior appropriators had failed to meet their burden of demonstrating that granting their application would not adversely affect the rights of the junior appropriators.


OUTCOME: The denial of an application for a change of place and use of water rights was affirmed.

Ford v. Burlington N. R.R.


Nos. 90-557, 90-558


Supreme Court of Montana


250 Mont. 188; 819 P.2d 169; 1991 Mont. LEXIS 208; 48 Mont. St. Rep. 740


April 18, 1991, Submitted on Briefs  
August 12, 1991, Decided


NOTICE: Released for Publication August 30, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from: District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Yellowstone, The Honorable Russell K. Fillner, Judge presiding; The Honorable William J. Speare, Judge presiding.



DISPOSITION: We affirm the denial of defendant's motion for a change of venue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant railroad sought review of the decision of the District Court of the Thirteenth Judicial District, In and for the County of Yellowstone (Montana), which denied its motion to transfer venue in plaintiff employees' actions under the Federal Employers Liability Act, 45 U.S.C.S. §§ 51-60.


OVERVIEW: The employees were injured while working on the railroad's premises in Sheridan, Wyoming. Both employees alleged that the railroad failed to provide them with a safe place to work at that location. The employees filed a cause of action under the Federal Employers Liability Act in Yellowstone County, where they resided. The railroad filed a motion to change venue on the basis that another county was its principal place of business in Montana. The trial court denied the railroad's motion. The railroad appealed, challenging the constitutionality of Mont. Code Ann. § 25-2-118 (1989). The court held that the national policy of allowing railroad workers to sue their employer at any location where the employer does business presented a justification for disparate treatment. The court concluded that § 25-2-118 was nondiscriminatory in its operation and did not violate the Equal Protection Clause. The court affirmed the decision of the trial court that denied a change in venue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of the railroad's motion for a change of venue in the employees' actions under the Federal Employers Liability Act.
